The First Objection: Logical Flaw in Conditional Syllogism
Ghazali presents his first objection to the logical form of the proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical flaw in the conditional syllogism used in the proof of the sun's decay.

Core Idea
A conditional syllogism fails if the link between antecedent and consequent is unproven.

Ghazali begins his refutation by dissecting the logical structure of the philosophers' argument. He shows that the conditional syllogism they employ is not valid unless an additional condition is added. This lesson focuses on his first objection, which targets the very form of the proof.

Guided Path
1
Read Ghazali's description of the proof
Start by reading the first paragraph where Ghazali lays out the form of the philosophers' argument as a conditional syllogism.
Notice that the philosophers claim: if the sun decays, it must wilt; but wilting is impossible, so decay is impossible.
2
Identify the logical flaw
Ghazali argues that the consequent (wilting) does not necessarily follow from the antecedent (decay) without an additional condition. Find where he states this.
He says: 'This consequent does not follow from this antecedent except by adding a condition.'
3
Understand the required condition
Ghazali explains that the condition is either that the decay is specifically of a wilting type, or that all decay occurs via wilting. Read his explanation carefully.
He then refutes the assumption that wilting is the only form of decay.
4
Reflect on the broader implication
Consider how this critique frees thought from assuming that only one mode of decay exists. Ghazali mentions that things can decay suddenly even in their perfect state.
This sets up his later point about habit and reason.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول : أن شكل هذا الدليل أن يقال : إن كانت الشمس تفسد ، فلابد أن يلحقها ذبول لكن التالى محال فالمقدم محال . وهذا قياس يسمى عندهم الشرطي المتصل وهذه النتيجة غير لازمة ؛ لأن المقدم غير صحيح ما لم يضف إليه شرط آخر ، وهو قوله : إن كانت تفسد ، فلابد أن تذبل . فهذا التالي لا يلزم هذا المقدم ؛ إلا بزيادة شرط ، وهو أن يقول : إن كانت تفسد فساداً ذبولياً ، فلابد أن تذبل في طول المدة أو يبين أنه لا فساد إلا بطريق الذبول ، حتى يلزم التالى للمقدم . ولا نسلم أنه لا يفسد الشيء ، إلا بالذبول ؛ بل الذبول أحد وجوه الفساد . أن يفسد الشيء بغتة ، وهو على حال كماله . ولا يبعد

الثاني : أنه لم سلم له هذا ، وأنه لا فساد إلا بالذبول ، فمن أين عرف أنه لا يعتريها الذبول ؟ أما التفاته إلى الأرصاد فمحال ؛ لأنها لا تعرف مقاديرها إلا بالتقريب والشمس التي يقال : إنها كالأرض مائة وسبعين مرة ، أو ما يقرب منه ، لو نقص منها مقدار جبال مثلا ، لكان لا يتبين للحس فلعلها في الذبول ، وإلى الآن قد نقص مقدار جبال فأكثر، والحس لا يقدر على أن يدرك ذلك ؛ لأن تقديره في علم المناظر ، لا يعرف إلا بالتقريب. وهذا كما أن الياقوت والذهب مركبان من العناصر عندهم ، وهي قابلة للفساد تم لو وضعت ياقوتة مائة سنة ، لم يكن نقصانها محسوساً فلعل نسبة ما ينقص من الشمس في مدة تاريخ الأرصاد ، كنسبة ما ينقص من الياقوتة ، في مائة سنة وذلك لا يظهر للحس . فدل أن دليله في غاية الفساد

إنهما طعنتان يصوبهما رجل الميتافيزيقا ، رجل العقل الصرف، والنظر المجرد ، نحو رجل الفيزيقا ، رجل الأرصاد ، والأبحاث المادية . طعنتان قاتلتان : أما أولاهما : فتسمو بالفكر عن أن يضيق ويجمد ، ويلتزم حدود الإلف والعادة ، لا يبرحهما ، ولا يتخلص من أثرهما . نعم . إنه لا يستطيع عاقل أن يزعم أنه إذا كنا لم نصادف في حياتنا طريقاً لفساد الكائنات ، سوى الذبول ، فلابد أن يكون الطريق الوحيد إلى الفساد ، هو الذبول . من ذاك الذي يستطيع أن يزعم ذلك ؟ اللهم لا أحد ، إلا رجل الألف والعادة ، لا رجل العقل المفكر ، والذهن المتحرر
The first [objection]: that the form of this proof is to say: If the sun decays, then it must be overtaken by wilting. But the consequent is impossible, so the antecedent is impossible. This is a syllogism called by them the 'conditional conjunctive', and this conclusion is not necessary because the antecedent is not valid unless another condition is added, namely his statement: 'If it decays, then it must wilt.' So this consequent does not follow from this antecedent except by adding a condition, i.e., that he says: 'If it decays in a wilting manner, then it must wilt over a long period,' or to show that there is no decay except by way of wilting, so that the consequent follows from the antecedent. And we do not accept that a thing only decays by wilting; rather wilting is one mode of decay. That a thing decays suddenly while it is in a state of perfection is not unlikely.

The second [objection]: that even if this is granted to him, and that there is no decay except by wilting, how does he know that wilting does not affect it? As for his reliance on observations, it is impossible because they only know their magnitudes approximately. And the sun, which is said to be like the earth one hundred and seventy times or thereabouts, if the amount of a mountain, for example, were to decrease from it, it would not be perceptible to sense. So perhaps it is in a state of wilting, and up to now the amount of a mountain or more has decreased, and sense cannot perceive that because its estimation in the science of optics is only known approximately. This is like ruby and gold being composed of the elements according to them, and they are subject to decay. Then if a ruby were placed for a hundred years, its decrease would not be sensed. So perhaps the proportion of what decreases from the sun in the duration of the history of observations is like the proportion of what decreases from the ruby in a hundred years, and that does not appear to sense. So it shows that his proof is extremely corrupt.

They are two strikes that the metaphysician, the man of pure reason and abstract speculation, directs towards the physicist, the man of observations and material research. Two fatal strikes. As for the first of them: it elevates thought from narrowing and freezing, and adhering to the limits of familiarity and habit, not moving beyond them and not escaping their effect. Yes, no rational person can claim that if we have not encountered in our lives any way of decay of beings other than wilting, then the only way to decay must be wilting. Who can claim that? None except a man of familiarity and habit, not a man of rational thought and free mind.
Source pages p. 14

Commentary
This passage is a refutation by Ghazali of a philosophical proof about the corruption of the sun. He presents two objections and then characterizes them as fatal strikes. The first objection targets the logical structure of the proof. The opponent uses a conditional syllogism: if the sun decays, it must wilt; but wilting is not observed, so decay is impossible. Ghazali points out that the consequent (wilting) does not necessarily follow from the antecedent (decay) unless one assumes that all decay occurs via wilting. He shows that the syllogism requires an additional condition: either that the decay is specifically of a wilting type, or that there is no other form of decay. Since we do not accept that wilting is the only mode of decay, the argument fails. Ghazali notes that a thing can decay suddenly even in its perfect state. The second objection grants, for argument's sake, that decay only occurs by wilting, but asks how one can know that the sun has not undergone wilting. Observations are only approximate, and the sun is enormous—even a loss equal to a mountain would be imperceptible. He draws an analogy with a ruby: over a hundred years, its decrease is not sensed, so the sun's decrease over recorded history might also be undetectable. Hence the proof is corrupt. Finally, Ghazali comments that these two objections are strikes from the metaphysician (pure reason) against the physicist (empirical observation). They free thought from being bound by habit and custom—no rational person can claim that merely because we have only observed wilting as a form of decay, it must be the only form.

Key Terms
الشرطي المتصل
المقدم
التالي
الفساد
الذبول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that a conditional syllogism always yields a valid conclusion if the premises are true.
Correction: Ghazali shows that the conditional syllogism is valid only if the link between antecedent and consequent is necessary and proven. Without that, the inference is invalid.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what must be added to make the conditional syllogism valid?
A) An additional observation of the sun
B) A condition that the decay is specifically wilting or that all decay is via wilting
C) A mathematical calculation of the sun's size
D) A metaphysical principle that the sun is eternal

Key Takeaway
A conditional syllogism is only as strong as the link between its antecedent and consequent; an unproven link renders the proof invalid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You are confronted with arguments daily—what is one belief you hold that you have never examined for logical flaws? Will you examine it now, or continue to build on an unproven link?
?
When someone presents a compelling proof, do you tend to accept the conclusion without scrutinizing the premises? Recall a time you did this—what was the cost of your assumption?
?
Ghazali challenges you to see that a false conditional can lead to false certainty. Is there a 'conditional' in your life—an 'if this, then that'—that you have treated as absolute, but might need a closer look? What would it take to test it today?

The Second Objection: Empirical Ignorance of the Sun's Wilting
Ghazali presents his second objection based on the limitations of empirical observation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why observations of the sun cannot rule out gradual wilting due to the limitations of sense perception.

Core Idea
We cannot trust sense perception to detect minute changes in enormous objects over time.

Even if we grant the philosophers' assumption that decay only occurs by wilting, Ghazali argues that we cannot know whether the sun has been wilting gradually. Observations are approximate, and the sun's vast size means that even a large decrease would be imperceptible. This lesson focuses on his second objection, which targets the empirical basis of the proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the second objection
Start with the second paragraph where Ghazali concedes for argument's sake that decay is only by wilting, then questions how we know the sun hasn't wilted.
He immediately dismisses reliance on observations as impossible.
2
Understand the limitation of observation
Ghazali points out that observations only give approximate magnitudes. The sun is enormous; a loss equal to a mountain would be imperceptible. Find this part.
He uses the analogy of a ruby to illustrate that slow, gradual decrease is not sensed over a century.
3
Grasp the analogy with ruby and gold
Read the analogy: ruby and gold are composed of elements and subject to decay, but a decrease over a hundred years is not sensed. Similarly, the sun's decrease over recorded history may be undetectable.
This shows that the proof is 'extremely corrupt'.
4
Reflect on the consequence
Consider how this objection undermines any empirical claim that the sun has not decayed. It shows that the philosophers' reliance on observation is insufficient.
This prepares for the final characterization of the two strikes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول : أن شكل هذا الدليل أن يقال : إن كانت الشمس تفسد ، فلابد أن يلحقها ذبول لكن التالى محال فالمقدم محال . وهذا قياس يسمى عندهم الشرطي المتصل وهذه النتيجة غير لازمة ؛ لأن المقدم غير صحيح ما لم يضف إليه شرط آخر ، وهو قوله : إن كانت تفسد ، فلابد أن تذبل . فهذا التالي لا يلزم هذا المقدم ؛ إلا بزيادة شرط ، وهو أن يقول : إن كانت تفسد فساداً ذبولياً ، فلابد أن تذبل في طول المدة أو يبين أنه لا فساد إلا بطريق الذبول ، حتى يلزم التالى للمقدم . ولا نسلم أنه لا يفسد الشيء ، إلا بالذبول ؛ بل الذبول أحد وجوه الفساد . أن يفسد الشيء بغتة ، وهو على حال كماله . ولا يبعد

The first [objection]: that the form of this proof is to say: If the sun decays, then it must be overtaken by wilting. But the consequent is impossible, so the antecedent is impossible. This is a syllogism called by them the 'conditional conjunctive', and this conclusion is not necessary because the antecedent is not valid unless another condition is added, namely his statement: 'If it decays, then it must wilt.' So this consequent does not follow from this antecedent except by adding a condition, i.e., that he says: 'If it decays in a wilting manner, then it must wilt over a long period,' or to show that there is no decay except by way of wilting, so that the consequent follows from the antecedent. And we do not accept that a thing only decays by wilting; rather wilting is one mode of decay. That a thing decays suddenly while it is in a state of perfection is not unlikely.

The second [objection]: that even if this is granted to him, and that there is no decay except by wilting, how does he know that wilting does not affect it? As for his reliance on observations, it is impossible because they only know their magnitudes approximately. And the sun, which is said to be like the earth one hundred and seventy times or thereabouts, if the amount of a mountain, for example, were to decrease from it, it would not be perceptible to sense. So perhaps it is in a state of wilting, and up to now the amount of a mountain or more has decreased, and sense cannot perceive that because its estimation in the science of optics is only known approximately. This is like ruby and gold being composed of the elements according to them, and they are subject to decay. Then if a ruby were placed for a hundred years, its decrease would not be sensed. So perhaps the proportion of what decreases from the sun in the duration of the history of observations is like the proportion of what decreases from the ruby in a hundred years, and that does not appear to sense. So it shows that his proof is extremely corrupt.

They are two strikes that the metaphysician, the man of pure reason and abstract speculation, directs towards the physicist, the man of observations and material research. Two fatal strikes. As for the first of them: it elevates thought from narrowing and freezing, and adhering to the limits of familiarity and habit, not moving beyond them and not escaping their effect. Yes, no rational person can claim that if we have not encountered in our lives any way of decay of beings other than wilting, then the only way to decay must be wilting. Who can claim that? None except a man of familiarity and habit, not a man of rational thought and free mind.

Commentary
This passage is a refutation by Ghazali of a philosophical proof about the corruption of the sun. He presents two objections and then characterizes them as fatal strikes. The first objection targets the logical structure of the proof. The opponent uses a conditional syllogism: if the sun decays, it must wilt; but wilting is not observed, so decay is impossible. Ghazali points out that the consequent (wilting) does not necessarily follow from the antecedent (decay) unless one assumes that all decay occurs via wilting. He shows that the syllogism requires an additional condition: either that the decay is specifically of a wilting type, or that there is no other form of decay. Since we do not accept that wilting is the only mode of decay, the argument fails. Ghazali notes that a thing can decay suddenly even in its perfect state. The second objection grants, for argument's sake, that decay only occurs by wilting, but asks how one can know that the sun has not undergone wilting. Observations are only approximate, and the sun is enormous—even a loss equal to a mountain would be imperceptible. He draws an analogy with a ruby: over a hundred years, its decrease is not sensed, so the sun's decrease over recorded history might also be undetectable. Hence the proof is corrupt. Finally, Ghazali comments that these two objections are strikes from the metaphysician (pure reason) against the physicist (empirical observation). They free thought from being bound by habit and custom—no rational person can claim that merely because we have only observed wilting as a form of decay, it must be the only form.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if we don't observe a change, no change is occurring.
Correction: Ghazali shows that lack of observation does not prove absence of change, especially for large objects where change is gradual.

Checkpoint
What analogy does Ghazali use to illustrate that the sun might be wilting without our knowledge?
A) A burning candle
B) A ruby placed for a hundred years
C) A mountain eroding
D) A growing plant

Key Takeaway
Empirical arguments for the sun's permanence fail because our senses are too coarse to detect gradual decay in immense objects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you been absolutely certain of something based on what you observed, only to later discover you were wrong? How does this lesson challenge the confidence you place in your own senses?
?
Ghazali shows that even centuries of data may not detect gradual decay in the sun. In your spiritual life, what outward practices or beliefs seem stable to you but might be slowly eroding without your notice? What will you do to check and renew them?
?
The philosophers' proof seemed powerful but collapsed because it ignored the limits of human perception. What 'proofs' in your own worldview—whether about yourself, others, or God—might be resting on assumptions that you have not examined? Are you willing to question them honestly?

The Two Fatal Strikes: Metaphysics vs Physics
Ghazali characterizes his two objections as fatal strikes from pure reason against empirical habit.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will appreciate Ghazali's characterization of his two objections as fatal strikes from pure reason against empirical habit.

Core Idea
True reasoning must break free from the limits of habit and custom.

Ghazali concludes his refutation by stepping back and framing his two objections as strikes from the metaphysician (the man of pure reason) against the physicist (the man of observations). He highlights that the first objection, in particular, frees thought from being confined by familiarity and habit. This lesson explores these concluding remarks and their broader philosophical significance.

Guided Path
1
Read the concluding paragraph
Start with the final paragraph where Ghazali describes the two objections as strikes from the metaphysician to the physicist.
Note the contrast between 'pure reason' and 'material research'.
2
Understand the characterization of the first strike
Ghazali says the first strike 'elevates thought from narrowing and freezing, adhering to the limits of familiarity and habit'. Read this part and reflect on what he means.
He argues that no rational person can claim that because we have only seen wilting, it is the only form of decay.
3
Identify the target of the criticism
He attributes the opposing view to a 'man of familiarity and habit', not a man of rational thought. Consider who this represents.
This is a critique of empiricists who generalize from limited experience.
4
Reflect on the broader lesson
Think about how this passage warns against restricting one's reasoning to observed patterns alone. True intellectual reasoning must allow for possibilities beyond habit.
This is a key teaching in Ghazali's approach to refuting the philosophers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول : أن شكل هذا الدليل أن يقال : إن كانت الشمس تفسد ، فلابد أن يلحقها ذبول لكن التالى محال فالمقدم محال . وهذا قياس يسمى عندهم الشرطي المتصل وهذه النتيجة غير لازمة ؛ لأن المقدم غير صحيح ما لم يضف إليه شرط آخر ، وهو قوله : إن كانت تفسد ، فلابد أن تذبل . فهذا التالي لا يلزم هذا المقدم ؛ إلا بزيادة شرط ، وهو أن يقول : إن كانت تفسد فساداً ذبولياً ، فلابد أن تذبل في طول المدة أو يبين أنه لا فساد إلا بطريق الذبول ، حتى يلزم التالى للمقدم . ولا نسلم أنه لا يفسد الشيء ، إلا بالذبول ؛ بل الذبول أحد وجوه الفساد . أن يفسد الشيء بغتة ، وهو على حال كماله . ولا يبعد

The first [objection]: that the form of this proof is to say: If the sun decays, then it must be overtaken by wilting. But the consequent is impossible, so the antecedent is impossible. This is a syllogism called by them the 'conditional conjunctive', and this conclusion is not necessary because the antecedent is not valid unless another condition is added, namely his statement: 'If it decays, then it must wilt.' So this consequent does not follow from this antecedent except by adding a condition, i.e., that he says: 'If it decays in a wilting manner, then it must wilt over a long period,' or to show that there is no decay except by way of wilting, so that the consequent follows from the antecedent. And we do not accept that a thing only decays by wilting; rather wilting is one mode of decay. That a thing decays suddenly while it is in a state of perfection is not unlikely.

The second [objection]: that even if this is granted to him, and that there is no decay except by wilting, how does he know that wilting does not affect it? As for his reliance on observations, it is impossible because they only know their magnitudes approximately. And the sun, which is said to be like the earth one hundred and seventy times or thereabouts, if the amount of a mountain, for example, were to decrease from it, it would not be perceptible to sense. So perhaps it is in a state of wilting, and up to now the amount of a mountain or more has decreased, and sense cannot perceive that because its estimation in the science of optics is only known approximately. This is like ruby and gold being composed of the elements according to them, and they are subject to decay. Then if a ruby were placed for a hundred years, its decrease would not be sensed. So perhaps the proportion of what decreases from the sun in the duration of the history of observations is like the proportion of what decreases from the ruby in a hundred years, and that does not appear to sense. So it shows that his proof is extremely corrupt.

They are two strikes that the metaphysician, the man of pure reason and abstract speculation, directs towards the physicist, the man of observations and material research. Two fatal strikes. As for the first of them: it elevates thought from narrowing and freezing, and adhering to the limits of familiarity and habit, not moving beyond them and not escaping their effect. Yes, no rational person can claim that if we have not encountered in our lives any way of decay of beings other than wilting, then the only way to decay must be wilting. Who can claim that? None except a man of familiarity and habit, not a man of rational thought and free mind.
Source pages p. 14

Commentary
This passage is a refutation by Ghazali of a philosophical proof about the corruption of the sun. He presents two objections and then characterizes them as fatal strikes. The first objection targets the logical structure of the proof. The opponent uses a conditional syllogism: if the sun decays, it must wilt; but wilting is not observed, so decay is impossible. Ghazali points out that the consequent (wilting) does not necessarily follow from the antecedent (decay) unless one assumes that all decay occurs via wilting. He shows that the syllogism requires an additional condition: either that the decay is specifically of a wilting type, or that there is no other form of decay. Since we do not accept that wilting is the only mode of decay, the argument fails. Ghazali notes that a thing can decay suddenly even in its perfect state. The second objection grants, for argument's sake, that decay only occurs by wilting, but asks how one can know that the sun has not undergone wilting. Observations are only approximate, and the sun is enormous—even a loss equal to a mountain would be imperceptible. He draws an analogy with a ruby: over a hundred years, its decrease is not sensed, so the sun's decrease over recorded history might also be undetectable. Hence the proof is corrupt. Finally, Ghazali comments that these two objections are strikes from the metaphysician (pure reason) against the physicist (empirical observation). They free thought from being bound by habit and custom—no rational person can claim that merely because we have only observed wilting as a form of decay, it must be the only form.

Key Terms
الميتافيزيقا
الفيزيقا
الإلف
العادة
العقل المفكر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that Ghazali is simply attacking empirical science as a whole.
Correction: He is critiquing a specific flawed inference from limited observation, not science per se. He calls for reasoning that is not bound by habit.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, who would make the claim that because we have only seen wilting, it must be the only form of decay?
A) A rational thinker
B) A man of familiarity and habit
C) A metaphysician

Key Takeaway
Rational thought must not be confined by habit and custom; to assume that only observed patterns are possible is a mark of intellectual limitation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Where in your life is your faith limited by experience rather than by revealed truth? Commit to one area where you will consciously affirm Allah's absolute power over what you have not witnessed.
?
Think of a belief you hold not because of evidence or revelation, but because it is what everyone around you believes and it is comfortable. Are you willing to examine it with pure reason and let it be shattered if it is false? What is one such belief you will honestly question today?
?
When you read the Qur'an or listen to a reminder, do you sometimes react with 'but that doesn't happen in my experience' rather than 'my Lord can do all things'? In what specific situation has your habit of expectation deafened you to the message of Allah's limitless power? Write down that situation and a prayer asking for freedom from the prison of habit.

Ghazali's Dialectical Counter on Causal Necessity
Ghazali counters an objection on causal necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali uses the opponent's own principle of causal necessity to expose inconsistency in their doctrine of divine knowledge.

Core Idea
Ghazali turns the causal necessity argument against its proponents.

In this passage, Ghazali responds to an objection that causal necessity is rationally undeniable. Rather than denying the principle, he shows that the opponents themselves violate it when they claim that a single divine essence knows all universals without multiplicity. This exposes a deeper inconsistency in their philosophical theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and Ghazali's response
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its translation. Identify the objector's claim and Ghazali's initial rebuttal.
Notice that Ghazali does not reject the principle of causal necessity outright.
2
Analyze the parallel argument
Observe how Ghazali draws a parallel between the objector's premise and their own doctrine of divine knowledge. He asks why they do not apply the same standard to their own beliefs.
The key phrase is 'What distinction is there between you and your opponents?'
3
Reflect on the rhetorical strategy
Consider how this dialectical move undermines the opponent's position without accepting or denying the original claim. This is a hallmark of Ghazali's method.
Ghazali also mentions that some philosophers, aware of the difficulty, claimed God knows only Himself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل نحن بضرورة العقل نعلم أنه لا يتصور موجيب بتمام شروطه من غير موجب ، ومجوز ذلك مكابر لضرورة العقل
قلنا : وما الفصل بينكم وبين خصومكم ، إذا قالوا لكم : إنا بالضرورة نعلم إحالة قول من يقول : إن ذاتاً واحدة عالمة بجميع الكليات من غير أن يوجب ذلك كثرة ، ومن غير أن يكون العلم زيادة على الذات ، ومن غير أن يتعدد العلم مع تعدد المعلوم ، وهذا مذهبكم فى حق الله ، وهو بالنسبة إلينا وإلى علومنا في غاية الإحالة ، ولكن تقولون ؛ لا يقاس العلم القديم بالحادث وطائفة منكم استشعروا إحالة هذا فقالوا : إن الله لا يعلم إلا نفسه ، فهو العاقل ، وهو العقل ، وهو المعقول ؛ والكل واحد
If it is said: 'We know by necessity of reason that a cause (mūjib) with all its conditions cannot be conceived without its effect, and whoever allows that is denying the necessity of reason,' we say: 'What distinction is there between you and your opponents when they say to you: "We know by necessity the impossibility of the statement that a single essence knows all universals without that causing multiplicity, without knowledge being an addition to the essence, and without knowledge being multiple with the multiplicity of the known. This is your doctrine concerning God, and it is, relative to us and our knowledge, utterly impossible. But you say: 'Eternal knowledge is not to be measured by temporal knowledge.' And a group among you, realizing the impossibility of this, said: 'God knows only Himself, so He is the intellect (al-ʿāqil), the intellect (al-ʿaql), and the intelligible (al-maʿqūl); all are one.'"'
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exchange. The objector claims that a cause with all its conditions necessarily produces its effect, and denying that is against reason. Ghazali turns this against them: why do they not apply the same standard to their own doctrine that God, a single essence, knows all universals without multiplicity, which seems equally impossible? He notes that they dismiss this by saying eternal knowledge is not like temporal, and some among them even went as far as claiming God knows only Himself, identifying Himself as intellect, intelligent, and intelligible. This shows the inconsistency of their position.

مكابر
ذات واحدة
العاقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) A cause with all its conditions necessarily produces its effect.
B) God cannot know universals.
C) Knowledge is multiple.
D) The world is eternal.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali demonstrates that the principle of causal necessity, if applied consistently, undermines the philosophers' own doctrine of divine knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When was the last time you applied a standard to others that you did not apply to yourself? How does this passage expose the danger of such inconsistency? What one relationship today will you examine for this double standard?
?
The philosophers reduced God's knowledge to self-knowledge to avoid a contradiction. In your own life, when have you settled for a smaller, safer idea of God rather than grapple with the mystery of His attributes? What might you need to let go of to truly worship Him as He is?
?
You claim to follow reason, but does your reasoning always lead you to truth, or can it be used to justify error? This passage shows reason can be turned against itself. What belief have you held so confidently that you have not examined its foundation before the Qur'an and Sunnah? Will you test it today?

Refuting God's Knowledge Only of Himself
Ghazali refutes the claim that God knows only Himself by showing its absurd consequence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali uses a reductio ad absurdum to refute the claim that God knows only Himself.

Core Idea
If God knew only Himself, He would not know His own creation—an absurd consequence.

Ghazali confronts a philosophical claim about divine knowledge. The philosophers argued that God knows only Himself, but Ghazali shows this leads to a contradiction. In this passage, he presents a reductio ad absurdum: if God had no knowledge of His creation, the very notion of a Maker who does not know His handiwork becomes impossible. Prepare to see how a single logical step refutes a whole doctrine.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that it is a single conditional statement beginning with 'So if someone were to say...'
2
Identify the opponent's claim
The opponent claims that the identity of intellect, intelligent, and intelligible is impossible. But Ghazali uses this to his advantage.
This is a reference to the philosophers' doctrine of divine intellection.
3
Trace the reductio
Follow the logic: if the Eternal knows only Himself, then He does not know His handiwork. But that is absurd because a Maker must know what He makes.
The absurdity is that a Maker ignorant of His creation is impossible by necessity.
4
Recognize the conclusion
The absurdity shows the original claim (God knows only Himself) is false. This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.
Ghazali adds an exclamation to emphasize the gravity of the error.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلو قال قائل : اتحاد العاقل ، والعقل ، والمعقول ، معلوم الاستحالة بالضرورة ؛ إذ تقدير صانع للعالم لا يعلم صنعته ، محال بالضرورة ، والقديم إذا لم يعلم إلا نفسه – تعالى عن قولكم ، وعن قول جميع الزائغين علوا كبيراً – لم يكن يعلم صنعته ألبتة
So if someone were to say: 'The identity of the intellect, the intelligent, and the intelligible is known by necessity to be impossible, for to suppose a Maker of the world who does not know His handiwork is impossible by necessity, and if the Eternal knows only Himself – exalted is He above your statement and above the statement of all deviators, a great exaltation – then He would never know His handiwork at all.'
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage is a reductio ad absurdum. Ghazali supposes an opponent who says the identity of the intellect, intelligent, and intelligible is known to be impossible. But if God knew only Himself, He would not know His own creation, which is absurd. Thus the original claim that God knows only Himself is refuted.

Key Terms

العاقل
العقل
المعقول
صانع
قديم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Ghazali is arguing about the ontological identity of God with creation
Correction: This passage refutes the claim that God knows only Himself; it is not about pantheistic identity.

Checkpoint
What does Ghazali argue would follow if God knew only Himself?
A) He would be all-knowing.
B) He would not know His handiwork.
C) He would be identical with His creation.
D) He would know only essences.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali refutes the claim that God knows only Himself by showing it would entail God not knowing His own creation, which is absurd.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever acted as if God were unaware of your private thoughts or actions? In what specific situation did you behave as though God's knowledge did not extend to your life, and how would that moment change if you truly believed He knows everything about His creation?
?
The philosophers claimed God knows only Himself—essentially limiting His knowledge. Do you find yourself limiting God's knowledge in your own life, perhaps by thinking He doesn't care about the details of your struggles? What would it mean for you to fully accept that God knows every aspect of His handiwork, including your deepest needs?
?
If you truly internalized that God is the Maker of all things and therefore knows them intimately, what one habit or sin would you be most compelled to change starting today? What is holding you back from that change?

Infinite Revolutions and the Impossibility of Eternity
Ghazali presents two arguments: the fractions problem and the even/odd dilemma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali uses the impossibility of infinite numbers having fractions and even/odd properties to argue that the world cannot be eternal.

Core Idea
An eternal world would require infinite past events, leading to absurdities in basic arithmetic.

In this passage, Ghazali presents a powerful logical argument against the philosophers' claim that the world is eternal. He focuses on the celestial revolutions—if the world had no beginning, there would be an actual infinite number of past rotations. But such an infinite would have to include fractions (like half or sixth) and also be either even or odd, both of which lead to contradictions. This lesson explores that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the original Arabic
Read the Arabic passage slowly, noting the key terms for fractions and even/odd.
The text contains a probable typo: 'زجل' should be 'زحل' (Saturn).
2
Understand the fractions argument
Notice how Ghazali uses the different orbital periods to show that if both are infinite, one infinite is a fraction of another.
This challenges the notion that infinite sets can be compared.
3
Analyze the even/odd dilemma
Ghazali forces the opponent to choose whether the infinite number is even or odd, both leading to contradiction.
The logic relies on adding one to an infinite set.
4
Connect to the eternity debate
Reflect on how this argument serves as a reductio ad absurdum against the eternity of the world.
If the world were eternal, such absurdities follow, so it must have a beginning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بل لا نتجاوز الزامات هذه المسألة فنقول : بم تنكرون على خصومكم إذا قالوا : قدم العالم محال ؛ لأنه يؤدى إلى إثبات دورات للفلك لا نهاية لأعدادها ، ولا حصر لآحادها ، أن لها سدساً ، وربعاً ، ونصفاً ؛ فإن فلك الشمس مع يدور في سنة ، وفلك زحل فى ثلاثين سنة ؛ فتكون أدوار زحل ثلث عشر أدوار الشمس . وأدوار المشترى نصف سدس أدوار الشمس ؛ فإنه يدور في اثنتي عشرة سنة . كما أنه لا نهاية لأعداد دورات زجل ، لا نهاية لأعداد دورات الشمس مع أنها ثلث عشرها ، بل لا نهاية لأدوار فلك الكواكب الذي يدور في ستة وثلاثين ألف سنة مرة واحدة ، كما لا نهاية للحركة المشرقية التي للشمس في اليوم والليلة مرة .
فإن قال قائل : هذا مما يعلم استحالته ضرورة . فبماذا تنفصلون عن قوله ؟ بل لو قال قائل : أعداد هذه الدورات شفع أو وتر ؟ أو شفع ووتر جميعاً ؟ أو لا شفع ولا وتر ؟ فإن قلتم : شفع ووتر جميعاً ، أو لاشفع ولا وتر ، فيعلم بطلانه
ضرورة ، وإن قلتم : شفع، فالشفع يصير وتراً بواحد ، فكيف أعوز ما لانهاية له واحد ؟ و إن قلتم : وتر ، فالوتر يصير بواحد شفعاً ، فكيف أعوزه ذلك الواحد الذي يصير به شفعاً ؛ فيلزمكم القول بأنه ليس بشفع ولا وتر
But let us not go beyond the necessary implications of this point and say: Why do you deny your opponents when they say: 'The eternity of the world is impossible because it leads to establishing revolutions of the sphere with no end to their numbers and no limit to their individual units, such that they have a sixth, a fourth, and a half; for the sphere of the sun makes a revolution in a year, and the sphere of Saturn in thirty years; so the revolutions of Saturn are three-tenths (thulth ʿushr) of the revolutions of the sun. And the revolutions of Jupiter are half a sixth of the revolutions of the sun, for it makes a revolution in twelve years. Similarly, there is no end to the numbers of the revolutions of Saturn [Zuhal?], no end to the numbers of the revolutions of the sun even though they are three-tenths of them. Rather, there is no end to the revolutions of the sphere of the fixed stars which makes one revolution in thirty-six thousand years, just as there is no end to the eastward motion of the sun which occurs once in a day and a night.
Then if someone says: 'That is known by necessity to be impossible,' how would you refute his statement? Or if someone says: 'Are the numbers of these revolutions even or odd? Or both even and odd? Or neither even nor odd?' If you say: 'Both even and odd' or 'neither even nor odd,' its falsity is known by necessity. And if you say: 'Even,' then the even becomes odd by adding one, so how could that which has no end lack a single one? And if you say: 'Odd,' then the odd becomes even by adding one, so how could it lack that one by which it becomes even? Thus you must say that it is neither even nor odd.'
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage is a proof against the eternity of the world. Ghazali argues that if the world were eternal, there would be an infinite number of past celestial revolutions. But infinite numbers having fractions (like half, quarter) is impossible, and the question of whether the number is even or odd leads to contradiction. Therefore, the world must have a beginning.

Correction: The argument concerns actual infinite historical events, not a potential or mathematical infinity.

Checkpoint

A) They are too many to count.
B) They would have fractions like a sixth, fourth, and half.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali demonstrates that the concept of an actual infinite set of past events leads to arithmetic absurdities, thereby refuting the eternity of the world.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Do you live as if this world is eternal, investing your time and energy only in fleeting things, while neglecting your eternal destination? Starting today, what is one concrete change you can make to prioritize what lasts forever?
?
Ghazali used sharp logical reasoning to defend the truth against false beliefs. When was the last time you examined your own assumptions about faith with such rigor? Are you willing to question your own comfortable beliefs and follow the evidence, even if it costs you? What belief are you most afraid to examine?
?
The fact that the world had a beginning means you are not an accident of chance but a purposeful creation. Does that truth really affect how you live each day? If you truly believed that every moment is a gift from the Creator who brought the world into existence, what would you change about your daily routine starting right now?

The First Challenge: Logic Fails in Divine Sciences
Ghazali's first challenge: philosophers fail to meet their own logical conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's first challenge: the philosophers' own logical criteria for demonstration cannot be satisfied in their divine sciences.

Core Idea
Philosophers' logic fails where it matters most.

In his famous Tahafut, al-Ghazali launches a series of challenges against the philosophers. The first challenge targets the very core of their methodology: if logic is supposed to guarantee certainty, why can't it be applied consistently to the divine sciences? This lesson unpacks that challenge.

Guided Path
1
Read the first quote
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then the translation. Notice how Ghazali uses the philosophers' own logical terms against them.
Pay attention to the terms: مادة القياس, صورة القياس, and the references to Porphyry's Isagoge and Aristotle's Categories.
2
Identify the logical criteria
Ghazali lists three conditions the philosophers set for valid demonstrations: material conditions, formal conditions, and rules from basic logic texts. Underline them.
These are conditions the philosophers themselves accept but fail to meet in metaphysics.
3
Reflect on the failure
Ask: Why is it significant that the philosophers cannot fulfill these conditions in divine sciences? What does this imply about the status of their metaphysical proofs?
This is Ghazali's opening move to expose inconsistency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن أمثلة ذلك تحديه للفلاسفة أن يخضعوا البحوث الإلهية لمعاييرهم المنطقية ، بقوله : ونناظرهم في هذا الكتاب بلغتهم - أعنى بعباراتهم في المنطق – ونوضح أن ما شرطوه في صحة مادة القياس في قسم البرهان من المنطق ، وما شرطوه في صورته في كتاب القياس ، وما وضعوه من الأوضاع في « إيساغوجي » و « قاطيغورس » - التى هى من أجزاء المنطق ومقدماته – لم يتمكنوا من الوفاء بشيء منه فى علومهم الإلهية ) وبقوله : --- فهذا ما أردنا أن نذكر تناقضهم فيه ، من جملة علومهم الإلهية والطبيعية ؛ وأما الرياضيات فلا معنى لإنكارها ولا للمخالفة فيها ، وأما المنطقيات فهي نظر فى آلة الفكر فى المعقولات ، ولا يتفق فيها خلاف به مبالاة ] ثم بقوله : · أين من يدعى أن براهين الإلهيات قاطعة كبراهين الهندسيات ؟)
And among examples of that is his challenge to the philosophers to subject divine investigations to their logical criteria, by his saying: 'And we debate them in this book in their language — I mean with their expressions in logic — and we clarify that what they stipulated for the validity of the material of syllogism in the section on demonstration from logic, and what they stipulated for its form in the book on syllogism, and the rules they laid down in 'Isagoge' and 'Categories' — which are parts of logic and its preliminaries — they were unable to fulfill any of it in their divine sciences.' And by his saying: 'This is what we intended to mention of their contradiction, from among their divine and natural sciences. As for mathematics, there is no meaning to denying it or opposing it. And as for logical matters, they are a reflection on the tool of thought in intelligibles, and no significant disagreement occurs in them.' Then by his saying: 'Where is the one who claims that the proofs of the divine sciences are decisive like the proofs of geometry?'
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage provides three direct quotes from al-Ghazali's Tahafut al-Falasifa, demonstrating his challenge to the philosophers. In the first quote, he states that the philosophers' own logical criteria for demonstration cannot be met in their divine sciences, despite their emphasis on logic. In the second, he distinguishes between mathematics (which is certain) and logical matters (which are merely tools and not subject to significant disagreement). In the third, he rhetorically asks where are those who claim that proofs in metaphysics are as certain as geometric proofs, implying they cannot be. These quotes illustrate Ghazali's argument that the philosophers' reliance on logic fails in the realm of theology.

Key Terms

قياس
مادة
صورة
إيساغوجي
قاطيغورس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ghazali is rejecting logic altogether.
Correction: Ghazali uses logic himself; he is criticizing the philosophers for not being consistent in applying their own logical standards to their divine theories.

Checkpoint

A) Mathematics
B) Natural sciences
C) Divine sciences
D) Both natural and divine sciences

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's first challenge exposes the philosophers' inability to meet their own logical standards in divine sciences, undermining their claim to certainty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What methods do you rely on to understand God and the unseen? Are you like the philosophers, assuming one tool works for every area of knowledge? Where might you be overconfident in your own reasoning? Take a moment to admit one area where your logic has failed to grasp a truth of faith.
?
Ghazali used the philosophers' own language to expose their failure. When you debate or discuss matters of religion, do you try to use the other person's framework to help them see the truth? Or do you insist on your own terms? Who in your life needs you to speak 'in their language' to reach them?
?
Imagine you have built an elaborate framework for understanding Islam — through study, logic, or scholarship. What if that framework cannot handle the deepest questions about God? Would you be humble enough to admit its limits, or would you twist the questions to fit your system? What step can you take today to guard against intellectual arrogance?

The Second Challenge: Certainty in Mathematics vs. Logical Tools
Ghazali distinguishes mathematics (certain) from logical tools.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's distinction between mathematics (certain) and logic (a mere tool) and why this matters for the philosophers' project.

Core Idea
Mathematics is certain; logic is just a tool—don't confuse them.

In his second quoted challenge, Ghazali makes a crucial distinction. He separates mathematics, which he admits is certain and cannot be denied, from logical matters, which he describes as merely a tool for thought. This distinction allows him to accept some aspects of philosophy while rejecting its metaphysical applications.

Guided Path
1
Read the second quote
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the separation between divine/natural sciences, mathematics, and logic.
Ghazali explicitly excludes mathematics from his critique.
2
Analyze the three categories
Identify the three domains: divine/natural sciences (where contradiction occurs), mathematics (certain, no objection), and logic (tool, no significant disagreement).
This helps Ghazali narrow his attack to metaphysics.
3
Reflect on the strategy
Why does Ghazali exclude mathematics and logic? How does this strengthen his position against the philosophers?
By accepting mathematics, he avoids a wholesale rejection of reason.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن أمثلة ذلك تحديه للفلاسفة أن يخضعوا البحوث الإلهية لمعاييرهم المنطقية ، بقوله : ونناظرهم في هذا الكتاب بلغتهم - أعنى بعباراتهم في المنطق – ونوضح أن ما شرطوه في صحة مادة القياس في قسم البرهان من المنطق ، وما شرطوه في صورته في كتاب القياس ، وما وضعوه من الأوضاع في « إيساغوجي » و « قاطيغورس » - التى هى من أجزاء المنطق ومقدماته – لم يتمكنوا من الوفاء بشيء منه فى علومهم الإلهية ) وبقوله : --- فهذا ما أردنا أن نذكر تناقضهم فيه ، من جملة علومهم الإلهية والطبيعية ؛ وأما الرياضيات فلا معنى لإنكارها ولا للمخالفة فيها ، وأما المنطقيات فهي نظر فى آلة الفكر فى المعقولات ، ولا يتفق فيها خلاف به مبالاة ] ثم بقوله : · أين من يدعى أن براهين الإلهيات قاطعة كبراهين الهندسيات ؟)
And among examples of that is his challenge to the philosophers to subject divine investigations to their logical criteria, by his saying: 'And we debate them in this book in their language — I mean with their expressions in logic — and we clarify that what they stipulated for the validity of the material of syllogism in the section on demonstration from logic, and what they stipulated for its form in the book on syllogism, and the rules they laid down in 'Isagoge' and 'Categories' — which are parts of logic and its preliminaries — they were unable to fulfill any of it in their divine sciences.' And by his saying: 'This is what we intended to mention of their contradiction, from among their divine and natural sciences. As for mathematics, there is no meaning to denying it or opposing it. And as for logical matters, they are a reflection on the tool of thought in intelligibles, and no significant disagreement occurs in them.' Then by his saying: 'Where is the one who claims that the proofs of the divine sciences are decisive like the proofs of geometry?'
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage provides three direct quotes from al-Ghazali's Tahafut al-Falasifa, demonstrating his challenge to the philosophers. In the first quote, he states that the philosophers' own logical criteria for demonstration cannot be met in their divine sciences, despite their emphasis on logic. In the second, he distinguishes between mathematics (which is certain) and logical matters (which are merely tools and not subject to significant disagreement). In the third, he rhetorically asks where are those who claim that proofs in metaphysics are as certain as geometric proofs, implying they cannot be. These quotes illustrate Ghazali's argument that the philosophers' reliance on logic fails in the realm of theology.

The Third Challenge: Are Divine Proofs as Certain as Geometry?
Ghazali questions certainty of divine proofs vs geometry.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's rhetorical challenge questioning whether proofs in divine sciences can ever achieve the certainty of geometric demonstrations.

Core Idea
If divine proofs were certain, they'd be like geometry—but they're not.

Ghazali's third and final quoted challenge is a sharp rhetorical question. He asks: where is the person who claims that proofs in divine sciences (metaphysics) are decisive and certain like proofs in geometry? The implied answer is that no one can legitimately make that claim, because metaphysical proofs lack the same level of certainty.

Guided Path
1
Read the third quote
Read the Arabic and translation. This is a single, powerful rhetorical question.
Notice the comparison between divine proofs (براهين الإلهيات) and geometric proofs (براهين الهندسيات).
2
Understand the comparison
Geometry is the paradigm of certain, demonstrative knowledge in the Aristotelian tradition. Ghazali uses it as a benchmark.
The philosophers claim their metaphysics is demonstrative; Ghazali challenges that.
3
Reflect on the implication
If no one can show that divine proofs are as certain as geometric ones, then the philosophers' claim to certainty in metaphysics is baseless.
This completes Ghazali's trilogy of challenges.

Truth as Correspondence with Reality: A Classical and Idealism Link
The editor compares Berkeley's idealism with the classical definition of truth from kalam and balaghah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the classical definition of truth as correspondence with reality, where reality is understood as God's knowledge, and how this parallels Berkeley's idealism.

Core Idea
Truth is alignment with God's knowledge, not just facts.

This passage is an editorial reflection that bridges Western philosophy and classical Islamic thought. The author reads about Berkeley's idealism and is reminded of a well-known definition of truth from kalam and balaghah. By examining this connection, we uncover a profound common ground: both traditions locate the ultimate reality of things in divine knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the editor connects two seemingly different intellectual traditions.
Pay attention to the definition of truth and the explanation of reality.
2
Identify the two definitions discussed
The passage presents the classical definition of truth (مطابقة الخبر للواقع) and Berkeley's theory of meaning (Idealism). Note how the editor relates them.
The key link is that both understand reality as being in God's knowledge.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this connection might be significant for kalam. How does grounding truth in God's knowledge affect our understanding of objectivity?
This lesson challenges a purely empirical view of truth.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لقد قرأت في الإنجليزية مثلا نظرية المعنى (٢) - ال Idealism وقرأت أن مؤسسها هو باركلي Berkeley الفيلسوف الإنجليزي الذائع الصيت وخلاصة رأيه في هذه النظرية أن الوجود الأصيل للأشياء هو كونها في علم الله ، فالأشياء ليست إلا أفكاراً في علم الله . ولقد حضرني عند قراءة هذه النظرية تعريف الأقدمين للصدق بأنه مطابقة الخبر للواقع ، وتفسيرهم الواقع بأنه علم الله وهذا يعنى أن أصحاب هذا التعريف - وهو تعريف مشهور يذكره علماء الكلام في كتبهم ، ويذكره علماء البلاغة في بحوثهم - يرون أن الوجود الأصيل للأشياء هو كونها في علم الله ، كما يرى ذلك باركلى . وربما عدت لهذه المسألة وأشباهها فيما بعد إن شاء الله . (٢) هكذا أحب أن أسميها ويسمها آخرون النظرية المثالية ، أو النظرية الصورية .
I have read in English, for example, the theory of meaning (2) - Idealism - and I read that its founder is Berkeley, the famous English philosopher, and the summary of his opinion in this theory is that the real existence of things is their being in God's knowledge; things are but ideas in God's knowledge. And when reading this theory, there came to my mind the definition of the ancients for truth as correspondence of the report with reality, and their explanation of reality as God's knowledge. This means that the proponents of this definition—which is a famous definition mentioned by scholars of kalam in their books and by scholars of rhetoric in their discussions—hold that the real existence of things is their being in God's knowledge, just as Berkeley holds it. And perhaps I will return to this issue and its likes later, God willing. (2) Thus I like to name it, and others name it the idealistic theory or the formal theory.
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage is a comparative observation. The editor mentions reading about Berkeley's idealism (theory of meaning) which holds that the real existence of things is their being in God's knowledge. He then recalls the classical definition of truth as correspondence with reality, where reality is explained as God's knowledge. He notes that this definition is well-known among kalam and rhetoric scholars. He points out that this definition implies that the real existence of things is in God's knowledge, exactly as Berkeley holds. He promises to return to this topic later. The footnote adds that he prefers to call it 'theory of meaning' while others call it 'idealistic theory' or 'formal theory'.

The First Type of Disagreement: Purely Verbal
Ghazali introduces the second preface and defines the first type of verbal disagreement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali considers some disputes with the philosophers to be merely verbal and thus not worthy of refutation.

Core Idea
Some disagreements are only about words, not about meaning.

Ghazali opens his second introduction by classifying three types of disagreement with the philosophers. The first type is purely verbal: both parties agree on the underlying reality but use different terms. He gives the example of calling God a 'jawhar' (substance) — a term that, once defined properly, causes no real conflict. This lesson examines that example and Ghazali's approach.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that Ghazali is making a classification. He says there are three types but only describes the first here.
The text ends mid-sentence; we focus on the first type.
2
Understand the verbal dispute example
Focus on the example of the term 'jawhar'. The philosophers define it as 'self-subsisting', while their opponents define it as 'corporeal body'. Once both agree that God is self-subsisting, the disagreement becomes merely verbal.
The key is that the definition differs, not the underlying concept.
3
Reflect on Ghazali's strategy
Ghazali says he will not bother refuting this verbal dispute because it has no real consequence. Ask yourself: Why is it important to identify purely verbal disagreements early on?
This distinguishes substantive theological disputes from terminological ones.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مقدمة ثانية ليعلم أن الخلاف بينهم وبين غيرهم من الفرق ثلاثة أقسام : قسم : يرجع النزاع فيه إلى لفظ مجرد ، كتسميتهم صانع العالم - تعالى عن قولهم - جوهراً ، مع تفسيرهم الجوهر بأنه الموجود لا في موضوع أى القائم بنفسه الذى لا يحتاج إلى مقوم يقومه ، ولم يريدوا بالجوهر المتحيز ، على ما أراده خصومهم ولسنا نخوض فى إبطال هذا ، لأن معنى القيام بالنفس إذا صار متفقاً
A second introduction: let it be known that the disagreement between them and others of the sects is of three types. One type: the dispute in it reduces to a mere word, such as their naming the Maker of the world—exalted is He above their saying—a 'jawhar' (substance), while their interpretation of jawhar is 'that which exists not in a subject' i.e., that which is self-subsisting and does not need a constituent to constitute it. And they did not mean by jawhar the corporeal body, as their opponents intended. And we do not delve into refuting this, because if the meaning of self-subsistence becomes agreed upon...
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage begins the second introduction of the Tahafut. Ghazali identifies three types of disagreement between the philosophers and their opponents. Here he describes the first type: it is a purely verbal dispute (lafẓ mujarrad). The example is the philosophers' use of the term 'jawhar' (substance) for God. They define jawhar as 'existing not in a subject', meaning self-subsisting and not needing a constituent. Their opponents understood jawhar as a corporeal body (mutaḥayyiz). Since the dispute is only about terminology—once both agree that God is self-subsisting—there is no real disagreement. Therefore, Ghazali says he will not waste effort refuting this verbal difference.

Key Terms

قائم بنفسه
مقوم
متحيز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that Ghazali is conceding that God can be called 'jawhar' in the sense of substance.
Correction: Ghazali is not endorsing the term; he is pointing out that the disagreement is merely verbal. Once both sides agree on the meaning (self-subsistence), the label becomes irrelevant.

Checkpoint
Why does Ghazali consider the first type of disagreement with the philosophers to be purely verbal?
A) Because the philosophers and their opponents agree on the meaning but use different words.
B) Because the dispute over God being called 'jawhar' hinges on the definition of the term, and if the meaning of self-subsistence is agreed, the verbal difference becomes irrelevant.
C) Because the philosophers refuse to discuss the matter.
D) Because the opponents do not understand the philosophers' language.

Key Takeaway
Disputes that are merely verbal can be resolved by clarifying definitions, and Ghazali sees no need to argue about them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
When you hear a term used for God that feels unfamiliar or wrong, do you immediately assume the speaker holds a deviant belief? Could it be a verbal difference? How does this lesson challenge you to be more charitable in your judgments?
?
Ghazali refuses to waste effort on purely verbal disputes. Where in your own life are you stubbornly defending mere words instead of seeking common ground? What one verbal argument can you commit to dropping today for the sake of unity?

Objection and Answer on Even and Odd of Infinite Numbers
Ghazali presents and refutes the objection that infinite totals cannot be even or odd.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali refutes the philosophers' claim that infinite totals cannot be even or odd, using logical necessity and their own principles.

Core Idea
The infinite also must be either even or odd—necessity strikes both sides.

This passage is a classic dialectical exchange between Ghazali and the philosophers on the nature of infinite numbers. The philosophers argue that only finite things can be even or odd. Ghazali counters with a devastating argument: a whole composed of units must be either even or odd, even if the units are infinite. He then turns their own doctrine of immortal souls against them to expose inconsistency.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and initial response
Read the objection from the philosophers and Ghazali's first answer. Notice how he uses a reductio ad absurdum.
The key is the claim that a whole must be even or odd.
2
Understand the counter-objection
The philosophers object that 'whole' refers to present existents, not past or future. Consider the implications.
3
Follow Ghazali's deeper argument
Ghazali argues that number is intrinsically even or odd regardless of the existence status of the counted things. This is a key point.
He uses the example of horses.
4
See the turn using souls
Ghazali turns the tables: according to the philosophers' own principles, there is an actual infinite of souls, which are neither even nor odd. This shows their objection is inconsistent.
This references Ibn Sina and Aristotle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : إنما توصف بالشفع والوتر المتناهى ، وما لا نهاية له فلا . قلنا : فجملة مركبة من آحاد ، لها سدس وعشر كما سبق ، ثم لا توصف بشفع ولا وتر ، يعلم بطلانه ضرورة من غير نظر ، فبماذا تنفصلون عن هذا ؟ ! فإن قيل : محل الغلط في قولكم : أنه جملة مركبة من جملة مركبة من آحاد ، فإن هذه الدورات معدومة ، أما الماضى فقد انقرض ، وأما المستقبل فلم يوجد ، والجملة إشارة إلى موجودات حاضرة ولا موجود ههنا . قلنا : العدد ينقسم إلى الشفع والوتر ، ويستحيل أن يخرج عنه ، سواء كان المعدود موجوداً باقياً ، أو فانياً ، فإذا فرضنا عدداً الأفراس ، لزمنا أن نعتقد أنه لا يخلو من كونه شفعاً أو وتراً ، سواء قدرناها موجودة أو معدومة ، فإن انعدمت بعد الوجود لم تتغير هذه القضية ، على أنا نقول لهم : لا يستحيل على أصلكم موجودات حاضرة ، هي آحاد متغايرة بالوصف ، ولا نهاية لها ، وهى نفوس الآدميين المفارقة للأبدان بالموت ، فهى موجودات لا توصف بالشفع والوتر ، فبم تنكرون على من يقول : بطلان هذا يعرف ضرورة ، كما ادعيم بطلان تعلق الإرادة القديمة بالأحداث ضرورة ، وهذا الرأى في النفوس ، هو الذي اختاره ابن سينا ، ولعله مذهب أرسطاطاليس .
If it is said: Only the finite is described as even and odd, not the infinite. We say: But a whole composed of units has a sixth and a tenth as earlier, yet it is not described as even or odd—its falsehood is known necessarily without reflection. So how do you separate yourselves from this? If it is said: The error in your statement is that it is a whole composed of another whole composed of units, for these revolutions are non-existent: the past has perished, the future not yet come, and 'whole' refers to present existents, but nothing exists here. We say: Number is divided into even and odd, and it is impossible for it to fall outside them, whether the counted things exist remaining or have perished. If we suppose a number of horses, we must believe that it is not free from being even or odd, whether we suppose them existent or non-existent. If they become non-existent after existence, this proposition does not change. Moreover, we say to them: According to your own principle, there are present existents, which are diverse individual units without end—namely, human souls separating from bodies at death—yet they are existents not described as even or odd. So why do you deny someone who says that the falsehood of this is known necessarily, just as you claim the falsehood of the eternal will's relation to temporal events as necessary? And this view of the souls is the one chosen by Ibn Sina, and perhaps it is the doctrine of Aristotle.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer exchange. Ghazali first states the philosophers' objection: only finite things are even or odd; the infinite is neither. He responds by noting that a whole composed of units (like a number) has properties like a sixth and tenth, yet is not even or odd—which is absurd, so their claim is false by necessity. The philosophers counter that the 'whole' they speak of refers only to present existents, not past or future non-existents. Ghazali then answers more thoroughly: number is intrinsically divided into even and odd regardless of whether the counted things exist or have perished. He then turns the tables: according to the philosophers' own principles, there are actually existing infinite beings—human souls after death—which are not even or odd. So if they claim necessary knowledge that the eternal will cannot relate to temporal events, they must also accept that this is absurd. He notes that Ibn Sina held this view of the souls, and it may be Aristotle's doctrine as well.

Key Terms
شفع
وتر
متناهي
غير متناه
عدد
نفوس
ضرورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That infinite numbers cannot be even or odd applies to all infinities equally.
Correction: Ghazali shows that the property of being even or odd applies to numbers themselves, not to the physical existence of counted items, and that actual infinities like souls must also be either even or odd.

Checkpoint
How does Ghazali turn the philosophers' objection about even and odd back on them?
A) He argues that all numbers are finite by definition.
B) He points to the souls of humans as an actual infinite that is neither even nor odd.
C) He denies that the past and future exist.
D) He claims that the philosophers' view is based on a mistaken definition of number.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali refutes the objection by showing that number must be even or odd necessarily, and uses the philosophers' own belief in infinite souls to expose inconsistency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the infinite number of your sins against a finite lifespan, do you treat them as manageable or overwhelming? How does Ghazali's insistence that number is inherently even or odd challenge your view of the magnitude of your faults?
?
Ghazali uses the philosophers' own belief in immortal souls to force them into contradiction. Are there beliefs you hold that, if followed consistently, would force you to accept something you otherwise deny? What would it cost you to examine that inconsistency today?
?
You have just read that number cannot escape being even or odd, even for what has perished. If your past deeds form a number that is either even or odd, does that number terrify you? What are you doing now to change that number before it becomes eternally fixed?

The Problem of the Fixed Poles in a Homogeneous Heaven
Ghazali presents the initial objection against the philosophers' view on fixed poles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali argues that the philosophers cannot explain the fixity of poles given their assumption of celestial homogeneity.

Core Idea
If all parts of heaven are equal, no reason can be given for any particular point to be a fixed pole.

This lesson presents the first part of Ghazali's objection: if the heavens are truly homogeneous and simple, then all points are equivalent. How then can two specific points be singled out as fixed poles? This is a key challenge to the coherence of Aristotelian cosmology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and English translation carefully to understand the structure of the objection.
Notice how Ghazali sets up the premise of homogeneity.
2
Identify the key question
Ghazali asks: why are the north and south poles the fixed points? What distinguishes them from any other point on the sphere?
This is the central point.
3
Reflect on the implications
If the philosophers cannot give a reason, then their claim of homogeneity is undermined or they must appeal to arbitrary choice.
This leads to a dilemma.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فنقول : نحن وإن كنا نقدر على معارضتكم بمثله في الأحوال إذ قال قائلون : خلقه في الوقت الذى كان الأصلح الخلق فيه ، لكنا لا نقتصر على هذه المقابلة ، بل نفرض على أصلكم تخصيصاً في موضعين لا يمكن أن يقدر فيهما اختلاف ؛ أحدهما اختلاف جهة الحركة ، والآخر تعيين موضع القطب في الحركة على المنطقة (۱) ويتصور أما القطب فبيانه أن السماء كرة متحركة على قطبين كأنهما ثابتان ، وكرة السماء متشابهة الأجزاء ، فإنها بسيطة ، لا سيما الفلك الأعلى الذي هو التاسع فإنه غير مركب (1) أصلا ، وهو متحرك على قطبين شمالي وجنوبي ، فنقول : نقطتين متقابلتين من النقط التي لا نهاية (۲) لها عندهم إلا أن يكونا ، هم ، هما القطبين ، فلم تعينت نقطتا الشمال والجنوب للقطبية والثبات ؟ ! ولم لم يكن خط المنطقة مارا بالنقتطين حتى يعود القطب إلى نقتطين متقابلتين على المنطقة ؟ ! فإن كان فى مقدار كبر السماء وشكلها حكمة ، فما الذي ميز محل القطب عن غيره ، حتى تعين لكونه قطباً، دون سائر الأجزاء والنقط ، وجميع أجزاء الكرة متساوية ؟ ! وهذا لا مخرج وجميع النقط متماثلة ، فإن قيل : لعل الموضع الذى عليه نقطة القطب يفارق غيره بخاصية تناسب كونه محلاً للقطب حتى يثبت ، فكأنه لا يفارق مكانه وحيزه ووضعه أو ما يفرض إطلاقه عليه من الأسامى ؛ وسائر مواضع الفلك يتبدل بالدور وضعها من الأفلاك ، والقطب ثابت الوضع ، فلعل ذلك الموضع كان أولى الأرض ومن بأن يكون ثابت الوضع من غيره . قلنا : ففى هذا تصريح بتفاوت أجزاء الكرة الأولى في الطبيعة ، وأنها ليست متشابهة الأجزاء ؛ وهو على خلاف أصلكم ، إذ أصل ما استدللتم به على لزوم كون السماء كرى الشكل أنها بسيط الطبيعة متشابهة لا تفاوت فيها ، وأبسط الأشكال الكرة ، فإن التربيع ، والتسديس ، وغيرهما ، يقتضى خروج زوايا وتفاوتها ، وذلك لا يكون إلا بأمر زائد على الطبع البسيط ، ولكنه وإن خالف مذهبكم فليس يندفع (۱) الإلزام به ، فإن السؤال في تلك الخاصية قائم إذ سائر الأجزاء هل كان قابلا تلك الخاصية أم لا ؟ ! فإن قالوا : نعم ، فلم اختصت الخاصية من بين المتشابهات ببعضها ؟ ! و إن قالوا : لم يكن ذلك إلا في ذلك الموضع ، وسائر الأجزاء لا تقبلها فنقول : سائر الأجزاء من حيث إنها جسم قابل للصور ، متشابهة بالضرورة ! وتلك الخاصية لا يستحقها ذلك الموضع لمجرد كونه جسما ، ولا بمجرد كونه سماء ، فإن هذا المعنى يشاركه فيه سائر أجزاء السماء ، فلا بد أن يكون تخصيصه به ، بتحكم ، أو بصفة من شأنها تخصيص الشيء عن مثله ، وإلا فكما يستقيم لهم قولهم : إن الأحوال في قبول وقوع العالم فيها متساوية ! يستقيم لخصومهم قولهم : إن أجزاء السماء في قبول المعنى الذى لأجله صار ثبوت الوضع أولى به في تبدل الوضع ، متساوية ؛ وهذا لا مخرج منه
We say: Although we are able to oppose you with the like of it concerning the states, when some say: He created it at the time that was most appropriate for creation, yet we do not restrict ourselves to this opposition, but we impose upon your principle a specification in two places where difference cannot be conceived: one is the difference of the direction of motion, and the other is the determination of the position of the pole in motion along the ecliptic. As for the pole, its explanation is that the heaven is a sphere moving on two poles as if they are fixed, and the sphere of the heaven is homogeneous in parts, for it is simple, especially the highest orb which is the ninth, for it is not composite at all, and it moves on two poles, northern and southern. So we say: Two opposite points from among the infinite points? according to them, except that they are the poles. Why were the points of north and south determined for polarity and fixity? And why was the line of the ecliptic not passing through those two points so that the pole would return to two opposite points on the ecliptic? If there is wisdom in the magnitude and shape of the heaven, what distinguished the place of the pole from others so that it was determined to be a pole, to the exclusion of the rest of the parts and points, while all parts of the sphere are equal? There is no way out of this. And all points are similar. If it is said: Perhaps the place on which the point of the pole lies differs from others by a property suitable for being a place for the pole so that it is fixed, as if it does not leave its place, its space, its position, or whatever names are applied to it; while other positions of the sphere change by rotation their position relative to the spheres, and the pole is fixed in position, so perhaps that place was more deserving [؟] than others to be fixed in position. We say: In this there is an explicit acknowledgment of the differentiation of the parts of the first sphere in nature, and that they are not homogeneous in parts; and this is contrary to your principle, for the basis of your proof that the heaven is spherical in shape is that it is simple in nature, homogeneous, having no differentiation, and the simplest of shapes is the sphere, for quadrature, hexagon, and others require protruding angles and their differentiation, and that does not occur except by something additional to the simple nature. But even if it contradicts your school, the required point is not thereby repelled, for the question concerning that property remains: Did the other parts have the capacity for that property or not? If they say: Yes, then why was the property singled out from among the similar things to some of them? And if they say: That property only belongs to that place, and the other parts do not accept it. We say: The other parts, insofar as they are bodies receptive to forms, are necessarily similar! And that property is not deserved by that place merely because it is a body, nor merely because it is heaven, for that meaning is shared by the rest of the parts of the heaven. So it is necessary that its being singled out for it be either by arbitrary choice, or by a quality whose property is to single out a thing from its like. Otherwise, just as their statement is valid: 'The states concerning the possibility of the occurrence of the world in them are equal', so the statement of their opponents is valid: 'The parts of the heaven are equal in receiving the meaning on account of which fixity of position is more appropriate in the change of position'. And there is no way out of this.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage presents a sustained objection-and-answer argument against the philosophers' claim that the heavens are homogeneous and simple. Ghazali begins by stating that he will impose two specifications on their principle, the first concerning the determination of the pole. He describes the heaven as a sphere moving on two fixed poles, with all parts homogeneous. The objection: if all points on the sphere are equivalent, why are two specific points (north and south) singled out to be the fixed poles? Why didn't the ecliptic line pass through them? If there is a wisdom in the size and shape, what distinguishes the pole's location? Since all parts are equal, no reason can be given. Ghazali then considers a possible response: the pole point might have a special property that makes it suitable for fixity. He counters that this admission implies that the parts of the sphere are not truly homogeneous—contradicting the philosophers' own principle that the sphere is simple and uniformly shaped. He then presses further: if other parts could have that property, why were these chosen? If they cannot, then there must be a differentiating factor, which again breaks homogeneity. The only way out is either arbitrary choice (which the philosophers reject) or a distinguishing quality, which leads back to the same problem. The argument concludes that the philosophers' position leads to an inescapable dilemma.

B) The heaven is composed of four elements.
C) The heaven is homogeneous and all parts are equal.
D) The poles are mentioned in scripture.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's objection shows that the assumption of celestial homogeneity cannot account for the existence of fixed poles without arbitrary specification.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Can you point to one area of your life where you assume uniformity without questioning the specific details that make it unique? How might that assumption be hiding your dependence on God's specific will?
?
When have you taken a blessing for granted as 'just the way things are' instead of seeing it as a deliberate act of divine determination? What would it look like to consciously thank God for one such specification today?
?
If the most basic structure of the universe—the poles of the heavens—cannot exist without God's direct choice, what does that imply about your own choices and their ultimate dependence on Him? Will you live today with that awareness?
