Refuting the Philosophers' Response on Polar Specification
Ghazali refutes the philosophers' response and highlights the contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali refutes the philosophers' attempted justification for fixed poles, exposing a contradiction in their principle of homogeneity.

Core Idea
The philosophers' defense that the pole point has a special property contradicts their own claim that heaven is homogeneous.

In this lesson, Ghazali considers a possible response from the philosophers: perhaps the pole location has a unique property that makes it suitable for fixity. He then shows that this admission undermines the entire principle of homogeneity and leads to an inescapable dilemma.

Guided Path
1
Read the response and counter
Read the passage containing the philosophers' hypothetical response and Ghazali's refutation.
Note how Ghazali turns the response against them.
2
Analyze the dilemma
Ghazali forces a choice: either other parts could have the property (then why not them?), or they cannot (then homogeneity is false).
This is a classic reductio.
3
Connect to the broader argument
This refutation shows that the philosophers cannot maintain both homogeneity and the existence of fixed poles.
The argument is airtight if the premises are accepted.

Commentary
This passage presents a sustained objection-and-answer argument against the philosophers' claim that the heavens are homogeneous and simple. Ghazali begins by stating that he will impose two specifications on their principle, the first concerning the determination of the pole. He describes the heaven as a sphere moving on two fixed poles, with all parts homogeneous. The objection: if all points on the sphere are equivalent, why are two specific points (north and south) singled out to be the fixed poles? Why didn't the ecliptic line pass through them? If there is a wisdom in the size and shape, what distinguishes the pole's location? Since all parts are equal, no reason can be given. Ghazali then considers a possible response: the pole point might have a special property that makes it suitable for fixity. He counters that this admission implies that the parts of the sphere are not truly homogeneous—contradicting the philosophers' own principle that the sphere is simple and uniformly shaped. He then presses further: if other parts could have that property, why were these chosen? If they cannot, then there must be a differentiating factor, which again breaks homogeneity. The only way out is either arbitrary choice (which the philosophers reject) or a distinguishing quality, which leads back to the same problem. The argument concludes that the philosophers' position leads to an inescapable dilemma.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The philosophers believe that the pole's location can be explained by a special property without contradicting homogeneity.
Correction: Ghazali shows that any special property implies differentiation, which contradicts the premise of homogeneity.

Checkpoint
What does Ghazali argue is the necessary implication of the philosophers' response that the pole location has a special property?
A) The heaven must be spherical.
B) The parts of the heaven are differentiated.
C) The poles are not real.
D) The motion is eternal.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers cannot explain the fixity of poles without either contradicting homogeneity or appealing to arbitrary choice, which they reject.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter an explanation that seems to solve a problem but contradicts your core beliefs, do you honestly examine the contradiction or do you settle for the comfortable answer? Give an example from your own life where you did one or the other.
?
The philosophers preferred a logical principle (homogeneity) over empirical observation (fixed poles). Are there principles you hold so tightly that you ignore evidence that contradicts them? What would it take for you to reconsider?
?
Ghazali forces a dilemma: either arbitrary choice or heterogeneity. In your relationship with Allah, do you accept that His will is sometimes arbitrary from our perspective, or do you seek a rational explanation for everything? How does this affect your trust in His decisions?

The Philosophers' Hierarchy of Existents
Philosophers classify existents into a three-level hierarchy to address the composition objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how philosophers classify existents (accidents, bodies, souls, intellects) to resolve the composition objection against their cosmology.

Core Idea
Composition is explained through a hierarchy of existents, not through a single direct emanation.

This passage from Al-Ghazali's Tahafut presents the philosophers' response to the charge of composition. They argue that once you understand their full classification of existents, the problem disappears. The key is that existents form a graded hierarchy from accidents to abstract intellects, and composition arises from the interaction of these distinct levels.

Guided Path
1
Read and identify the passage structure
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice it begins with a hypothetical objection ('If it is said...'), then presents a multi-level classification, and ends with the three self-subsistent principles.
The passage is a continuous argument; try to see how each division builds on the previous.
2
Trace the classification divisions
Follow the divisions: existents in subjects vs. not; not-in-subjects into subjects-for-others vs. self-subsistent; self-subsistent into souls and intellects. Note that accidents and bodies are secondary; the core is the three self-subsistent principles.
The classification tree is the backbone of the philosophers' answer.
3
Understand the hierarchy of self-subsistent principles
Focus on the three: bodies (lowest), souls (intermediate, affecting bodies), and abstract intellects (noblest, affecting only souls). This hierarchy shows that composition is not a flaw but a structured ordering.
Souls are affected by intellects and affect bodies, forming a chain of causation.
4
Connect to the composition objection
Reflect on how this classification answers the objection: Composition is not a problem because it arises from the interaction of distinct levels, not from a single undifferentiated emanated being.
Al-Ghazali is presenting the philosophers' own argument, not endorsing it.
5
Review key terms and quiz yourself
Go over the key terms (mahall, a'rad, jawaahir, nufus, uqool mujarrada) and test your understanding with the checkpoint.
These terms are essential for understanding Islamic philosophical cosmology.

فإن قيل : إذا عرف مذهبنا ، اندفع الإشكال ، فإن الموجودات تنقسم : إلى ما هى فى محال ، كالأعراض والصور ؛ وإلى ما ليست في محال ؛ وهذه تنقسم : إلى ما هى محال لغيرها كالأجسام ، وإلى ما ليست بمحال كالموجودات التي هي جواهر قائمة بأنفسها، وهي تنقسم إلى ما يؤثر في الأجسام ونسميها نفوساً ، وإلى ما لا يؤثر فى الأجسام ، بل في النفوس ، ونسميها عقولا مجردة . أما الموجودات التي تحل فى المحال كالأعراض ، فهى حادثة ، ولها علل حادثة ، وتنتهى إلى مبدأ هو حادث من وجه دائم من وجه ، وهو الحركة الدورية وليس الكلام فيها . وإنما الكلام فى الأصول القائمة بأنفسها لا في محال، وهى ثلاثة : أجسام ، وهى أخسها . وعقول مجردة ، وهي التي لا تتعلق بالأجسام لا بالعلاقة الفعلية ، ولا بالانطباع فيها ، وهى أشرفها . ونفوس ، وهى أوسطها ، فإنها تتعلق بالأجسام نوعاً من التعلق ، وهو التأثير والفعل فيها ، فهي متوسطة في الشرف ، فإنها تتأثر عن العقول ، وتؤثر في الأجسام .
If it is said: When our doctrine is known, the problem is removed. For existents are divided: into those that are in subjects (mahall), like accidents and forms; and into those that are not in subjects. And these are divided: into those that are subjects for others, like bodies; and into those that are not subjects, like existents that are substances subsisting by themselves. And these are divided into those that affect bodies – we call them souls – and into those that do not affect bodies, but rather affect souls – we call them abstract intellects. As for existents that inhere in subjects, like accidents, they are originated (haditha), and they have originated causes, and they end with a principle that is originated in one aspect and perpetual in another, which is circular motion – and this is not our concern. Our concern is only with the principles subsisting by themselves, not in subjects. These are three: bodies, which are the lowest; abstract intellects, which do not relate to bodies either by active relation or by immanence in them – these are the noblest; and souls, which are intermediate, for they relate to bodies in a kind of relation, namely affecting and acting in them. Thus they are intermediate in nobility, for they are affected by intellects and affect bodies.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' classification of existents in response to the composition objection. It begins by stating that once their doctrine is understood, the problem disappears. They divide existents into those that inhere in subjects (accidents and forms) and those that do not. The latter are further divided into subjects for others (bodies) and non-subjects (self-subsistent substances). These self-subsistent substances are then divided into those that affect bodies (souls) and those that affect only souls (abstract intellects). Accidents are originated and trace back to circular motion, which is not the focus. The core discussion is about the three self-subsistent principles: bodies (lowest), abstract intellects (noblest, unaffected by bodies), and souls (intermediate, affected by intellects and affecting bodies). This hierarchy shows that composition arises from the interaction of these distinct levels, not from a single direct emanation.

Key Terms
محال (subject/inherent)
أعراض (accidents)
جواهر (substances)
نفوس (souls)
عقول مجردة (abstract intellects)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Philosophers deny any composition in the universe.
Correction: They affirm composition but locate it in the interaction of distinct levels of existents (bodies, souls, intellects), each with different relations to causation.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers, which existents are the noblest?
A) Bodies
B) Souls
C) Abstract intellects
D) Accidents

Key Takeaway
The philosophers classify existents into a hierarchy (bodies, souls, intellects) to show that composition arises from the interaction of distinct levels, not from a single emanation.

What is one concrete action you will take in the next hour to reverse that?
?
The text says souls 'affect bodies' and are 'affected by intellects'. Think of a recent struggle you had with a physical habit (like anger, laziness, or overindulgence). Did your soul actually act on your body, or did your body overpower your soul? What truth does this reveal about the current state of your soul's nobility?

If abstract intellects are the noblest because they have no attachment to bodies, what does that imply about your own attachments? Name one material thing, person, or status you are holding onto that you know is weighing your soul down. How will you begin to loosen that attachment today?

Second Objection: Intellect's Knowledge and Identity
Ghazali poses a dilemma about the first intellect's self-knowledge and its principle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's second objection against the philosophers' claim of simplicity: whether the intellect's knowledge of itself and its principle is identical to its essence, and the implications for multiplicity.

Core Idea
If the intellect knows its essence, no multiplicity arises—contradicting the philosophers' own argument.

Ghazali now challenges the philosophers' notion of the first intellect's simplicity. He asks a critical question: Is the intellect's knowledge of its principle (its cause) the same as its essence, or something added? The answer forces a dilemma: either there is multiplicity in the first intellect or the philosophers' own reasoning leads to the same simplicity they attribute only to the First Principle. This passage sets up the argument that the intellect's self-knowledge, if identical to its essence, eliminates any difference between cause and effect within its nature.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation to grasp the logical dilemma Ghazali presents.
Notice the two conditional statements beginning with 'فإن كان عينه' and 'و إن كان غيره'.
2
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
Identify the two possibilities: identity of intellection with essence vs. difference. Note the consequence of each: no multiplicity vs. multiplicity in the first intellect.
The philosophers might try to avoid the second horn by claiming that self-intellection includes intellection of others.
3
Follow Ghazali's rebuttal
See how Ghazali turns the philosophers' own principle against them: if the first intellect's self-intellection is identical to its essence, then it must intellect itself as an effect, and the whole returns to itself—no multiplicity.
The argument relies on the principle that 'the intellect corresponds to the intelligible'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاعتراض الثانى : هو أن نقول عقله مبدؤه ، عين وجوده ، وعين عقله نفسه ، أم غيره ؟ فإن كان عينه فلا كثرة فى ذاته ، إلا في العبارة عن ذاته ، و إن كان غيره فهذه الكثرة موجودة في الأول ، فإنه يعقل ذاته ، ويعقل غيره ، فإن زعموا أن عقله ذاته عين ذاته ، ولا يعقل ذاته ، ما لم يعقل أنه مبدأ لغيره فإن العقل يطابق المعقول، فيكون راجعاً إلى ذاته ؛ فنقول : والمعلول، عقله ذاته عين ذاته ، فإنه عقل بجوهره ، فيعقل نفسه ، والعقل والعاقل والمعقول منه أيضاً واحد ؛ ثم إذا كان عقله ذاته عين ذاته ، فليعقل ذاته معلولا لعلة ، فإنه كذلك ، والعقل يطابق المعقول ، فيرجع الكل إلى ذاته ، فلا كثرة إذن ، وإن
The second objection: We say: Is its intellection of its principle identical to its existence and identical to its intellection of itself, or other than it? If it is identical, then there is no multiplicity in its essence, except in the expression about its essence. But if it is other, then this multiplicity exists in the First, for it intellects itself and intellects other. If they claim that its intellection of itself is identical to its essence, and it does not intellect itself unless it intellects that it is a principle for others—for the intellect corresponds to the intelligible, so it returns to itself—we say: And the effect, its intellection of itself is identical to its essence, for it intellects by its substance, so it intellects itself, and the intellect, the intelligent, and the intelligible from it are also one. Then if its intellection of itself is identical to its essence, let it intellect itself as an effect of a cause, for it is so, and the intellect corresponds to the intelligible, so the whole returns to itself; thus there is no multiplicity.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage presents Ghazali's second objection. He asks whether the first intellect's intellection of its principle and of itself are identical to its existence or distinct. If identical, there is no multiplicity. If distinct, then the same multiplicity would exist in the First Principle, which intellects itself and others—contradicting their claim that the First is absolutely simple. The philosophers might object that the First's intellection of itself is identical to its essence, and its intellection of others is included in this because intellect corresponds to intelligible. Ghazali applies the same reasoning to the first intellect: its intellection of itself is identical to its essence, so it should intellect itself as an effect of a cause (which it is), and this whole returns to itself, eliminating multiplicity. Thus the philosophers' own principles undermine their claims.

عقله نفسه
العقل
العاقل
المعقول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Ghazali accepts the identity of intellect and intelligible as a positive doctrine.
Correction: Ghazali uses the identity principle only to refute the philosophers' own argument, not to endorse it.

Checkpoint
What does Ghazali's second objection show?
A) The First Principle has multiplicity
B) The first intellect's intellection of itself must be identical to its essence, preventing multiplicity
C) The intellect cannot intellect itself
D) The effect is separate from its cause

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's second objection shows that the philosophers' own principle of identity between intellect and intelligible, when applied to the first intellect, eliminates multiplicity—contradicting their claim that only the First is simple.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
When you reflect on the multiplicity of distractions in your life, do you see how they fragment your soul? Would you be willing to simplify your heart by making your knowledge of yourself identical to your knowledge of God, so that all your thoughts return to Him?

Clarifying the Meaning of 'Possible' and 'Necessary' in Causality
Ghazali responds to an objection about infinite causes by clarifying the meaning of 'possible' and 'necessary'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali insists that the terms 'possible' and 'necessary' must be precisely defined before arguments about infinite causes can proceed.

Core Idea
Ambiguous terms break arguments — define before you decide.

In this passage, Ghazali faces an objection from the philosophers that an infinite chain of causes is impossible because each cause must be either necessary or possible. Instead of accepting the dichotomy outright, Ghazali pauses to ask: what do we really mean by 'possible' and 'necessary'? This simple question is the key to unlocking his entire critique.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how Ghazali immediately identifies an ambiguity in the key terms.
The objection appears in the first half of the passage; Ghazali's response begins with 'قلنا'.
2
Identify the two definitions
Ghazali offers definitions for 'necessary' and 'possible'. Find these and understand the distinction he makes.
He says 'by necessary is meant what has no cause' and 'by possible is meant what has a cause beyond itself'.
3
Reflect on why this matters
Ask yourself: how does defining these terms affect the objection's validity? Why is this a good move by Ghazali?
This connects to the learning objective about the necessity of precise terms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: البرهان القاطع على استحالة علل لا نهاية لها، أن يقال: كل آحاد العلل، ممكنة في نفسها أو واجبة؟! فإن كانت واجبة، فلم تفتقر إلى علة؟! وإن كانت ممكنة، فالكل موصوف بالإمكان، وكل ممكن فيفتقر إلى علة زائدة على ذاته، فيفتقر الكل إلى علة خارجة عنه. قلنا: لفظ الممكن والواجب لفظ مبهم، إلا أن يراد بالواجب ما لا علة لوجوده، ويراد بالممكن ما لوجوده علة زائدة على ذاته؛ فإن كان المراد هذا، فلنرجع إلى هذه اللفظة، فنقول: كل واحد ممكن: على معنى أن له علة.
If it is said: The decisive proof for the impossibility of infinite causes is that it is said: Each individual cause is either possible in itself or necessary. If it is necessary, then why does it need a cause? And if it is possible, then the whole [chain] is described by possibility, and everything possible requires a cause additional to its essence, so the whole requires an external cause. We say: The term 'possible' [al-mumkin] and 'necessary' [al-wajib] is ambiguous [mubham], except if by 'necessary' is meant what has no cause for its existence, and by 'possible' is meant what has a cause for its existence additional to its essence. If this is meant, then let us return to this expression, and we say: Each individual is possible in the sense that it has a cause.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and Ghazali's preliminary clarification. The objector argues that an infinite chain of causes is impossible because each member must be either necessary or possible. If necessary, it would not need a cause; if possible, then the whole chain would be possible and require an external cause, which would be the First Cause. Ghazali responds that the terms 'possible' and 'necessary' are ambiguous. To make the argument valid, we must define them precisely: 'necessary' means that which has no cause for its existence; 'possible' means that which has a cause for its existence beyond itself. Given that definition, each member of the chain is 'possible' in the sense of having a cause. But this does not automatically prove an infinite chain is impossible; rather, it simply states that each has a cause. The full response continues on the next page, but Ghazali here begins to deconstruct the objection by insisting on clear definitions. This is a typical kalam method: clarifying the meaning of terms before engaging with the argument.

ممكن
واجب
علة
لفظ مبهم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The terms 'possible' and 'necessary' have self-evident meanings that do not require definition.
Correction: Ghazali shows that these terms are ambiguous (mubham) and must be defined before using them in arguments.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, how should the terms 'possible' (mumkin) and 'necessary' (wajib) be defined to avoid ambiguity?
A) Necessary is that which exists, possible is that which does not exist.
B) Necessary is that which can be thought without contradiction, possible is that which cannot.
C) Necessary is that which has no cause, possible is that which has a cause beyond itself.
D) Necessary is that which is eternal, possible is that which is temporal.

Key Takeaway
The meaning of theological terms must be pinned down before they can be used in proofs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
Ghazali insists that we must return to the meaning of words before accepting an argument. When you discuss matters of faith with others, do you first establish common definitions, or do you argue past each other? Will you change how you engage in religious dialogue?

The Impossibility of Knowledge in an Inanimate Object
Ghazali argues impossibility of knowledge in an inanimate object.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali argues that creating knowledge in an inanimate object is logically impossible, grounded in the law of contradiction.

Core Idea
Knowledge cannot be created in an inanimate object without contradicting its definition.

Ghazali presents a tight definitional argument to show that knowledge cannot be created in an inanimate object. He first links will to knowledge, then examines what 'inanimate' means and tests two possibilities: either perception is created or it is not. Both lead to contradiction, revealing that the only true impossibility is the combination of contradictories.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the logical structure: will→knowledge, then two cases for inanimate objects.
This passage is dense; take your time.
2
Understand the definition of inanimate
Identify what 'inanimate' (جماد) means according to Ghazali: something that does not perceive (لا يدرك). This definition is crucial for the argument.
The word 'inanimate' here is a technical term, not just lifeless matter.
3
Follow the two-case argument
Ghazali presents two scenarios: if perception is created in the object, it is no longer inanimate; if no perception is created, then the accident cannot be called knowledge. See how each case leads to a contradiction.
Contradiction arises because we would have to affirm and deny the same thing.
4
Connect to the law of contradiction
Notice how Ghazali compares this to examples like a person in two places or black and white together. He reduces the impossibility to the law of contradiction, citing Aristotle.
This shows that impossibility is not arbitrary but based on logical principles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذلك تفهم من الإرادة ، طلب معلوم ، فإن فرض طلب ولا علم ، لم يكن إرادة فكان فيه نفى ما فهمناه . والجماد يستحيل أن يخلق فيه العلم ، لأنا نفهم من الجماد ، ما لا يدرك ، فإن خلق فيه إدراك فتسميته جماداً ، بالمعنى الذي فهمناه ، محال ، وإن لم يدرك فتسمية الحادث علماً ، ولا يدرك به محله شيئاً، محال ، فهذا وجه استحالته فيؤول إلى نفى الشيء ، إثباته ، كما آل كون الشخص الواحد في مكانين ، وكما مع آل الجمع بين السواد والبياض ، فإن كل ذلك راجع - كما أوضحه - إلى قانون التناقض ، ومن هنا صح ما يروى عن أرسطو من أن المحال هو الجمع بين النقيضين ، فكل ما لا يمكن إرجاعه إلى قانون التناقض فهو غير محال .
And likewise you understand from will, the seeking of something known. For if a seeking is supposed without knowledge, it would not be will, so there would be in it a negation of what we understood. And it is impossible for knowledge to be created in an inanimate object, because we understand from 'inanimate' that which does not perceive. For if perception is created in it, then calling it inanimate, in the sense we understood, is impossible. And if it does not perceive, then calling the accident 'knowledge' while its locus does not perceive anything through it is impossible. This is the aspect of its impossibility. It results in the negation of a thing being its affirmation, just as the case of one person being in two places resulted, and similarly the gathering of blackness and whiteness. For all that reduces - as he explained - to the law of contradiction. And from this is correct what is narrated from Aristotle, that the impossible is the combination of two contradictories. So everything that cannot be reduced to the law of contradiction is not impossible.
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage is a definitional argument demonstrating the impossibility of knowledge being created in an inanimate object. Ghazali begins by connecting will to knowledge: will is the seeking of something known, so if one supposes a seeking without knowledge, it is not will. This establishes the inseparability of knowledge from will. Then he focuses on the inanimate (جماد). The definition of 'inanimate' is that it does not perceive (ما لا يدرك). If God were to create perception in it, then it would no longer fit that definition, so calling it inanimate would be impossible. Conversely, if the created attribute does not cause perception in its locus (the object), then calling it 'knowledge' is also impossible, because knowledge inherently involves perception by the knower. Thus, in either case, it is impossible for knowledge to be created in an inanimate object. Ghazali then explains that this impossibility ultimately reduces to the law of contradiction (قانون التناقض): it would entail affirming and negating the same thing, like a person being in two places simultaneously, or blackness and whiteness coexisting. Just as Aristotle said, the only true impossibility is the combination of contradictories. Therefore, anything that does not reduce to contradiction is not impossible.

Key Terms
إرادة
علم
جماد
إدراك
محال
قانون التناقض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that impossibility is arbitrary or simply due to lack of capacity in the object.
Correction: Ghazali shows that impossibility is grounded in logical contradiction, not arbitrary divine decree or natural incapacity.

Checkpoint
Why does Ghazali say it is impossible for knowledge to be created in an inanimate object?
A) Because inanimate objects lack the capacity for knowledge
B) Because the definition of inanimate excludes perception; if perception is created, the object is no longer inanimate
C) Because knowledge requires a soul, which inanimate objects lack
D) Because God's power does not extend to creating knowledge in inanimate objects

Key Takeaway
Creating knowledge in an inanimate object is impossible because it would contradict the definition of inanimate or the nature of knowledge, both of which reduce to the law of contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In this passage, Ghazali shows that calling something 'knowledge' without perception is impossible. Are there areas in your life where you claim to 'know' something without truly perceiving or understanding it? Will you commit to verifying such knowledge even if it costs you comfort?
?
The law of contradiction is the bedrock of Ghazali's argument. Is there a contradiction in your own beliefs that you have been avoiding? What would it mean for you to honestly confront it today?
?
Ghazali links will to knowledge: you cannot seek what you do not know. Think of a deep spiritual or intellectual truth you say you seek but avoid pursuing with real effort. What is holding you back from truly knowing it?

The Logical Impossibility of Thing-Becoming-Other
Ghazali deconstructs the concept of transformation with a logical trilemma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali considers the concept of a thing turning into another different kind of thing unintelligible through his analysis of blackness becoming turbidity.

Core Idea
Real transformation cannot be mere replacement or addition of properties.

Ghazali begins his refutation by examining what we actually mean when we claim a thing becomes another thing. He tests the idea with a concrete example—blackness turning into turbidity—and shows that every possible interpretation leads to a logical contradiction or reduces to something else. This lesson walks you through that logical test.

Guided Path
1
Read the core argument
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Focus on the three scenarios Ghazali presents for blackness turning into turbidity.
Notice that Ghazali uses a question-and-answer style to eliminate possibilities.
2
Identify the three possibilities
List the three possibilities Ghazali considers for the blackness: (1) it becomes non-existent, (2) it exists alongside turbidity, (3) it remains while turbidity is absent. For each, see how he concludes that no transformation occurred.
3
Understand the conclusion of this part
After eliminating all possibilities, Ghazali concludes that the notion of one substance turning into another distinct substance is not intelligible. Ask yourself: what does this imply for the claim that God can transform species?
This sets the stage for the next lesson on what genuine transformation actually requires.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قلب الأجناس ، فقد قال بعض المتكلمين : إنه مقدور لله تعالى . فنقول : مصير الشيء شيئاً آخر ، غير معقول ، لأن السواد إذا انقلب كدرة ، مثلا ، فالسواد باق أم لا ؟ ! فإن كان معدوماً ، فلم ينقلب ، بل عدم ذاك ، ووجد غيره ، وإن كان موجوداً ، مع الكدرة ، فلم ينقلب ، ولكن انضاف إليه غيره ، وإن بقى السواد ، والكدرة معدومة ، فلم ينقلب ، بل بقى على ما هو عليه. وإذا قلنا : انقلب الدم منيا ، أردنا به أن تلك المادة بعينها ، خلعت صورة ، ولبست صورة أخرى ، فرجع الحاصل ، إلى أن صورة عدمت ، وصورة حدثت ، وثم مادة قائمة ، تعاقبت عليها ، الصورتان . وإذا قلنا : انقلب الماء هواء ، بالتسخين ، أردنا به أن المادة القابلة لصورة المائية ، خلعت هذه الصورة ، وقبلت صورة أخرى ، فالمادة مشتركة والصورة متغيرة . وكذلك : إذا قلنا : انقلبت العصا ثعباناً ، والتراب حيواناً . وليس بين العرض والجوهر مادة مشتركة ولا بين السواد والكدرة ، ولا بين سائر الأجناس مادة مشتركة ، فكان هذا محالا من هذا الوجه . حتى يصح قلب الجوهر عرضاً أو العكس ، كما صح في الدم والمنى؛ والماء والهواء؛ والعصا والثعبان؛ والتراب والحيوان.
As for the transformation of species, some theologians have said that it is within the power of God Most High. So we say: the becoming of a thing into another thing is not intelligible. Because if blackness turns into turbidity, for example, does the blackness remain or not? If it is non-existent, then it did not transform, rather that one ceased and another came to be. If it exists along with the turbidity, then it did not transform, but another was added to it. If the blackness remains while the turbidity is non-existent, then it did not transform, but remained as it was. And when we say 'the blood turned into semen', we mean that that same matter stripped off a form and put on another form. So the result is that a form ceased and a form came to be, and there was a persistent matter upon which the two forms successively followed. And when we say 'water turned into air by heating', we mean that the matter receptive to the form of water stripped off that form and accepted another form. So the matter is shared and the form changes. Similarly when we say 'the staff turned into a snake' and 'the dust turned into an animal'. But there is no shared matter between accident and substance, nor between blackness and turbidity, nor between the rest of the species. So this is impossible from this aspect. For the transformation of substance into an accident or vice versa to be valid, there would need to be a common matter, as was valid in blood and semen, water and air, staff and snake, dust and animal.
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the claim that 'transformation of species' (قلب الأجناس) is within God's power. Ghazali begins by stating that the notion of a thing becoming another thing is unintelligible. He analyzes a hypothetical case: if blackness becomes turbidity, one must consider whether the blackness remains. If it ceases, then no transformation occurred—it is merely replacement. If both exist together, it is addition, not transformation. If only blackness remains, again no change. Thus, the concept of transformation is incoherent. He then explains what is commonly meant by transformation in physical changes like blood-to-semen or water-to-air: a common underlying matter (مادة) remains while the forms (صور) change. This is a change of form, not substance. However, between different categories (أجناس) like accident (عرض) and substance (جوهر), or blackness and turbidity (which are different species), there is no shared matter. Therefore, the kind of transformation that would involve turning one species into another is impossible because it lacks a common substrate. The footnote adds that for such transformation to be valid, a common matter would be needed, as seen in examples where the matter is shared.

Key Terms
قلب الأجناس
سواد
كدرة
معدوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That transformation of species is simply a change in properties while the thing remains the same.
Correction: Ghazali shows that if the properties change, either the original thing is replaced or added to, not transformed. True transformation requires a common matter that persists, which is absent in species change.

Checkpoint
In Ghazali's analysis of blackness turning into turbidity, what happens if blackness remains while turbidity is non-existent?
A) Blackness transforms into turbidity
B) Blackness is replaced by turbidity
C) No transformation occurs; blackness remains as it was
D) Blackness and turbidity coexist

Key Takeaway
Ghazali argues that the concept of one thing becoming another different kind of thing is logically incoherent when examined through simple examples.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you ask God to change you—your habits, your character, your heart—do you expect the old you to die and be replaced, or do you expect a gradual addition of new qualities? Which scenario does Ghazali's argument show is actually coherent, and what does that mean for how you pray for change?
?
If even God cannot make a thing become another thing without logical contradiction, then what does that say about your desires for radical personal transformation? Are you asking for the impossible, or are you willing to accept the only kind of real change possible?
?
The thinkers Ghazali argues against claimed transformation is within God's power. Do you ever assume that God's power means He can do anything you imagine, even logical absurdities? How does this argument force you to rethink what it means to say 'nothing is impossible for God'?

Genuine Transformation Requires Common Matter
Ghazali defines genuine transformation and denies it across species.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that what we call transformation actually involves a shared matter that changes form, and why this is impossible between different species like substance and accident.

Core Idea
Real change is form-change on a common matter, not species-change.

Having shown that simple replacement or addition is not transformation, Ghazali now explains what genuine transformation actually means. He uses examples like blood turning into semen and water turning into air to illustrate that real change requires a persistent common matter. He then argues that between different species (e.g., accident and substance) no such common matter exists, making species transformation impossible.

Guided Path
1
Understand the examples of genuine transformation
Read Ghazali's examples: blood to semen, water to air, staff to snake, dust to animal. Identify what remains the same (the matter) and what changes (the form).
Notice that in each case, there is an underlying matter that persists.
2
Grasp the key requirement: common matter
Ghazali states that in all valid transformations, the matter is shared (مادة مشتركة) and only the form changes. This is the criterion for a genuine transformation.
3
Apply the criterion to species transformation
Now see why transformation of species is impossible: between different species (e.g., blackness and turbidity, accident and substance) there is no common matter. Therefore, the kind of change that turns one species into another is impossible.
This concludes Ghazali's argument.

Source Text

The Philosophers' First Proof: The Soul is Non-Bodily
Ghazali presents the philosophers' first proof that the soul is non-bodily.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of the philosophers' first argument that the soul is a non-bodily substance.

Core Idea
The indivisibility of sciences implies an indivisible soul.

This passage presents the first philosophical proof that the human soul is a non-bodily substance. The argument is based on the nature of intellectual sciences: they contain indivisible units, so their locus must be indivisible. Since every body is divisible, the soul cannot be a body. Ghazali states this proof using a conditional syllogism, which we will analyze.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation carefully. Notice the logical connectives and how the argument is structured.
The passage contains a conditional statement and a negation of the consequent.
2
Identify premises and conclusion
Break down the argument: what are the premises? What conclusion follows? Look for 'if... then' and 'but... so'.
The key premise is that sciences are indivisible; the conclusion is that the locus is not a body.
3
Recognize the logical form
The argument is a conditional syllogism (qiyās shartī) where the consequent is negated. This is known as modus tollens.
Understanding the logical form helps evaluate the argument later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قولهم الأول : إن العلوم العقلية ، تحل النفوس الإنسانية ، وهي محصورة ، وفيها آحاد لا تنقسم ، فلابد أن يكون محلها أيضاً لا ينقسم ، وكل جسم ، فمنقسم فدل على أن محلها شيء لا ينقسم . ويمكن إيراد هذا على شرط المنطق بأشكاله ، ولكن أقربه أن يقال : إن كان محل العلم جسما منقسما ، فالعلم الحال فيه أيضاً منقسم ، لكن الحال غير منقسم ، فالمحل ليس جسما . وهذا « قياس شرطى ( استثنى فيه « نقيض التالي »
Their first statement: The intellectual sciences reside in human souls, and they are limited, and among them are indivisible units, so necessarily their locus also must be indivisible. And every body is divisible, so this indicates that their locus is something indivisible. It is possible to express this according to the condition of logic with its figures, but the closest is to say: If the locus of science were a divisible body, then the science residing in it would also be divisible; but the resident (science) is not divisible, so the locus is not a body. This is a conditional syllogism (qiyās shartī) in which the contradictory of the consequent is excluded.
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage presents the first philosophical proof for the soul as a non-bodily substance. Ghazali states the argument: The intellectual sciences reside in human souls. These sciences are limited (finite) and contain indivisible units (like individual concepts). Therefore, their locus must also be indivisible. Every body is divisible, so the locus is not a body. He notes it can be formulated as a logical syllogism, but gives a concise version: If the locus (the soul) were a divisible body, then the science in it would be divisible. But the science is not divisible (since thoughts are not split), so the locus is not a body. This is a conditional syllogism (modus tollens).

Key Terms
العلوم العقلية
محل العلم
الجسم المنقسم
قياس شرطي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the philosophers' first proof, what is the reason the locus of sciences cannot be a body?
A) Because bodies are eternal
B) Because sciences are divisible, so the body must be divisible
C) Because sciences are indivisible, but every body is divisible
D) Because bodies cannot contain sciences

Key Takeaway
The philosophers argue that since sciences contain indivisible units, their locus (the soul) cannot be a divisible body.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your soul is the locus of knowledge that cannot be divided, then every thought you hold is entrusted to something indivisible. How does this truth change the way you treat your own mind? Do you guard your thoughts as something sacred, or do you neglect them as just another part of your body?
?
The philosophers argue that because you have indivisible knowledge, your soul cannot be a body. But your actions often suggest you live as if your soul is just another physical thing—driven by desires and habits. Which actions in your life today betray a belief that your soul is divisible? Will you choose one act this week that reflects the indivisible nature of your soul?
?
Consider a single, unshakable conviction you hold about God. According to this argument, that conviction is an indivisible unit of science in your soul. If your soul were divisible, that conviction could be split or lost. Do you live with the confidence that your faith is preserved by an indivisible locus, or do you let distractions chip away at it? What will you do to protect that conviction?

Why Knowledge is Not Like Color: Ghazali's Refutation
Ghazali refutes the analogy of knowledge as impression.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's critique of the philosophers' analogy that knowledge is impressed in the body like color, and his argument that the relation of knowledge to the body may be non-spatial, thereby showing their claim lacks certainty.

Core Idea
Knowledge may relate to the body not by spatial impression but by a relation like perceiving enmity.

In this passage, Ghazali takes on the philosophers' analogy that knowledge resides in the body like color in a colored object. He identifies the key point of confusion: the word 'impression' assumes a spatial relationship that may not apply to knowledge. By proposing an alternative relation—like the perception of enmity, which is not spatially extended—he undermines the analogy and argues that their conclusion is mere conjecture, not certain knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Let Ghazali's argument unfold naturally.
2
Identify the point of confusion
Ghazali says the philosophers neglected the point of deception in the analogy. What is that deception? Look for the word 'impression' (al-intibāʿ) and why it is flawed.
3
Examine the alternative relation
Ghazali suggests that knowledge might relate to the body like the perception of enmity. Think about why that is different from color—enmity is not spatially extended.
This shows the relation could be non-spatial.
4
Understand the broader principle
Ghazali generalizes: we do not know all the ways attributes relate to their loci. So judging without full knowledge is unreliable.
5
Reflect on epistemic certainty
The philosophers' claim, at best, yields strong conjecture, not certain knowledge free from doubt. This is the core conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول : هذه المناقضة ، تبين أنهم غفلوا عن موضع تلبيس فى القياس ، ولعل موضع الالتباس قولهم : إن العلم منطبع في الجسم ، انطباع اللون في المتلون ، وينقسم اللون بانقسام المتلون ، فينقسم العلم بانقسام محله ، والخلل في لفظ الانطباع ، إذ يمكن ألا تكون نسبة العلم إلى محله ، كنسبة اللون إلى المتلون ، حتى يقال : إنه منبسط عليه ومنطبع فيه ومنتشر في جوانبه ، فينقسم بانقسامه ، فلعل نسبة العلم إلى محله ، على وجه آخر ، وذلك الوجه لا يجوز فيه الانقسام ، عند انقسام المحل ، بل نسبته إليه ، كنسبة إدراك العداوة إلى الجسم . و وجوه نسبة الأوصاف إلى محالها ، ليست محصورة فى فن واحد ، ولا هي معلومة التفاصيل لنا ، علماً نثق به ، فالحكم عليه دون الاحاطة، بتفصيل النسب حكم غير موثوق به . وعلى الجملة لا ينكر أن ما ذكروه ، مما يقوى الظن ، ويغلبه ، وإنما ينكر كونه معلوماً يقيناً ، علماً لا يجوز الغلط فيه ، ولا يتطرق إليه الشك ، وهذا القدر مشكك فيه
Then we say: This contradiction shows that they neglected the point of deception in the analogy. Perhaps the point of confusion is their statement that knowledge is impressed in the body like the impression of color in the colored object, and color is divided by the division of the colored object, so knowledge is divided by the division of its locus. The flaw is in the word 'impression', because it is possible that the relation of knowledge to its locus is not like the relation of color to the colored object, such that it is said that it extends over it and is impressed in it and spreads in its parts, so it is divided by its division. Perhaps the relation of knowledge to its locus is in another manner, and that manner does not permit division when the locus is divided. Rather, its relation to it is like the perception of enmity to the body. The aspects of the relation of attributes to their loci are not confined to one art, nor are their details known to us with certain knowledge. So judging without encompassing the details of the relations is an unreliable judgment. In sum, it is not denied that what they mentioned strengthens and overcomes conjecture, but it is denied that it is known with certainty, a knowledge in which error is impossible and doubt does not approach it. This matter is open to doubt.
Source pages p. 259

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the philosophers' analogy that knowledge is 'impressed' in the body like color in a colored object. Ghazali begins by identifying the point of confusion: the term 'impression' (al-intibāʿ). He argues that the relation of knowledge to its bodily locus may be fundamentally different from that of color. Color extends over the colored object and is divisible with it, but knowledge might have a relation more like the perception of enmity (which is a relation not spatially extended). He then generalizes: the ways attributes relate to their loci are not all known in detail, so a judgment without comprehensive knowledge of relations is unreliable. He concludes that the philosophers' argument yields only conjecture (ẓann), not certain knowledge (yaqīn) free from error. Thus, their proof is not decisive. Ghazali follows a point-by-point critique: first identifies the flaw, then offers an alternative conception, then notes the general fallibility of such claims.

Key Terms
علم
جسم
انطباع
نسبة
يقين
ظن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Knowledge must be divisible if its locus (the body) is divisible, because all attributes relate to their loci by spatial extension.
Correction: Ghazali argues that the relation of knowledge to its locus may be non-spatial, like the perception of enmity, which does not divide with the body.

Checkpoint
What is the fundamental flaw Ghazali identifies in the philosophers' analogy between knowledge and color?
A) The philosophers admit they are unsure about the analogy.
B) The term 'impression' might not accurately describe the relation of knowledge to the body.
C) Color is not actually divisible in the way they claim.
D) The body is not actually divisible.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali shows that the philosophers' analogy of knowledge as impression fails because the relation of knowledge to the body may be non-spatial, undermining their claim to certainty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held a belief about your faith simply because it seemed logical or persuasive, without subjecting it to rigorous scrutiny? Which analogy or assumption in your own thinking might be like the philosophers' 'impression'—accepted without critical reflection?
?
Ghazali warns that judging without full knowledge of the relations is unreliable. In what areas of your life—your character, your relationships, your worship—have you made judgments without understanding the deeper relations? What would it take to seek certainty instead?
?
The term 'impression' sounded plausible but hid a deception. What seemingly harmless but potentially misleading ideas have you internalized about Islam from culture, family, or even scholars? Are you ready to re-examine them, even if it means abandoning comfortable certainties?

The Necessity and Impossibility of Relation in Knowledge
Ghazali proves that knowledge must have a relation to the knower, then refutes three possible forms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why knowledge must have a relation to the knower and why all possible forms of that relation lead to contradiction.

Core Idea
Relation is necessary; every possible form is impossible.

In this passage, Ghazali deepens the critique of the philosophers' claim that knowledge is impressed in a divisible locus. He proves that relation is indispensable, and then systematically shows that no form of relation can avoid absurdity. The argument is a masterclass in logical enumeration and refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the main claim
Read the Arabic and translation to see the overall structure: Ghazali establishes that relation is necessary, then enumerates three possibilities and refutes each.
2
Understand why relation cannot be cut
Notice the first step: if there is no relation, any knower could know the object equally, which is absurd.
This shows the necessity of relation.
3
Follow the refutation of the three cases
Trace how each of the three possible forms of relation is shown impossible, leading to the conclusion that the original assumption is false.
Pay attention to the logical contradictions in each case.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دلیل ثان - قالوا : إن كان العلم بالمعلوم الواحد العقلى – وهو المعلوم المجرد عن المواد منطبعاً في المادة ، انطباع الأعراض في الجواهر الجسمانية ، لزم انقسامه بالضرورة بانقسام الجسم كما سبق ، وإن لم يكن منطبعاً فيها ، ولا منبسطاً عليها . وإن استكره لفظ الانطباع ، فلنعدل إلى عبارة أخرى ، ونقول : هل للعلم نسبة إلى العالم به ، أم لا ؟ ! ومحال قطع النسبة ، لأنه إن قطعت النسبة عنه ؛ فكونه عالماً به ، لم صار أولى به ، من كون غيره عالماً به ؟ ! وإن كان له نسبة ، فلا يخلو من ثلاثة أقسام : إما أن تكون النسبة لكل جزء من أجزاء المحل . أو تكون لبعض أجزاء المحل دون بعض . أو لا يكون لواحد من الأجزاء نسبة إليه . وباطل أن يقال : لا نسبة لواحد من الأجزاء ، فإنه إذا لم يكن للآحاد نسبة ، لم يكن للمجموع نسبة ، فإن المجتمع من المباينات ، مباين . وباطل أن يقال : النسبة للبعض ، فإن الذى لا نسبة له ، ليس هو من معناه فى شيء ، وليس كلامنا فيه . وباطل أن يقال : لكل جزء مفروض نسبة إلى ذات العلم ، لأنه إن كانت النسبة إلى ذات العلم بأسره ، فمعلوم كل واحد من الأجزاء ليس هو جزءاً من المعلوم ، بل هو المعلوم كما هو ، فيكون معقولا مرات لا نهاية لها بالفعل ، وإن كان كل جزء له نسبة أخرى غير النسبة التي للجزء الآخر إلى ذات العلم ، فذات العلم إذن منقسمة في المعنى ، وقد بينا أن العلم بالمعلوم الواحد من كل وجه ، ينقسم في المعنى ، وإن كانت نسبة كل واحد إلى شيء من ذات العلم، غير ما إليه نسبة الآخر ، فانقسام ذات العلم بهذه الطريقة أظهر وهو محال
Second proof. They said: If knowledge of a single intelligible — that is, the intelligible abstracted from matter — is impressed in matter, similar to the impression of accidents in corporeal substances, then its division would necessarily follow from the division of the body, as previously mentioned. And if it is not impressed in it nor extended over it — even if the word 'impression' is disliked, let us turn to another expression — we say: Does knowledge have a relation to the knower or not? It is impossible to cut off the relation, because if the relation is cut off from it, then why is it more fitting for it to be a knower of it than for someone else to be a knower of it? And if it has a relation, it cannot be free from three divisions: either the relation is to every part of the locus, or is to some parts of the locus without others, or there is no relation to any of the parts. It is false that there is no relation to any of the parts, because if there is no relation to the individuals, then there is no relation to the aggregate, for the aggregate of disparate things is disparate. It is false that the relation is to some [parts], because that which has no relation is not included in its meaning, and we are not discussing it. It is false that every assumed part has a relation to the essence of knowledge, because if the relation is to the entire essence of knowledge, then each of the parts is not part of the known object but is the known object as it is, so it would be intelligible an infinite number of times de facto. And if each part has another relation, different from the relation of the other part to the essence of knowledge, then the essence of knowledge is as such divided in meaning. And we have shown that knowledge of a single object from every aspect is divided in meaning. And if the relation of each [part] is to something of the essence of knowledge, different from that to which the other is related, then the division of the essence of knowledge in this way is more apparent, and it is impossible.
Source pages p. 259

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' second argument and its refutation. It begins by restating the dilemma: if knowledge is impressed in matter, it must divide with the body; if not, the term 'impression' is dropped and replaced with 'relation'. The argument then forces a choice: Does knowledge have a relation to the knower or not? If there is no relation, then any knower could know the same object equally, which is absurd. So there must be a relation. This relation must take one of three forms: (1) to every part of the locus, (2) to some parts only, (3) to no part. Each is then shown to be impossible. (1) If to every part, then each part would know the entire object, leading to an infinite number of knowings. (2) If to some parts, the parts without relation are irrelevant, and the relation cannot be to a subset without absurdity. (3) If to no part, then the aggregate has no relation. Thus, all possibilities are impossible, proving that the original assumption — that knowledge is in a divisible locus — leads to contradiction. Ghazali follows a rigorous logical structure: establishing a dichotomy, enumerating possibilities, and refuting each.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If knowledge is not divisible, it must have no relation to the body.
Correction: Ghazali argues that relation is necessary; the impossibility comes from the specific forms of relation, not from having a relation.

Checkpoint
What is the first possibility for the relation of knowledge to the parts of its locus that Ghazali refutes?
A) The relation is to every part.
B) The relation is to no part.
C) The relation is to some parts.
D) The relation is to the whole only.

Key Takeaway
Knowledge must have a relation to the knower, and all possible forms of that relation are impossible, thus the original assumption that knowledge is in a divisible locus is false.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often think of your faith as a set of beliefs stored in your mind. But this lesson forces you to ask: Is your knowledge of God truly a relation to your very self, or is it just a passive imprint? If it is a relation, how can it be that your physical state (tiredness, distraction) affects your awareness of God? Does that not imply your knowledge is divisible and dependent on your body? Examine your own experience: When you are physically weak, does your certainty in God waver? If so, what does that say about the reality of your knowledge? Commit today to seeking a knowledge that is not contingent on your physical condition.
?
The philosophers' argument shows that if knowledge were in a divisible locus, it would lead to absurdities. But consider your own acts of knowing: You read this lesson in a particular place, with your eyes scanning letters. Is your understanding of this argument itself 'located' in your brain? If so, does it also suffer from the same problems? How can you reconcile your physical act of learning with the immaterial reality of knowledge? Let this question unsettle you until you find an answer that does not reduce knowledge to mere brain states.
?
Ghazali shows that cutting off the relation makes any knower equal. Do you ever treat your knowledge of Allah as if it were unrelated to you—something you can acquire and then put aside, like a book on a shelf? If your knowledge had no real relation to your soul, why would you be more accountable than someone who never studied? Let this thought press you: Your knowledge must be a living relation, not a dead fact. What will you do today to ensure your knowledge of these truths is truly related to you?

Ghazali's Extension to Sense Perception and Rebuttal
Ghazali extends argument to sense perception and rebuts term change.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali applies his argument to sense perception and why changing terminology does not resolve the logical problem.

Core Idea
Changing words cannot change the underlying logical problem.

In this passage, Ghazali extends his critique from intellectual knowledge to sense perception. He first explains how the philosophers conceive of sensation as an impression of forms, then shows that even if they replace the term 'impression' with 'relation', the same objections apply. The example of the ewe perceiving the wolf's enmity illustrates the persistence of the problem.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once carefully, then read the English translation. Let the full argument settle before analyzing.
The Arabic is from Tahafut al-Falasifa.
2
Identify Ghazali's definition of perception
Find where Ghazali defines what perception means. Notice he says perception involves obtaining an 'example' (mithal) of the perceived object in the soul.
3
Understand the objection via the ewe example
The ewe's perception of the wolf's enmity is used as a test case. Ask yourself: What is the 'relation' here? Why does the same problem arise?
The 'estimative faculty' (al-quwwa al-wahmiyya) is a technical term in Islamic psychology.
4
See why replacing 'impression' with 'relation' fails
Ghazali argues that the same difficulties about division apply to the relation. Changing the label does not avoid the original objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن هذا يتبين أن المحسوسات المنطبعة فى الحواس الخمس ، لا تكون إلا أمثلة ، لصور جزئية منقسمة ، فإن الإدراك معناه حصول مثال المدرك ، في نفس المدرك ، ويكون لكل جزء من مثال المحسوس ، نسبة إلى جزء من الآلة الجسمانية . والاعتراض على هذا ما سبق ، فإن تبديل لفظ الانطباع بلفظ النسبة ، لا يدرأ الشبهة ، فيما ينطبع في القوة الوهمية للشاة ، من عداوة الذئب ، كما ذكروه ، فإنه إدراك لا محالة ، وله نسبه إليه ، ويلزم في تلك النسبة ما ذكرتموه
From this it becomes clear that the sensibles impressed in the five senses are only examples of partial divisible forms. For perception means the obtaining of an example of the perceived object in the soul of the perceiver, and each part of the example of the sensible has a relation to a part of the bodily instrument. The objection to this is what preceded, for replacing the word 'impression' with the word 'relation' does not avert the doubt concerning what is impressed in the estimative faculty of the ewe, of the enmity of the wolf, as they mentioned, for it is perception without doubt, and it has a relation to it, and what you mentioned necessarily applies to that relation.
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage is an application of the previous refutation to the case of sense perception. Ghazali points out that the same problem arises for sensibles perceived by the five senses: they are only examples of partial divisible forms. Perception is defined as obtaining an example of the perceived object in the soul, and each part of that example has a relation to a part of the bodily organ. He then delivers the final rebuttal: replacing the term 'impression' with 'relation' does not remove the doubt. He uses the example of the ewe's perception of the wolf's enmity in its estimative faculty (al-quwwa al-wahmiyya). That perception certainly has a relation to the ewe, and the same difficulties about relation and division apply. Thus, changing terminology is futile; the underlying problem remains. This demonstrates that the philosophers' attempted evasion fails, and the original objection stands.

Correction: Ghazali shows that the same problem applies to sensibles because perception involves obtaining an example that is divisible and has parts relating to bodily organs, so the objection holds for sensation and estimation as well.

Fifth proof: The intellect's self-awareness proves it is not bodily
Ghazali refutes the philosophers' proof that intellect's self-knowledge shows it is non-bodily.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's two refutations of the philosophers' proof that the intellect's self-awareness implies it is non-bodily.

Core Idea
Self-awareness does not prove non-bodiliness; senses differ and customs can be broken.

The philosophers argued that since the intellect is aware of itself, it cannot be a bodily sense. Ghazali challenges this by questioning the analogy with the senses and noting that the logical implication must be proven. In this passage, he presents two key refutations: one based on the possibility of a sense perceiving itself, and another on the diversity of sense perceptions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the flow of argument. Notice the structure: philosophers' proof, Ghazali's objection, philosophers' support, and Ghazali's two refutations.
The passage uses logical terminology like 'استثناء نقيض التالي'.
2
Identify the philosophers' proof
Find the conditional statement: if intellect uses a bodily instrument, it cannot perceive itself. But it does perceive itself, so the antecedent is false. Notice the logical form.
This is a contrapositive argument.
3
Understand Ghazali's first refutation
Ghazali argues that a sense could in principle perceive itself, though custom prevents it. But customs can be violated (miracles). Thus the analogy fails even for vision.
This refutation is based on Ash'ari occasionalism.
4
Understand Ghazali's second refutation
Even if some senses cannot perceive themselves, it does not follow for all. Senses differ in mode (touch vs. sight). So intellect might be a bodily sense that perceives itself.
This is considered the stronger refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دلیل خامس
قولهم : إن كان العقل يدرك بآلة جسمانية ، فهو لا يعقل نفسه ، والتالى محال ، لأنه يعقل نفسه ، فالمقدم محال
قلنا : مسلم أن "استثناء نقيض التالي" ، ينتج "نقيض المقدم" ، ولكن إذا ثبت اللزوم بين التالي والمقدم ، بل نقول : لا يسلم لزوم التالى للمقدم وما الدليل عليه
فإن قيل : الدليل عليه أن الإبصار لما كان بجسم ، فالإبصار لا يتعلق بالإبصار فالرؤية لا ترى ، والسمع لا يسمع ، وكذا سائر الحواس ، فإن كان العقل أيضاً لا يدرك إلا بجسم ، فلا يدرك نفسه ، والعقل كما يعقل غيره ، يعقل نفسه ، فإن الواحد منا ، كما يعقل غيره ، يعقل نفسه ، ويعقل أنه عقل غيره ، وأنه عقل نفسه
قلنا : ما ذكرتموه فاسد من وجهين أحدهما : أن الإبصار عندنا ، يجوز أن يتعلق بنفسه، فيكون إبصاراً لغيره ولنفسه ، كما يكون العلم الواحد ، علماً بغيره ، وعلماً بنفسه ، ولكن العادة جارية بخلاف ذلك ، وخرق العادات عندنا جائز
والثانى : وهو أقوى، أنا نسلم هذا فى الحواس ، ولكن لم قلتم : إذا امتنع ذلك في بعض الحواس ، يمتنع في بعض ؟ ! وأى بعد في أن يفترق حكم الحواس فى وجه الإدراك ، مع اشتراكها في أنها جسمانية ؟ ! كما اختلف البصر واللمس ، فى أن اللمس لا يفيد الإدراك ، إلا باتصال الملموس بالآلة اللامسة ، وكذا الذوق ، ويخالفها البصر ، فإنه يشترط فيه الانفصال ، حتى إن الواحد إذا أطبق أجفانه ، لم ير لون الجفون ، لأنها لم تبعد
.
وهذا الاختلاف ، لا يوجب الاختلاف في الحاجة إلى الجسم ، فلا يبعد أن يكون فى الحواس الجسمانية ، ما يسمى عقلا ، ويخالف سائرها ، في أنها تدرك نفسها
Fifth Proof. Their statement: If the intellect perceives by a bodily instrument, then it does not intellect itself; and the consequent is impossible, because it intellects itself; so the antecedent is impossible. We said: It is granted that 'the exception of the contradictory of the consequent' produces 'the contradictory of the antecedent', but only if the implication between the consequent and the antecedent is established. Rather we say: The implication of the consequent for the antecedent is not conceded; and what is the proof of it? If it is said: The proof of it is that when vision is by a body, vision does not pertain to vision, so sight does not see [itself], and hearing does not hear [itself], and likewise the rest of the senses. Then if the intellect also perceives only by a body, it does not perceive itself; but the intellect, just as it intellects other things, intellects itself; so the one among us, just as he intellects other things, intellects himself, and intellects that he intellects other things, and that he intellects himself. We said: What you mentioned is invalid from two aspects. One of them: For us, vision can pertain to itself, so it would be a sight of other and of itself, just as a single knowledge is knowledge of other and knowledge of itself; but custom runs contrary to that, and the violation of customs is permissible for us. The second, which is stronger: We concede this for the senses; but why did you say: If that is impossible in some senses, it is impossible in some? And what distance is there that the judgment of the senses might differ in the mode of perception, despite their sharing in being bodily? As sight and touch differ in that touch does not yield perception except with the connection of the touched object to the touching instrument, and likewise taste, but sight differs from them, for it requires separation, so much so that if one closes his eyelids, he does not see the color of the eyelids because they are not distant. And this difference does not necessitate difference in the need for a body; so it is not far that among the bodily senses there is what is called intellect and differs from the rest in that it perceives itself.
Source pages p. 263

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' fifth proof for the non-bodiliness of the intellect and Ghazali's refutation. The proof runs: If the intellect perceives by a bodily instrument, it would not be able to perceive itself (by analogy with the senses which cannot perceive themselves). But the intellect does perceive itself; therefore it does not have a bodily instrument. Ghazali first notes that the logical structure (contrapositive) is valid only if the implication is granted. He then reports the opponent's support: the senses do not perceive themselves, so if intellect is like them, it too cannot. Ghazali then gives two refutations. The first: it is not impossible for a sense to perceive itself; vision could, in principle, see itself, but custom prevents it; but violation of custom is possible (miracles). The second (stronger): even if it is impossible for some senses to perceive themselves, it does not follow for all; the senses differ in modes of perception (e.g., touch requires contact, sight requires distance). Thus, intellect might be a different kind of bodily sense that can perceive itself. Therefore the proof fails.

عقل
إبصار
آلة جسمانية
استثناء نقيض التالي
عادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Ghazali denies the intellect's self-awareness.
Correction: Ghazali does not deny self-awareness; he denies that it proves non-bodiliness.

Checkpoint
What is the second and stronger refutation Ghazali gives against the philosophers' proof?
A) The senses cannot perceive themselves at all, so the intellect is different.
B) The senses differ in their modes of perception, so intellect may be a sense that perceives itself.
C) Custom prevents vision from seeing itself, but it is possible in principle.
D) The intellect does not actually perceive itself because it is a mental abstraction.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali refutes the philosophers' proof by showing that the analogy with the senses is flawed and that the logical implication is not established.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that because something is true in one area of your life (like a habit or a relationship), it must be true in another—without checking the underlying cause? What would it cost you to examine that assumption today?
?
Ghazali points out that what seems impossible can be made possible if God breaks the custom. Where in your own worldview do you treat God’s power as limited by your experience? Will you ask God to expand your vision beyond what you think is possible?
?
The philosophers relied on an intellectual argument to prove the intellect’s nature, yet they missed a flaw in their own reasoning. Are you willing to let someone point out a blind spot in your most cherished beliefs—even if it makes you uncomfortable?

Ghazali's Objection to the Sixth Proof
Ghazali presents and objects to the sixth proof from the intellect's perception of its organ.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's critique of the argument that the intellect's perception of its own organ proves it is non-bodily.

Core Idea
Perception of one's own organ does not disprove bodiliness.

In this passage, Ghazali presents the philosophers' sixth proof for the non-bodiliness of the intellect, based on its ability to perceive its own instrument (the brain or heart). He then offers two objections, showing that the proof's inference is not logically necessary. The lesson examines both the proof and the rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof
Read the philosophers' argument that if the intellect were bodily, it could not perceive its own organ, but it does, so it is not bodily.
Note the analogy with sight.
2
Identify Ghazali's first objection
Ghazali suggests that custom, not impossibility, prevents sight from seeing its own eye.
3
Identify Ghazali's second objection
He questions whether all senses must be alike in not perceiving their own organs.
The text cuts off here.
4
Reflect on the argument
Consider why Ghazali's objections undermine the proof's certainty.
This shows the fallibility of relying on empirical analogies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دلیل سادس
قالوا : لو كان العقل يدرك بآلة جسمانية ، كالإبصار ، لما أدرك آلته كسائر الحواس ، ولكنه يدرك الدماغ والقلب ، وما يدعى أنه آلة له ، فدل أنه ليس آلة له ولا محلا ، وإلا لما أدركه .
*
الاعتراض على هذا الكلام ، كالاعتراض على الذي قبله ، فإنا نقول : لا يبعد أن يدرك الإبصار محله ، ولكنه جرى على العادة
أو نقول : لم يستحيل أن نفترق الحواس فى هذا المعنى ؟ ! وإن اشتركت في الانطباع فى الأجسام كما سبق ، ولم قلتم : إن ما هو قائم في
(۱) يعني « آخر »
جسم ،
Sixth Proof. They said: If the intellect perceived by a bodily instrument, like sight, it would not perceive its instrument like the rest of the senses; but it perceives the brain and the heart and what is claimed to be its instrument, so that proves that it is not its instrument nor a locus; otherwise it would not perceive it. The objection to this speech is like the objection to the one before it. For we say: It is not far that sight perceives its locus, but it goes according to custom. Or we say: Is it impossible that the senses differ in this meaning? Even if they share in being impressed in bodies as before? And why do you say: That which subsists in a body?
Source pages p. 264

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' sixth proof and Ghazali's objection. The proof: if the intellect were bodily like sight, it would not be able to perceive its own organ (just as sight does not see the eye); but the intellect does perceive its organ (e.g., brain or heart); therefore it is not a bodily faculty. Ghazali's objection mirrors his previous one: he gives two possibilities. First, it is not impossible for sight to perceive its own location (i.e., the eye), but custom prevents it; custom could be broken. Second, the senses may differ in this characteristic even if they all are bodily impressed. The text cuts off mid-sentence, but the gist is that the proof is not conclusive.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ghazali denies that the intellect can perceive its own organ.
Correction: Ghazali does not deny the perception; he questions the inference from 'does not perceive' (by analogy) to 'is not bodily'.

Checkpoint
What is Ghazali's main objection to the sixth proof?
A) The intellect does not actually perceive the brain or heart.
B) The proof is logically invalid because the senses might differ in what they perceive.
C) The philosophers have not proven that the intellect is bodily.
D) The proof assumes the intellect is like sight, but it could be like touch.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's objection shows that the empirical analogy between intellect and sight is not sufficient to prove the intellect is non-bodily.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Ghazali argues that what seems 'impossible' may just be a matter of custom. Is there a belief you hold about your own mind or soul that you have never examined because it seems 'obvious'? What would it take for you to genuinely challenge that belief today?
?
The philosophers used an analogy between sight and intellect to prove that the intellect is non-bodily. Think of an analogy you rely on in your own understanding of something important. When was the last time you examined whether that analogy truly holds, or could it be merely customary?
?
You have just seen that Ghazali objects by suggesting that even if senses are bodily, they may differ in their properties. How willing are you to accept diversity in how things work, even when they belong to the same category? Does your current worldview allow for such flexibility, or do you force uniformity where it may not exist?
