Ghazali's First Objection: Vision Can Perceive Itself by Analogy
First objection: vision may perceive itself, like knowledge, and custom can be broken.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's first argument that vision can potentially perceive itself, analogous to knowledge, and the role of breaking customs.

Core Idea
Vision could perceive itself just as knowledge knows itself, but custom prevents it.

Ghazali begins his refutation by challenging the assumption that senses cannot perceive themselves. He draws a compelling analogy with knowledge, which can be both of itself and of other things. This lesson unpacks that analogy and introduces the key Ash'ari principle that God can break natural customs.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for the first objection
Read the Arabic and translation for the first objection. Notice the analogy between vision and knowledge.
2
Analyse the analogy
Identify the parallel: just as one knowledge can know itself and other things, so vision could see itself and other things. Ask: what makes this plausible?
Ghazali is not saying it actually happens, but that it is rationally possible.
3
Understand 'breaking customs'
Ghazali says custom runs contrary, but breaking customs (kharaq al-‘ādāt) is permissible. This is a core Ash'ari principle that God can alter natural patterns.
This shows Ghazali is working within a theological framework.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قلنا : ما ذكرتموه فاسد من وجهين أحدهما : أن الإبصار عندنا ، يجوز أن يتعلق بنفسه، فيكون إبصاراً لغيره ولنفسه ، كما يكون العلم الواحد ، علماً بغيره ، وعلماً بنفسه ، ولكن العادة جارية بخلاف ذلك ، وخرق العادات عندنا جائز
والثانى : وهو أقوى، أنا نسلم هذا فى الحواس ، ولكن لم قلتم : إذا امتنع ذلك في بعض الحواس ، يمتنع في بعض ؟ ! وأى بعد في أن يفترق حكم الحواس فى وجه الإدراك ، مع اشتراكها في أنها جسمانية ؟ ! كما اختلف البصر واللمس ، فى أن اللمس لا يفيد الإدراك ، إلا باتصال الملموس بالآلة اللامسة ، وكذا الذوق ، ويخالفها البصر ، فإنه يشترط فيه الانفصال ، حتى إن الواحد إذا أطبق أجفانه ، لم ير لون الجفون ، لأنها لم تبعد .
وهذا الاختلاف ، لا يوجب الاختلاف في الحاجة إلى الجسم ، فلا يبعد أن يكون فى الحواس الجسمانية ، ما يسمى عقلا ، ويخالف سائرها ، في أنها تدرك نفسها
We said: What you have mentioned is corrupt from two aspects. First: that vision, according to us, may relate to itself, so it would be vision of other and of itself, just as a single knowledge can be knowledge of other and knowledge of itself, but custom runs contrary to that, and breaking customs is permissible for us. Second, and it is stronger: we concede this regarding the senses, but why do you say: if that is impossible in some senses, it is impossible in others? And what impossibility is there that the rulings of the senses differ in the mode of perception, despite their sharing in being corporeal? Just as sight and touch differ: touch does not yield perception except by contact of the touched with the touching organ, and similarly taste; but sight differs from them, for it requires separation, so that if one closes his eyelids, he does not see the color of the eyelids because they are not distant. And this difference does not necessitate difference in the need for a body, so it is not far that among the corporeal senses there be that which is called intellect and which differs from the others in that it perceives itself.
Source pages p. 264

Commentary
This passage is a two-fold refutation by Ghazali of an earlier argument that senses cannot perceive themselves. The first objection uses the example of vision: Ghazali holds that vision can relate to itself, just as knowledge can be knowledge of itself, though customarily it does not, but breaking customs (kharaq al-'adat) is possible. The second objection is stronger: even if we grant that some senses cannot perceive themselves, why conclude that all senses are alike? Ghazali argues that the senses differ in their modes of perception—e.g., touch requires contact, sight requires distance—so they may also differ in the ability to perceive themselves. Therefore, it is plausible that one of the bodily senses, called 'intellect', perceives itself.

Key Terms
إبصار (ibṣār: vision)
خرق العادات (kharaq al-‘ādāt: breaking of customs)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Ghazali is claiming that vision actually perceives itself normally, but he only says it is possible in principle.
Correction: Ghazali explicitly says 'custom runs contrary' to it; he is arguing for logical possibility, not actual occurrence.

Checkpoint
What analogy does Ghazali use to argue that vision could perceive itself?
A) The analogy of touch and sight.
B) The analogy of knowledge knowing itself.
C) The analogy of the intellect and the senses.
D) The analogy of the eye and the mirror.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's first objection shows that self-perception is rationally possible, using the analogy of self-knowledge and the principle that God can break custom.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed a belief about God's power because you have never witnessed it? How does Ghazali's argument about the permissibility of breaking customs challenge you to broaden your view of what God can do?
?
If knowledge can know itself, why do you find it difficult to accept that vision could perceive itself? What does your resistance reveal about your reliance on customary experience rather than rational possibility in matters of faith?
?
When you pray for something extraordinary, do you truly believe God can break the normal patterns of cause and effect, or do you limit Him by your own expectations? How can the principle of kharaq al-‘ādāt reshape your confidence in divine intervention?

Ghazali's Second Objection: Senses Differ in Their Modes
Second objection: senses differ in modes, so one may perceive itself, e.g., intellect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's stronger argument that even if some senses cannot perceive themselves, others may differ, and his conclusion that a corporeal sense called intellect may perceive itself.

Core Idea
Senses differ in their rules; why must they be alike in self-perception?

Ghazali moves to his second, stronger objection. He concedes, for argument's sake, that some senses cannot perceive themselves. But he challenges the assumption that all senses are identical in their capabilities. Drawing on the differences between touch and sight, he argues that it is plausible that one of the corporeal senses—namely, the intellect—can perceive itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the second objection passage
Read the Arabic and translation for the second objection. Note that Ghazali starts by conceding the point for some senses, then asks a rhetorical question.
2
Examine the comparison of touch and sight
Ghazali contrasts touch (requires contact) with sight (requires distance). This shows that senses have different conditions for perception.
This establishes that difference in mode is natural.
3
Understand the conclusion about intellect
Since senses can differ in other respects, it is not far-fetched that one corporeal sense—the intellect—differs from others by perceiving itself.
This is Ghazali's main point: the intellect is a sense, but with a special property.

Ghazali's Refutation of the Sixth Proof: The Fallacy of Induction
Ghazali critiques the philosophers' sixth proof using the fallacy of induction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali uses the logical fallacy of incomplete induction (istiqrā' nāqiṣ) to refute the philosophers' claim that the intellect must be immaterial because it perceives its supposed bodily instrument.

Core Idea
A few examples do not prove a universal rule—the crocodile shows why.

The philosophers argued that if the intellect were a bodily faculty, it could not perceive its own instrument (brain/heart). But since we do perceive these, the intellect must be immaterial. Ghazali pounces: This is a hasty generalization from only five senses. He illustrates with the classic 'crocodile' example from logic, where one might falsely conclude that all animals move their lower jaw when chewing, forgetting the crocodile. This lesson unpacks his devastating critique.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' proof
Start by reading the sixth proof as presented by Ghazali. Notice its structure: a conditional statement with a conclusion that the intellect is not a bodily instrument or locus.
The proof relies on an analogy with the five senses, which cannot perceive their own instruments.
2
Identify Ghazali's two initial objections
Ghazali first offers two short objections: (1) it is not impossible for a sense to perceive its own locus, just unusual; (2) why can't senses differ in this capacity? These weaken the proof's foundation.
3
Analyze the induction fallacy
Ghazali then points out the core fallacy: the philosophers induced from only five senses that all corporeal faculties cannot perceive their locus. This is like the crocodile example—an incomplete induction leading to a false universal.
The crocodile moves its upper jaw, contradicting the generalization from other animals.
4
Consider the implication
Since the intellect might be an exception (like crocodile among animals), the argument yields only conjecture (ẓann), not certainty (yaqīn). This is a decisive refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دلیل سادس
قالوا : لو كان العقل يدرك بآلة جسمانية ، كالإبصار ، لما أدرك آلته كسائر الحواس ، ولكنه يدرك الدماغ والقلب ، وما يدعى أنه آلة له ، فدل أنه ليس آلة له ولا محلا ، وإلا لما أدركه .
*
الاعتراض على هذا الكلام ، كالاعتراض على الذي قبله ، فإنا نقول : لا يبعد أن يدرك الإبصار محله ، ولكنه جرى على العادة
أو نقول : لم يستحيل أن نفترق الحواس فى هذا المعنى ؟ ! وإن اشتركت في الانطباع فى الأجسام كما سبق ، ولم قلتم : إن ما هو قائم في جسم ،
يستحيل أن يدرك الجسم الذي هو محله ، لم يلزم أن يحكم من جزئى معين ،
على كلى مرسل ؟ !

وهؤلاء لم يستقرئوا إلا الحواس الخمس ، فوجدوها على وجه معلوم ، فحكموا على الكل به ، فلعل العقل حاسة أخرى ، تجرى من سائر الحواس ، مجرى التمساح من سائر الحيوانات ، فتكون إذن الحواس ، مع كونها جسمانية ، منقسمة إلى : ما تدرك محلها ، وإلى ما لا تدرك ، كما انقسمت إلى ما يدرك مدركه ، من غير مماسة كالبصر ، وإلى ما لا يدرك إلا بالاتصال كالذوق
واللمس .
•
فما ذكروه أيضاً ، إن أورث ظناً ، فلا يورث يقيناً موثوقاً به .
Sixth proof. They said: If the intellect perceives by a bodily instrument, like vision, it would not perceive its instrument, like the other senses; but it perceives the brain and the heart and what is claimed to be its instrument; so that indicates that it is not an instrument for it nor a locus, otherwise it would not perceive it.
The objection to this statement is like the objection to the previous one, for we say: It is not far that vision should perceive its locus, but it is the custom that it does not. Or we say: Why is it impossible that the senses differ in this meaning? Even though they share in being impressed in bodies, as before. And why do you say: That which subsists in a body is impossible to perceive the body which is its locus? It does not follow that one judges from a particular instance about a universal. And among things known by agreement to be invalid, and mentioned in logic, is to judge from many particulars about a universal, such as when a man says: Every animal moves its lower jaw when chewing, because we have examined all animals and found them thus; that would be due to his neglect of the crocodile, for it moves its upper jaw. These people have examined only the five senses and found them in a known manner, so they judged the whole by that; perhaps the intellect is another sense, running among the other senses like the crocodile among other animals. Thus the senses, despite being corporeal, are divided into what perceives its locus and what does not, just as they are divided into what perceives its object without contact, like sight, and what perceives only by contact, like taste and touch. So what they mentioned also, if it yields conjecture, does not yield reliable certainty.
Source pages p. 264

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' sixth proof and Ghazali's refutation. The proof: if the intellect used a bodily instrument, it would not perceive that instrument, but it does (it perceives the brain and heart), so it is neither an instrument nor a locus. Ghazali objects with two points: first, it is possible that vision could perceive its locus if custom were broken; second, senses can differ—why assume that all senses must be alike? He then points out a logical fallacy: the philosophers have induced from only five senses that all corporeal faculties cannot perceive their own locus. This is like the example of the crocodile: one might wrongly conclude that all animals move the lower jaw when chewing, forgetting the crocodile which moves the upper jaw. Similarly, the intellect might be an exception among senses. Thus, their argument yields only conjecture, not certainty.

Key Terms
آلة (āla: instrument)
محل (maḥall: locus)
استقراء (istiqrā’: induction)
تمساح (timsāḥ: crocodile)
ظن (ẓann: conjecture)
يقين (yaqīn: certainty)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that Ghazali is rejecting the validity of induction entirely.
Correction: He only criticizes incomplete induction used to support a metaphysical claim; he does not reject induction in general.

Checkpoint
What logical fallacy does Ghazali identify in the philosophers' sixth proof?
A) Circular reasoning
B) Incomplete induction (fallacy of the crocodile)
C) Argument from ignorance
D) False dichotomy

Key Takeaway
Ghazali refutes the proof by showing that generalizing from only five senses is like the 'crocodile fallacy'—it yields only conjecture, not certainty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you made a sweeping judgment about a group of people based on only a few encounters, like the philosophers judging from five senses? What would it take for you to admit you might be committing a 'crocodile fallacy'?
?
Ghazali shows that even brilliant thinkers can be fooled by incomplete evidence. What deeply held belief about your faith or life might be based on an incomplete induction? Are you prepared to examine it honestly, even if it means changing your mind?
?
The crocodile is an exception that breaks the rule. Is there a 'crocodile' in your life—a person, experience, or idea that challenges your neat categories? How will you respond to that exception instead of ignoring or dismissing it?

The Philosophers' Burhan Against the Self as a Bodily Organ
Philosophers present a burhan that if the self is bodily, it would always perceive its organ.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' demonstration (burhan) that the self cannot be identical to any bodily organ, and the logical dilemma they use to prove this.

Core Idea
The self always knows itself but not its heart or brain until taught.

In this passage, the philosophers move from relying on mere induction to presenting a formal demonstration. They argue that if the self were a bodily organ like the heart or brain, it would always be aware of that organ, just as it is always aware of itself. But in fact, we are not born knowing our heart or brain; we only learn about them through external means. This contradiction proves that the intellect cannot reside in a body.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the opening phrase where they emphasize using demonstration (burhan) rather than induction.
2
Identify the two parts of the argument
The passage contains two logical moves: first, a conditional argument about the self being the heart or brain; second, a dilemma about intellect residing in a body. Identify each.
The conditional: if self = heart/brain then always aware. The dilemma: if intellect in body, then always perceive or never perceive.
3
Understand the contradiction
Ask yourself: why does the fact that we are not always aware of our heart or brain disprove the claim? Connect to the idea that the self is always self-aware.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider what this argument says about the nature of the self. Does it prove the self is entirely non-bodily, or just not identical to these specific organs? The passage leaves room for further discussion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : لسنا نعول على مجرد الاستقراء للحواس ، بل نعول على البرهان ، ونقول : لو كان القلب أو الدماغ ، هو نفس الإنسان ، لكان لا إدراكهما ، حتى لا يخلو عن أن يعقلهما جميعاً ، كما أنه لا يخلو عن إدراك نفسه ، فإن أحداً لا ذاته يعزب عن ذاته ، بل يكون مثبتاً لنفسه ، في نفسه أبداً ، والإنسان ما لم يسمع حديث القلب والدماغ ، أو لم يشاهما بالتشريح ، من إنسان آخر ، لا يدركهما ولا يعتقد وجودهما ، فإن كان العقل حالا في جسم ، فينبغي أن يعقل ذلك الجسم أبداً ، أو لا يدركه أبداً ، وليس واحد من الأمرين بصحيح ، بل يعقل في حالة ، ولا يعقل في حالة
If it is said: We do not rely on mere induction of the senses, but we rely on demonstration (burhān), and we say: If the heart or the brain is the very self of man, then it would be [necessary] that it be not unaware of them both, so that it never lacks intellection of them both, just as it never lacks awareness of itself; for no one's essence is absent from his essence, but is always self-affirming. And a man, unless he has heard talk of the heart and brain, or seen them by dissection from another man, does not perceive them nor believe in their existence. If the intellect resides in a body, then it must either intellection of that body always, or never perceive it; and neither of the two is correct; rather, it intellets in a state and does not intellet in a state.
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage is the philosophers' further objection, now claiming to rely on demonstration (burhān) rather than mere induction. They argue: if the heart or brain were the self (nafs) of man, then the self would always be aware of them, just as it is always aware of itself (since nothing is absent from itself). But in fact, man is not always aware of his heart or brain; he only knows them through hearing or dissection. Therefore, the intellect cannot be located in a bodily organ. The argument sets up a dilemma: if the intellect resides in a body, it must either always perceive that body or never perceive it. Neither is true; sometimes it perceives (when taught) and sometimes it does not. Thus, the intellect is not in a body.

Key Terms
برهان (burhān: demonstration)
نفس (nafs: self)
عقل (‘aql: intellect)
حلول (ḥulūl: indwelling)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the philosophers are denying any relation between self and body, but they are only denying identity with a bodily organ.
Correction: The argument specifically targets the claim that the self is identical to the heart or brain, not that the self has no connection to the body.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers' demonstration, why cannot the intellect reside in the heart or brain?
A) Because the heart and brain are not organs.
B) Because if it did, it would always perceive them, but it does not.
C) Because the intellect is immaterial and cannot perceive material things.
D) Because the self is identical to the body.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers use a demonstration to show that if the self were a bodily organ, it would be self-aware of that organ at all times, which is false; therefore the self is not identical to any body part.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to know yourself, but have you ever considered that your knowledge of your own essence does not require knowledge of your body's organs? How does this affect your understanding of who you truly are? Pause and ask yourself: If I am not my heart or my brain, what am I? Commit to reflecting on this question until you find an answer that satisfies your intellect and your conscience.
?
The philosophers argue that you are always aware of yourself, even when you sleep. Is that true? Test it: last night, were you aware of being yourself? What does that imply about the nature of your self? Will you live today with the awareness that your self is not reducible to your physical body?
?
If your self does not depend on your body for its identity, then how should you value your body compared to your soul? Are you spending more time caring for your body than for your soul? What would change in your daily life if you truly believed this demonstration?

The Tenth Proof: Intellect Perceives Universals
Ghazali presents the argument that the intellect's grasp of universals proves the soul is immaterial.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali argues that the intellect's ability to perceive universals free from material properties proves the soul is immaterial.

Core Idea
Abstracting universals from particulars proves the soul is immaterial.

In this passage, Ghazali presents the tenth proof for the soul's immateriality, based on the intellect's unique capacity to grasp universal concepts. When we see a specific person, the mind perceives 'absolute man'—a reality stripped of particular place, color, and size. This abstraction, he argues, cannot be performed by a purely material faculty. Understanding this argument is key to appreciating the philosopher's case for an immaterial soul.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation, to get a sense of the argument's flow.
Notice the example of 'absolute man'.
2
Identify the key components of the proof
The proof has three parts: (1) the rational faculty perceives universals, (2) these universals are free from particular properties, (3) this abstraction implies immateriality.
The example of man, tree, and animal illustrates essential vs accidental attributes.
3
Reflect on the implication for the soul
Consider why the ability to abstract universals indicates that the soul itself is not material. How does this connect to the overall thesis?
This is a crucial philosophical move.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهو دلیل عاشر قالوا : القوة العقلية تدرك الكليات العامة العقلية التي يسميها المتكلمون أحوالا ، فتدرك الإنسان المطلق ، عند مشاهدة الحس ، لشخص إنسان معين ، غير الشخص المشاهد ، فإن المشاهد فى مكان مخصوص ، ولون مخصوص ، ومقدار مخصوص ، ووضع مخصوص ، والإنسان المعقول المطلق ، مجرد عن هذه الأمور بل يدخل فيه ، كل ما ينطبق عليه اسم الإنسان ، و إن لم يكن على لون المشاهد وقدره و وضعه ، ومكانه ، بل الذي يمكن وجوده في المستقبل يدخل فيه ، بل أو عدم الإنسان ، تبقى حقيقة الإنسان في العقل مجردة هذه الخواص ، وهكذا كل شيء شاهده الحس مشخصاً ، فيحصل منه للعقل، حقيقة ذلك الشخص كلياً مجرداً عن المواد والأوضاع حتى تنقسم أوصافه إلى ما هو ذاتى ، كالجسمية للشجر والحيوان ، والحيوانية للإنسان ، وإلى ما هو عرضى له ، كالبياض والطول للإنسان والشجر ، ويحكم بكونه ذاتياً وعرضياً على جنس الإنسان والشجر وكل ما يدرك ، لا على الشخص المشاهد
And it is the tenth proof. They said: The rational faculty perceives universal rational concepts, which the theologians call 'states' (ahwal). It perceives absolute man when the senses observe a specific individual man, other than the observed individual. For the observed [individual] is in a specific place, specific color, specific magnitude, and specific position, while the absolute intelligible man is stripped of these matters; rather, it includes everything to which the name 'man' applies, even if it is not of the color, magnitude, position, and place of the observed [individual], but rather what can exist in the future enters into it, or even the non-existence of man; the reality of man remains in the intellect stripped of these properties. Similarly, everything that the senses observe as particular yields to the intellect the universal reality of that thing, stripped of matters and positions, so that its attributes divide into what is essential, such as corporeality for tree and animal, and animality for man, and what is accidental to it, such as whiteness and tallness for man and tree. And it judges that it is essential and accidental with respect to the genus of man and tree and everything perceived, not with respect to the individual observed.
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage presents the tenth proof for the soul's immateriality, based on the intellect's ability to grasp universals. It begins by stating that the rational faculty perceives universal rational concepts, which theologians call 'states' (ahwal). For example, when we see a specific man, the intellect perceives 'absolute man' (al-insan al-mutlaq) — a universal concept stripped of particular place, color, magnitude, and position. This universal includes all possible individuals that fit the definition of 'man', even future ones or the abstract reality of man if all men ceased to exist. The same applies to all sensed objects: the intellect extracts a universal reality free from material properties and particular positions. Consequently, the attributes of these universals are divided into essential (dhati) — like corporeality for tree and animal, and animality for man — and accidental (aradi) — like whiteness or tallness. The intellect judges these as essential or accidental with respect to the whole genus, not to the particular observed individual. This capacity to abstract universals from matter demonstrates that the soul operates independently of the body and is itself immaterial.

Correction: Ghazali argues that the capacity to grasp universals free from material properties indicates that the perceiving faculty itself is not material, because material faculties are limited to particular, physical inputs.

The Intellect's Abstraction of Universals: Proof for the Soul's Immateriality
Ghazali presents the philosophers' tenth argument on the intellect's abstraction of universals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' tenth argument that the soul's ability to perceive universals stripped of sensory qualities implies the soul itself is immaterial (without position or magnitude).

Core Idea
The intellect's universal concepts require a non-physical intellect.

The philosophers present a subtle argument for the immateriality of the rational soul. They observe that the intellect can grasp universal concepts (like 'man') that are free from particular sensory attachments such as color, place, and size. This abstraction, they claim, is only possible if the intellect itself is not tied to matter. In this passage, Ghazali records their reasoning, posing a dilemma that forces the conclusion that the soul has no spatial attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage twice
First read the Arabic (or translation) to get the flow of the argument. Then re-read, noting the key parts: perception of universals, the distinction of essential vs. accidental, and the final dilemma.
The argument is dense. Don't worry about every detail on the first read.
2
Identify the core observation about universals
The philosophers claim that when we see a particular person, our intellect forms the concept of 'man' that applies to all individuals, past, present, and future, regardless of specific accidents. This universal concept has no location or size.
This contrasts with sensory perception which always has particular qualities.
3
Understand the dilemma about abstraction
The philosophers ask: does the stripping of position and matter happen relative to the object (the particular person) or relative to the intellect (the soul)? It cannot be from the object, because the object has position. Therefore it must be from the soul, meaning the soul itself has no position or magnitude.
This is the crucial step linking universals to the soul's nature.
4
Connect to the conclusion
If the soul had material attributes, it could not be the source of abstract universals. Therefore, the rational soul is immaterial. This argument is used to support the soul's survival after death and its independence from the body.
Ghazali will later critique this argument, but first he presents it faithfully.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دلیل عاشر
قالوا : القوة العقلية تدرك الكليات العامة العقلية التي يسميها المتكلمون أحوالا ، فتدرك الإنسان المطلق ، عند مشاهدة الحس ، لشخص إنسان معين ، غير الشخص المشاهد ، فإن المشاهد فى مكان مخصوص ، ولون مخصوص ، ومقدار مخصوص ، ووضع مخصوص ، والإنسان المعقول المطلق ، مجرد عن هذه الأمور بل يدخل فيه ، كل ما ينطبق عليه اسم الإنسان ، و إن لم يكن على لون المشاهد وقدره و وضعه ، ومكانه ، بل الذي يمكن وجوده في المستقبل يدخل فيه ، بل أو عدم الإنسان ، تبقى حقيقة الإنسان في العقل مجردة هذه الخواص ، وهكذا كل شيء شاهده الحس مشخصاً ، فيحصل منه للعقل، حقيقة ذلك الشخص كلياً مجرداً عن المواد والأوضاع حتى تنقسم أوصافه إلى ما هو ذاتى ، كالجسمية للشجر والحيوان ، والحيوانية للإنسان ، وإلى ما هو عرضى له ، كالبياض والطول للإنسان والشجر ، ويحكم بكونه ذاتياً وعرضياً على جنس الإنسان والشجر وكل ما يدرك ، لا على الشخص المشاهد عنه
فدل على أن الكلى المجرد عن القرائن المحسوسة ، معقول عنده ، وثابت في عقله
وذلك الكلى المعقول ، لا إشارة إليه ، ولا وضع له ، ولا مقدار ، فإما أن يكون تجرده عن الوضع والمادة ، بالإضافة إلى المأخوذ منه ، وهو محال ، فإن المأخوذ منه ، ذو وضع ، وأين ، ومقدار ، وإما أن يكون بالإضافة إلى الآخذ ، وهو النفس العاقلة ، فينبغى ألا يكون للنفس وضع ، ولا إليه إشارة ، ولا له مقدار وإلا لو ثبت له ذلك ، لثبت للذي حل فيه
The tenth proof: They said: The rational faculty perceives universal intellectual universals, which the mutakallimun call 'states' (ahwal). So it perceives absolute man (al-insan al-mutlaq), upon sensory observation of a particular individual man, other than the observed individual. For the observed is in a particular place, a particular color, a particular magnitude, and a particular position. And absolute intelligible man is stripped of these matters; rather, it includes everything to which the name 'man' applies, even if not with the color, magnitude, position, and place of the observed. Rather, even what can exist in the future enters into it; and even if man is absent, the reality of man remains in the intellect stripped of these properties. And likewise, everything that the sense observes as particular, there results from it for the intellect the reality of that particular as a universal stripped of matters and positions, so that its attributes divide into what is essential (dhati), like corporeality (jismiyya) for tree and animal, and animality (hayawaniyya) for man, and into what is accidental ('aradi) to it, like whiteness and height for man and tree. And it judges them as essential and accidental with respect to the genus of man and tree and everything perceived, not with respect to the observed individual. Thus it indicates that the universal stripped of sensory qualities is intelligible in the intellect and established in the intellect. And that intelligible universal has no reference (ishara) to it, no position (wad'), and no magnitude (miqdar). So either its stripping of position and matter is in relation to that from which it is taken, which is impossible, for that from which it is taken has position, where, and magnitude; or it is in relation to the taker, which is the rational soul (al-nafs al-aqila), then it must be that the soul has no position, no reference to it, and no magnitude; otherwise if that were established for it, it would be established for that in which it dwells.
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' tenth proof for the immateriality of the rational soul. It is a proof based on the intellect's apprehension of universals. The argument proceeds in several steps: 1) The intellect perceives universals (such as absolute man) from particular sensory data. 2) These universals are stripped of particularizing accidents like place, color, magnitude. 3) The intellect also distinguishes essential attributes (dhati) from accidental ('aradi) across the whole genus. 4) Therefore, the universal is in the intellect without reference, position, or magnitude. 5) Then the philosophers pose a dilemma: either this abstraction is relative to the object from which it is taken (impossible, because the object has position), or relative to the intellect (the taker). 6) If relative to the intellect, then the intellect itself must have no position or magnitude; otherwise the universal could not be abstracted. Hence the intellect is immaterial.

الكليات (al-kulliyyat)
أحوال (ahwal)
النفس العاقلة (al-nafs al-aqila)
ذاتي (dhati)
عرضي ('aradi)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument proves the soul's immateriality simply because we can think about universals.
Correction: The argument rests on the specific claim that the universal is devoid of spatial attributes, leading to a dilemma about the source of this abstraction. It is not merely that we think abstractly, but that the content of thought is non-spatial, requiring a non-spatial intellect.

Checkpoint

C) Because the soul can distinguish essential from accidental attributes.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers argue that the soul's ability to form non-spatial universal concepts proves that the soul itself is not material.

Yet, when faced with a situation that calls for justice, you often confuse it with personal interest. Ghazali's Objection: The Universal is Not an Abstracted Entity in the Intellect
Ghazali objects to the philosophers' claim that a universal is in the intellect as abstracted.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's argument that the universal in the intellect is not an abstracted reality but a particular image that serves for all similar individuals.

Core Idea
The universal is not a separate entity but a particular image that applies equally to all.

Ghazali challenges the philosophers' claim that the intellect contains abstracted universal meanings. Instead, he argues that what enters the intellect comes from the senses, and the intellect merely details composites. The resulting image is particular, yet it functions universally because it applies equally to all individuals of the same kind. This passage presents his alternative account of universality.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation to grasp the flow of Ghazali's objection.
Notice the dense argument style; take your time.
2
Identify the initial objection
Find where Ghazali rejects the philosophers' claim. He says 'the universal meaning... is not conceded.' This sets up his counter-argument.
This is the core of his denial.
3
Follow the argument about intellect and senses
Ghazali claims nothing enters the intellect except through the senses, but senses give composites. The intellect details, but the detailed item is still particular.
Pay attention to the distinction between composite and detailed.
4
Understand the analogy of seeing multiple individuals
Ghazali uses the example of seeing another man: no new image occurs, showing the image serves universally. Contrast this with seeing a horse after a man.
This illustrates the universal function.
5
Reflect on the analogy from sense perception
He gives an example from imagination: the image of water seen once serves for all subsequent water, just like in the intellect.
This reinforces that the universal is not abstracted but a particular image with general application.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاعتراض، أن المعنى الكلى الذى وضعتموه حالا في العقل ، غير مسلم بل لا يحل فى العقل إلا ما يحل فى الحس ، ولكن يحل في الحس مجموعاً ولا يقدر الحس على تفصيله ، والعقل يقدر على تفصيله ، ثم إذا فصل ، كان المفصل المفرد عن القرائن ، في العقل ، في كونه جزئياً كالمقرون بقرائنه ، إلا أن الثابت فى العقل، يناسب المعقول وأمثاله ، مناسبة واحدة ، فيقال : إنه كلى على هذا المعنى ، وهو أن في العقل صورة المعقول المفرد الذى أدركه الحس أولا ، ونسبة تلك الصورة ، إلى سائر آحاد ذلك الجنس ، نسبة واحدة ، فإنه لو رأى إنساناً آخر ، لم تحدث له هيأة أخرى ، كما إذا رأى فرساً بعد إنسان ، فإنه يحدث فيه صورتان مختلفتان ومثل هذا قد يعرض فى مجرد الحس ، فإن من رأى الماء حصل في خياله صورة ، فلو رأى الدم بعده ، حصلت صورة أخرى ، فلو رأى ماء آخر ، لم تحدث صورة أخرى بل الصورة التي انطبعت في خياله من المال [؟]، مثال لكل
The objection: That the universal meaning which you posited as a state (hala) in the intellect is not conceded. Rather, nothing enters the intellect except what enters the senses, but it enters the senses as a composite, and the senses are not able to detail it, while the intellect is able to detail it. Then when it details it, the detailed item stripped of its associations is, in the intellect, in its being particular (juz'i) like that which is accompanied by its associations. However, that which is established in the intellect corresponds to the intelligible and its like in one correspondence, so it is said that it is universal in this sense: that in the intellect there is an image (sura) of the particular intelligible which the sense first perceived, and the relation of that image to all the individuals of that genus is one relation. For if he were to see another man, no other form would occur to him, just as if he sees a horse after a man, then two different forms occur in him. And something similar may occur in mere sense: for one who sees water, an image occurs in his imagination; if he then sees blood, another image occurs; and if he then sees another water, no other image occurs; rather, the image that was impressed in his imagination of the water is an example for every [water].
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage is Ghazali's objection (i'tirad) to the philosophers' tenth argument. He disputes the claim that a universal meaning (ma'na kulli) exists in the intellect as a reality abstracted from all particularizing accidents. Instead, he argues that the intellect only knows what the senses provide, but the senses provide it in a composite manner. The intellect can analyze and detail this composite, but the resulting discrete elements are still particular, just like the original accompanied by its associations. However, the intellect's image of a particular thing (e.g., a man) serves as a universal because it relates equally to all individuals of that species—when seeing another man, no new image arises. This is akin to the imagination: the image of water seen once serves for all subsequent water. Therefore, the universal is not a separate entity but a particular image with universal applicability.

Key Terms
المعنى الكلى
الحس
العقل
الصورة
الجزئي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Ghazali is denying universals entirely.
Correction: He is only denying that universals exist in the intellect as abstracted realities; they are particular images with universal application.

Checkpoint

B) It relates equally to all individuals of that species
C) It is completely different due to accidents
D) It cannot relate to future individuals

Key Takeaway
Ghazali argues that the universal in the intellect is not an abstracted reality but a particular image that stands equally for all individuals of that species.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

This lesson warns against mistaking the universal for an abstraction. Ask yourself: 'Which of my beliefs do I hold as if they were abstracted from all bias? Am I willing to see them as particular images that could be incomplete?'

The Dilemma of Universal Abstraction
Ghazali presents a dilemma about the abstraction of universals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's argument that the abstraction of universals cannot be consistently attributed to either the source or the rational soul.

Core Idea
Universals cannot be abstract in both source and soul without contradiction.

Ghazali opens his refutation by presenting a logical dilemma. If universals are abstract entities, either they are abstract relative to the objects they are taken from or relative to the mind that knows them. Both options lead to absurdities. This lesson examines that dilemma.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let Ghazali's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the two options presented.
2
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
The passage presents two possibilities for how the universal is abstracted. Identify each one and see why both lead to problems.
First horn: relative to the source. Second horn: relative to the soul.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Ask yourself what Ghazali achieves by this dilemma. It sets up the need for a different understanding of universals.
This connects to the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فدل على أن الكلى المجرد عن القرائن المحسوسة ، معقول عنده ، وثابت في عقله. وذلك الكلى المعقول ، لا إشارة إليه ، ولا وضع له ، ولا مقدار ، فإما أن يكون تجرده عن الوضع والمادة ، بالإضافة إلى المأخوذ منه ، وهو محال ، فإن المأخوذ منه ، ذو وضع ، وأين ؛ ومقدار ، وإما أن يكون بالإضافة إلى الآخذ ، وهو النفس العاقلة ، فينبغى ألا يكون للنفس وضع ، ولا إليه إشارة ، ولا له مقدار وإلا لو ثبت له ذلك ، لثبت للذي حل فيه. الاعتراض : أن المعنى الكلى الذي وضعتموه حالا في العقل ، غير مسلم. بل لا يحل فى العقل إلا ما يحل فى الحس ، ولكن يحل فى الحس مجموعاً، ولا يقدر الحس على تفصيله، والعقل يقدر على تفصيله. ثم إذا فصل، كان المفصل المفرد عن القرائن، في العقل، في كونه جزئياً كالمقرون بقرائنه، إلا أن الثابت فى العقل، يناسب المعقول وأمثاله، مناسبة واحدة، فيقال : إنه كلى على هذا المعنى، وهو أن في العقل صورة المعقول المفرد الذي أدركه الحس أولا، ونسبة تلك الصورة، إلى سائر آحاد ذلك الجنس، نسبة واحدة، فإنه لو رأى إنساناً آخر، لم تحدث له هيأة أخرى، كما إذا رأى فرساً بعد إنسان، فإنه يحدث فيه صورتان مختلفتان. ومثل هذا قد يعرض فى مجرد الحس، فإن من رأى الماء حصل في خياله صورة، فلو رأى الدم بعده، حصلت صورة أخرى، فلو رأى ماء آخر، لم تحدث صورة أخرى بل الصورة التي انطبعت في خياله من الماء، مثال لكل واحد من آحاد المياه، فقد يظن أنه كلى بهذا المعنى. فكذلك إذا رأى اليد مثلا حصل في الخيال وفي العقل، وضع أجزائها، بعضها مع بعض، وهو انبساط الكف، وانقسام الأصابع عليه، وانتهاء الأصابع على الأظفار، ويحصل مع ذلك صغره وكبره، ولونه، فإن رأى يداً أخرى تماثلها في كل شيء، لم تتجدد له صورة أخرى، بل لا تؤثر المشاهدة الثانية، في إحداث شيء جديد في الخيال، كما إذا رأى الماء بعد الماء فى إناء واحد، على قدر واحد، وقد يرى يداً أخرى، تخالفها فى اللون والقدر، فيحدث له لون آخر وقدر آخر، ولا تحدث له صورة جديدة لليد، فإن اليد الصغيرة السوداء، تشارك اليد الكبيرة البيضاء فى وضع الأجزاء، وتخالفها في اللون والقدر، فما تساوى فيه الأولى لا تتجدد له صورته، إذ تلك الصورة، هي هذه الصورة بعينها، وما تخالفها تتجدد صورته. فهذا معنى الكلى فى العقل والحس جميعاً، فإن العقل إذا أدرك صورة الجسم من الحيوان، فلا يستفيد من الشجر صورة جديدة في الجسمية، كما في المثال في إدراك صورة المائين، في وقتين، وكذا فى كل متشابهين، وهذا لا يؤذن بثبوت كلى لا وضع له أصلا. على أن العقل قد يحكم بثبوت شيء لا إشارة إليه، ولا وضع له، كحكمه بوجود صانع العالم، ولكن من أين أن ذلك لا يتصور قيامه بجسم؟! وفى هذا القسم يكون المنتزع عن المادة، هو المعقول في نفسه، دون العقل والعاقل، فأما فى المأخوذ من المواد، فوجهه ما ذكرناه.
It is shown that the universal abstracted from sensory qualities is intelligible to him and established in his intellect. That intelligible universal has no reference to it, no position, and no magnitude. So either its abstraction from position and matter is in relation to that from which it is taken—which is impossible, for that from which it is taken has position, place, and magnitude—or it is in relation to the taker, which is the rational soul. Then the soul should have no position, no reference to it, and no magnitude; otherwise, if that were established for it, it would be established for that in which it inheres. The objection: The universal meaning that you have posited as inhering in the intellect is not conceded. Rather, nothing inheres in the intellect except what inheres in sense, but it inheres in sense as a whole, and sense cannot detail it, while the intellect can detail it. Then when it is detailed, the detailed item stripped of its associations, in the intellect, is particular like that which is accompanied by its associations, except that what is established in the intellect corresponds to the intelligible and its likes in a single correspondence. So it is said that it is universal in this sense: that in the intellect there is an image of the particular intelligible that the sense first perceived, and the relation of that image to all the individuals of that genus is a single relation. For if he saw another human, no other form would occur in him, as when he sees a horse after a human, then two different images occur in him. Something similar may occur in mere sense: whoever sees water, an image occurs in his imagination; if he then sees blood, another image occurs; if he then sees other water, no other image occurs, but the image impressed in his imagination from the water is an example for every individual of waters. So one might think it is universal in this sense. Likewise, if he sees a hand, for example, there occurs in the imagination and in the intellect the arrangement of its parts, one with another—that is, the extension of the palm and the division of the fingers upon it and the termination of the fingers at the nails—and along with that its smallness, largeness, and color occur. If he sees another hand that resembles it in everything, no other image occurs anew; rather, the second sight does not effect the generation of anything new in the imagination, just as when he sees water after water in one vessel of equal measure. He may see another hand that differs from it in color and measure, so another color and measure occur, but no new image of the hand occurs. For the small black hand shares with the large white hand in the arrangement of parts, but differs in color and measure. So what is equal in the first does not have its image occur anew, for that image is this same image, and what differs has its image occur anew. Thus this is the meaning of universal in both intellect and sense. For if the intellect perceives the image of body from animal, it does not gain a new image in bodiliness from tree, as in the example of perceiving the image of two waters at two times, and likewise for all similars. This does not indicate the establishment of a universal with no position at all. Moreover, the intellect may judge the existence of something with no reference to it and no position, like its judgment of the existence of the Maker of the world. But how does it follow that this cannot be imagined to inhere in a body? In this category, that which is abstracted from matter is the intelligible itself, without the intellect and the intelligent. As for that which is taken from matters, its aspect is what we have mentioned.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the philosophers' claim that universals exist in the mind as abstract entities without any reference to position or matter. Ghazali begins by stating that the universal abstracted from sensory qualities is indeed intelligible, but he then raises a dilemma: either its abstraction is relative to the thing from which it is taken (which is impossible because that thing has position and matter) or relative to the rational soul, which would then require the soul to be free of position—but if the soul had position, the universal would have position. He then presents an objection: the universal meaning they posit is not conceded; only what is in sense can be in intellect, but sense perceives things as wholes, and intellect details them. The detailed item in the intellect is particular, but because the intellect's image corresponds equally to all individuals of a genus, it is called universal. Ghazali explains this with examples: seeing a human and then another human does not generate a new image, whereas seeing a horse after a human does. Similarly, seeing water after water yields the same image, but seeing blood yields a different one. He extends this to the example of a hand: the image of the hand includes the arrangement of parts, but specific qualities like color and size differ; the common arrangement is the same image, while differing qualities generate new images. Thus, universality in the intellect is nothing but the correspondence of a single image to multiple particulars. This, Ghazali argues, does not prove the existence of a universal with absolutely no position. He then acknowledges that the intellect can judge non-spatial things like the existence of the Creator, but questions whether such a thing cannot inhere in a body. Finally, he distinguishes between the intelligible abstracted from matter (which is the intelligible itself) and the intelligible taken from matters (which is as described).

مجرد
عقل
وضع
إشارة
مقدار
مادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that universals are completely abstract entities with no relation to matter or mind.
Correction: Ghazali shows that this notion leads to a dilemma: either they are abstract relative to matter (which is impossible) or relative to the mind (which then must be non-spatial).

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali's dilemma, what is the problem with saying the universal is abstracted from matter relative to the source?
A) The source has position, so the universal would also have position.
B) The source is non-spatial.
C) The universal cannot be abstract.
D) The soul cannot think of universals.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's dilemma shows that the philosophers' notion of an abstract universal cannot be consistently grounded in either the source or the knower.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of concepts like 'justice' or 'truth', do you assume they exist in your mind as abstract universals? How does Ghazali's dilemma challenge your confidence in the reality of your own abstract thoughts? This week, try to identify one universal you hold as certain and ask yourself: is it really abstract, or is it tied to something concrete? Sit with the discomfort of not knowing.
?
You often rely on your intellect to grasp abstract ideas. According to Ghazali, your intellect itself might be spatial. Does this make you question the reliability of your reasoning? What would it mean if your deepest convictions are shaped by a soul that might have position and magnitude? Spend five minutes contemplating whether your soul feels more like a thing or a process, and why that matters for your faith.
?
The philosophers claimed universals are real and abstract. Ghazali shows this leads to a contradiction. Have you ever held a belief that seemed logical but later admitted was inconsistent? What was it? How did you resolve it? This lesson invites you to examine your own intellectual framework; are you willing to let go of a comfortable abstraction if it doesn't hold up? Commit to testing one abstract belief this week against the reality of your experience.

The Objection: Universals as Mental Images
Ghazali explains his alternative account of universals as mental images.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's explanation that universals are mental images that correspond equally to multiple particulars.

Core Idea
Universality is just a single mental image applying equally to many individuals.

Ghazali now presents an objection to the philosophers' claim and offers his own account. He argues that nothing enters the intellect except what enters the senses, but the intellect can detail what the sense perceives as a whole. The detailed mental image, stripped of accidental qualities, is called universal because it applies uniformly to all individuals of that genus. This lesson walks through his examples.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and initial response
Start by reading the passage where Ghazali rejects the philosophers' universal. Notice his key claim: only what is in sense enters the intellect.
The intellect details what sense perceives as a whole.
2
Follow the examples
Ghazali gives several examples (human/horse, water/blood, hand). For each, note how the mental image behaves: some generate new images, others do not.
The hand example is the most detailed.
3
Understand the conclusion
After the examples, Ghazali concludes that universality is the correspondence of a single image to many individuals. This is the meaning of universal in both intellect and sense.
He denies any universal with no position at all.

فدل على أن الكلى المجرد عن القرائن المحسوسة ، معقول عنده ، وثابت في عقله. وذلك الكلى المعقول ، لا إشارة إليه ، ولا وضع له ، ولا مقدار ، فإما أن يكون تجرده عن الوضع والمادة ، بالإضافة إلى المأخوذ منه ، فيه. الاعتراض : أن المعنى الكلى الذي وضعتموه حالا في العقل ، غير مسلم. بل لا يحل فى العقل إلا ما يحل فى الحس ، ولكن يحل فى الحس مجموعاً، ولا يقدر الحس على تفصيله، والعقل يقدر على تفصيله. ثم إذا فصل، It is shown that the universal abstracted from sensory qualities is intelligible to him and established in his intellect. That intelligible universal has no reference to it, no position, and no magnitude. So either its abstraction from position and matter is in relation to that from which it is taken—which is impossible, for that from which it is taken has position, place, and magnitude—or it is in relation to the taker, which is the rational soul. Rather, nothing inheres in the intellect except what inheres in sense, but it inheres in sense as a whole, and sense cannot detail it, while the intellect can detail it. Then when it is detailed, the detailed item stripped of its associations, in the intellect, is particular like that which is accompanied by its associations, except that what is established in the intellect corresponds to the intelligible and its likes in a single correspondence. Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the philosophers' claim that universals exist in the mind as abstract entities without any reference to position or matter. Ghazali begins by stating that the universal abstracted from sensory qualities is indeed intelligible, but he then raises a dilemma: either its abstraction is relative to the thing from which it is taken (which is impossible because that thing has position and matter) or relative to the rational soul, which would then require the soul to be free of position—but if the soul had position, the universal would have position. He then presents an objection: the universal meaning they posit is not conceded; only what is in sense can be in intellect, but sense perceives things as wholes, and intellect details them. The detailed item in the intellect is particular, but because the intellect's image corresponds equally to all individuals of a genus, it is called universal. Ghazali explains this with examples: seeing a human and then another human does not generate a new image, whereas seeing a horse after a human does. Similarly, seeing water after water yields the same image, but seeing blood yields a different one. He extends this to the example of a hand: the image of the hand includes the arrangement of parts, but specific qualities like color and size differ; the common arrangement is the same image, while differing qualities generate new images. Thus, universality in the intellect is nothing but the correspondence of a single image to multiple particulars. This, Ghazali argues, does not prove the existence of a universal with absolutely no position. He then acknowledges that the intellect can judge non-spatial things like the existence of the Creator, but questions whether such a thing cannot inhere in a body. Finally, he distinguishes between the intelligible abstracted from matter (which is the intelligible itself) and the intelligible taken from matters (which is as described).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that universals exist as separate abstract entities in the mind.
Correction: Ghazali argues that universals are just mental images of particulars that apply uniformly to all individuals of a genus.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali's hand example, what is shared between a small black hand and a large white hand?
A) Their color and size
B) Their arrangement of parts (palm, fingers, etc.)
C) Both color and arrangement
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's analysis grounds universality in the mind's ability to use a single image for multiple individuals, not in a separate abstract entity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of concepts like 'justice' or 'mercy', do you tend to imagine them as existing somewhere 'out there'? How does Ghazali's account of universals as mere mental images challenge you to take personal responsibility for embodying these concepts in your own life, rather than expecting them to be abstract realities?
?
The passage shows that when you see two similar hands, your mind does not create a new image. In your daily interactions, how often do you reduce people to your pre-existing mental 'images' instead of perceiving their unique particularity? What would it take to truly see each individual as a new creation, not just another example of your mental template?
?
Ghazali argues that the intellect does not gain a new image from similar individuals. Does this insight confront you with the danger of complacency in your understanding of familiar things, including religious concepts? Commit to one area where you will intentionally seek to renew your understanding, not relying on old mental images.

Intellect's Judgment of Non-Spatial Entities
Ghazali distinguishes non-spatial concepts from material-derived universals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's final distinction between intellect's ability to conceive non-spatial beings and the nature of universals derived from matter.

Core Idea
The intellect can conceive non-spatial realities, but that does not support abstract universals.

Ghazali concludes by addressing a potential objection: the intellect can judge existence of something without position, like the Creator. However, he distinguishes between abstractions that are the intelligible itself (like God) and universals taken from material things. This lesson clarifies his final point.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the short final passage. Notice Ghazali acknowledges the intellect can judge existence of non-spatial things like the Creator.
He then asks a critical question: does that imply such a thing cannot inhere in a body?
2
Identify the two categories
Ghazali distinguishes two categories: what is abstracted from matter and is the intelligible itself (like God), and what is taken from matters (the universals discussed earlier).
These are different.
3
Understand the conclusion
The existence of non-spatial intelligibles does not prove the philosophers' abstract universals derived from material things.
This wraps up his refutation.

ولونه، فإن رأى يداً أخرى تماثلها في كل شيء، لم تتجدد له صورة أخرى، بل لا تؤثر المشاهدة الثانية، في إحداث شيء جديد في الخيال، كما إذا رأى الماء بعد الماء فى إناء واحد، على قدر واحد، وقد يرى يداً أخرى، تخالفها فى اللون والقدر، فيحدث له لون آخر وقدر آخر، ولا تحدث له صورة جديدة لليد، فإن اليد الصغيرة السوداء، تشارك اليد الكبيرة البيضاء فى وضع الأجزاء، وتخالفها في اللون والقدر، فما تساوى فيه الأولى لا تتجدد له صورته، إذ تلك الصورة، هي هذه الصورة بعينها، وما تخالفها تتجدد صورته. فهذا معنى الكلى فى العقل والحس جميعاً، فإن العقل إذا أدرك صورة الجسم من الحيوان، فلا يستفيد من الشجر صورة جديدة في الجسمية، كما في المثال في إدراك صورة المائين، في وقتين، وكذا فى كل متشابهين، وهذا لا يؤذن بثبوت كلى لا وضع له أصلا. على أن العقل قد يحكم بثبوت شيء لا إشارة إليه، ولا وضع له، كحكمه بوجود صانع العالم، ولكن من أين أن ذلك لا يتصور قيامه بجسم؟! وفى هذا القسم يكون المنتزع عن المادة، هو المعقول في نفسه، دون العقل والعاقل، فأما فى المأخوذ من المواد، فوجهه ما ذكرناه.
It is shown that the universal abstracted from sensory qualities is intelligible to him and established in his intellect. That intelligible universal has no reference to it, no position, and no magnitude. So either its abstraction from position and matter is in relation to that from which it is taken—which is impossible, for that from which it is taken has position, place, and magnitude—or it is in relation to the taker, which is the rational soul. Then the soul should have no position, no reference to it, and no magnitude; otherwise, if that were established for it, it would be established for that in which it inheres. The objection: The universal meaning that you have posited as inhering in the intellect is not conceded. Rather, nothing inheres in the intellect except what inheres in sense, but it inheres in sense as a whole, and sense cannot detail it, while the intellect can detail it. Then when it is detailed, the detailed item stripped of its associations, in the intellect, is particular like that which is accompanied by its associations, except that what is established in the intellect corresponds to the intelligible and its likes in a single correspondence. So it is said that it is universal in this sense: that in the intellect there is an image of the particular intelligible that the sense first perceived, and the relation of that image to all the individuals of that genus is a single relation. For if he saw another human, no other form would occur in him, as when he sees a horse after a human, then two different images occur in him. Something similar may occur in mere sense: whoever sees water, an image occurs in his imagination; if he then sees blood, another image occurs; if he then sees other water, no other image occurs, but the image impressed in his imagination from the water is an example for every individual of waters. So one might think it is universal in this sense. Likewise, if he sees a hand, for example, there occurs in the imagination and in the intellect the arrangement of its parts, one with another—that is, the extension of the palm and the division of the fingers upon it and the termination of the fingers at the nails—and along with that its smallness, largeness, and color occur. If he sees another hand that resembles it in everything, no other image occurs anew; rather, the second sight does not effect the generation of anything new in the imagination, just as when he sees water after water in one vessel of equal measure. He may see another hand that differs from it in color and measure, so another color and measure occur, but no new image of the hand occurs. For the small black hand shares with the large white hand in the arrangement of parts, but differs in color and measure. So what is equal in the first does not have its image occur anew, for that image is this same image, and what differs has its image occur anew. Thus this is the meaning of universal in both intellect and sense. For if the intellect perceives the image of body from animal, it does not gain a new image in bodiliness from tree, as in the example of perceiving the image of two waters at two times, and likewise for all similars. This does not indicate the establishment of a universal with no position at all. Moreover, the intellect may judge the existence of something with no reference to it and no position, like its judgment of the existence of the Maker of the world. But how does it follow that this cannot be imagined to inhere in a body? In this category, that which is abstracted from matter is the intelligible itself, without the intellect and the intelligent. As for that which is taken from matters, its aspect is what we have mentioned.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the philosophers' claim that universals exist in the mind as abstract entities without any reference to position or matter. Ghazali begins by stating that the universal abstracted from sensory qualities is indeed intelligible, but he then raises a dilemma: either its abstraction is relative to the thing from which it is taken (which is impossible because that thing has position and matter) or relative to the rational soul, which would then require the soul to be free of position—but if the soul had position, the universal would have position. He then presents an objection: the universal meaning they posit is not conceded; only what is in sense can be in intellect, but sense perceives things as wholes, and intellect details them. The detailed item in the intellect is particular, but because the intellect's image corresponds equally to all individuals of a genus, it is called universal. Ghazali explains this with examples: seeing a human and then another human does not generate a new image, whereas seeing a horse after a human does. Similarly, seeing water after water yields the same image, but seeing blood yields a different one. He extends this to the example of a hand: the image of the hand includes the arrangement of parts, but specific qualities like color and size differ; the common arrangement is the same image, while differing qualities generate new images. Thus, universality in the intellect is nothing but the correspondence of a single image to multiple particulars. This, Ghazali argues, does not prove the existence of a universal with absolutely no position. He then acknowledges that the intellect can judge non-spatial things like the existence of the Creator, but questions whether such a thing cannot inhere in a body. Finally, he distinguishes between the intelligible abstracted from matter (which is the intelligible itself) and the intelligible taken from matters (which is as described).

صانع
مادة
مجرد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that because the intellect can conceive non-spatial entities, all universals are non-spatial.
Correction: Ghazali distinguishes: the concept of God is non-spatial in itself, but universals taken from matter are mental images that derive from spatial particulars.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali's final passage, what does the intellect judge existence of with no reference or position?
A) Universals
B) The Maker of the world
C) All images
D) Nothing

Key Takeaway
Ghazali distinguishes the inherently non-spatial concept of the Creator from universals derived from material things, affirming the former while rejecting the latter as independent abstract entities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of God as 'not in a place,' do you actually conceive Him as a real, living Being or just as an abstract concept? How does Ghazali's distinction challenge you to worship God as He truly is, not as a mental category? This week, before each prayer, pause to reflect that you are addressing the Creator whose existence is more certain than any universal idea.
?
You likely affirm that God's attributes like 'All-Knowing' are real. But have you ever, even subtly, treated them as separate abstract entities floating in your mind? Examine your belief: do you truly believe that God's knowledge is His very essence, or do you mentally push it into a Platonic form? Make a firm intention today to speak of Allah's attributes only as the Qur'an and Sunnah describe, without philosophical additions.
?
Ghazali shows that the intellect can know God without spatial reference, yet that does not reduce God to a universal. How often do you rely on your own reasoning to 'prove' God, forgetting that He is beyond human analogy? What is one specific way you will humble your intellect before divine revelation today?

The Universal in Intellect and Sense
Ghazali explains the meaning of the universal (kulli) in both intellect and sense.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali explains the universal (kulli) as the common form abstracted from particulars, and why he rejects the notion of a separate Platonic Form.

Core Idea
The universal is not a separate entity but the common form abstracted from similar particulars.

Ghazali challenges the Platonic view of universals as independently existing entities. Through concrete examples of seeing water and hands, he shows that the universal is simply the common aspect perceived across multiple instances, both in sense and intellect. This passage is a key part of his critique of the philosophers' metaphysics.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation silently. Notice the flow of examples and argument.
Pay attention to the hand example – it is the core illustration.
2
Analyze the water example
Why does Ghazali mention seeing one water? How does it relate to the idea of a universal? Consider how the first observation might create a general notion.
This sets the stage for the hand example.
3
Examine the hand example in detail
Follow Ghazali's analysis of seeing two identical hands vs. two different hands. Identify what changes (color, size) and what stays the same (arrangement of parts). How does this show abstraction of the common form?
The form of 'hand' is the universal – it does not recur because it is the same form.
4
Extend to intellect
How does Ghazali apply the same logic to intellectual concepts, like 'body' taken from animal and tree? What does this reveal about the nature of universals in mind?
The intellect does not gain a new form of bodiliness from a tree – it already has the universal.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Ghazali questions whether this process implies a separate universal with no position. What is his stance? Why does he bring up the example of the Creator?
He denies that abstraction proves a separate universal, but notes that the intellect can conceive of non-spatial entities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحد من آحاد المياه ، فقد يظن أنه كلى بهذا المعنى ، فكذلك إذا رأى اليد مثلا حصل في الخيال وفى العقل ، وضع أجزائها ، بعضها مع بعض ، وهو انبساط الكف ، وانقسام الأصابع عليه ، وانتهاء الأصابع على الأظفار ، ويحصل مع ذلك صغره وكبره ، ولونه ، فإن رأى يداً أخرى تماثلها في كل شيء ، لم تتجدد له صورة أخرى ، بل لا تؤثر المشاهدة الثانية ، فى إحداث شيء جديد في الخيال ، كما إذا رأى الماء بعد الماء فى إناء واحد ، على قدر واحد ، وقد يرى يداً أخرى ، تخالفها فى اللون والقدر ، فيحدث له لون آخر وقدر آخر ، ولا تحدث له صورة جديدة لليد ، فإن اليد الصغيرة السوداء ، تشارك اليد الكبيرة البيضاء فى وضع الأجزاء ، وتخالفها في اللون والقدر ، فما تساوى فيه الأولى لا تتجدد له صورته ، إذ تلك الصورة ، هى هذه الصورة بعينها ، وما تخالفها تتجدد صورته . فهذا معنى الكلى فى العقل والحس جميعاً ، فإن العقل إذا أدرك صورة الجسم من الحيوان ، فلا يستفيد من الشجر صورة جديدة في الجسمية ، كما في المثال فى إدراك صورة المائين ، في وقتين ، وكذا فى كل متشابهين ، وهذا لا يؤذن بثبوت كلى لا وضع له أصلا على أن العقل قد يحكم بثبوت شيء لا إشارة إليه ، ولا وضع له ، كحكمه بوجود صانع العالم ، ولكن من أين أن ذلك لا يتصور قيامه بجسم ؟ ! وفى هذا القسم يكون المنتزع عن المادة ، هو المعقول في نفسه ، دون العقل والعاقل ، فأما فى المأخوذ من المواد ، فوجهه ما ذكرناه .
One of the individual waters, so one might think that it is a universal in this sense. Likewise, if one sees a hand, for example, there occurs in the imagination and in the intellect the arrangement of its parts, some with others, which is the extension of the palm, the division of the fingers upon it, and the ending of the fingers at the nails, and along with that there occurs its smallness and largeness and color. Then if he sees another hand that resembles it in everything, no other form occurs to him, but rather the second observation does not effect the generation of anything new in the imagination, just as when one sees water after water in one vessel of the same measure. And he may see another hand that differs from it in color and measure, so another color and another measure occur to him, but no new form of the hand occurs to him. For the small black hand shares with the large white hand the arrangement of parts, and differs from it in color and measure. So what is equal in it between the first and second – its form does not recur, because that form is precisely this form; and what differs, its form recurs. So this is the meaning of the universal in both intellect and sense. For if the intellect apprehends the form of body from an animal, it does not gain from a tree a new form in bodiliness, as in the example of apprehending the form of two waters at two times, and likewise in all similar things. And this does not indicate the subsistence of a universal that has no position at all, even though the intellect may judge the subsistence of something that has no indication and no position, like its judgment of the existence of the Maker of the world. But how does it follow from that that such a thing cannot subsist in a body? And in this category, that which is abstracted from matter is the intelligible (al-ma'qūl) in itself, without the intellect and the intelligent being. As for that which is taken from materials, its aspect is what we have mentioned.
Source pages p. 273

Commentary
This passage is a continuation of a discussion about the nature of universals. Ghazali explains that the universal (kullī) is not a separate Platonic entity but arises from abstraction. He uses the example of water: seeing one sample of water might make one think it is a universal, but that is not the case. Then he analyzes the perception of a hand: when one sees a hand, the imagination forms an image that includes the arrangement of parts (the shape), size, and color. If a second identical hand is seen, no new image arises because the form is the same. If a different hand (e.g., small black vs. large white) is seen, only the differing attributes (color, size) create a new image, while the common hand-shape does not produce a new form because it is already present. This shows that the universal is the common aspect abstracted from individuals. Ghazali then extends this to the intellect: when the intellect apprehends the form of body from an animal, it does not gain a new form of bodiliness from a tree, because the universal 'body' is already conceived. This does not prove the existence of a universal that has no position (i.e., a separate Form), even though the intellect can conceive of things with no spatial location, like the Creator. Ghazali questions whether such a thing can inhere in a body. He concludes that in the case of abstraction from matter, the intelligible is considered in itself without relation to the intellect or the intelligent being; whereas in the case of taking from materials, the matter is as mentioned.

خيال
عقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the universal is a separate entity existing independently of particulars.
Correction: Ghazali shows through examples that the universal is the common form abstracted from multiple instances, not an independently existing substance.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali's example of seeing two identical hands, why does the second hand not produce a new image in the imagination?
A) Because the first image is forgotten.
B) Because the form of the hand is exactly the same form.
C) Because the imagination cannot store two identical images.
D) Because the second hand is imaginary.

Key Takeaway
The universal is not a separate entity but the common form abstracted from similar particulars in both sensory and intellectual perception.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the diversity of creation—different people, things, events—do you tend to focus on their differences or the common patterns? How does that habit shape your understanding of God's oneness and the unity of His creation? What would it take for you to see more of the universal commonalities rather than the particulars?
?
Ghazali argues that the universal 'hand' exists only in the mind, not outside. Consider a concept you hold very strongly—like 'justice' or 'truth'. Do you treat it as a mere mental abstraction, or as a reality that ought to be manifested in your life? How does your answer affect your commitment to living that concept?
?
The intellect can conceive of realities without position, like the Creator. But is God for you a mere concept in your mind, or the Living Reality that demands your entire being? If He is the latter, why do you not live every moment in His presence? What would change if you treated His existence as more real than the objects you see?
