First Proof of the Soul's Immortality
Ghazali presents and refutes the first proof of the soul's immortality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's first philosophical proof that the soul cannot be annihilated by the death of the body, based on the distinction between the body as a locus and an instrument, and the soul's independent actions.

Core Idea
The soul does not depend on the body because it acts and exists without it.

In this passage, Ghazali begins his refutation of the philosophers' claim that human souls are eternal and cannot be destroyed. He presents their first proof and then dismantles it by arguing that the body is merely an instrument for the soul, not its locus, and that the soul has actions that are independent of the body. This establishes a key foundation for understanding the soul's immortality in Islamic philosophy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the philosophers' claim
Read the first part of the passage to see what the philosophers assert about the soul and what they demand as proof.
The claim is that the soul cannot be annihilated after existence and is eternal.
2
Understand the three possible causes
The philosophers enumerate three ways annihilation could theoretically occur: death of the body, a contrary, or God's power. Notice that they will argue each is impossible.
This is a typical kalam strategy of exhaustive division.
3
Analyze the first argument: body as instrument
Ghazali argues that the body is not a locus for the soul but an instrument. The corruption of an instrument does not corrupt its user unless the user is imprinted in it.
Contrast with animal souls that are imprinted.
4
Analyze the second argument: independent actions
The soul has actions like apprehending intelligibles that do not require the body; in fact, the body hinders them. This shows the soul's existence does not depend on the body.
If an entity has an action without the body, it can exist without it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة [ ١٩ ] في إبطال قولهم : إن النفوس الإنسانية ، يستحيل عليها العدم بعد وجودها وإنها سرمدية لا يتصور فناؤها فيطالبون بالدليل عليه ، ولهم دليلان : أحدهما : قولهم إن عدمها لا يخلو : إما أن يكون بموت البدن . أو بضد يطرأ عليها أو بقدرة القادر . وباطل أن تنعدم بموت البدن ، فإن البدن ليس محلا لها ، بل هو آلة تستعملها النفس بوساطة القوى التى فى البدن ، وفساد الآلة لا يوجب فساد مستعمل الآلة ، إلا أن يكون حالا فيها منطبعاً ، كالنفوس البهيمية والقوى الجسمانية . فلا ولأن للنفس فعلا بغير مشاركة البدن ، وفعلا بمشاركته فالفعل الذى لها بمشاركة البدن ، التخيل والإحساس والشهوة والغضب ، جرم يفسد بفساد البدن ، ويقوى بقوته . وفعلها بذاتها دون مشاركة البدن ، إدراك المعقولات المجردة عن المواد ، ولا حاجة فى كونها مدركة للمعقولات إلى البدن ، بل الاشتغال بالبدن يعوقها عن المعقولات ، ومهما كان لها فعل دون البدن ، ووجود دون البدن ، لم تفتقر في قوامها إلى البدن
Problem [19]: On refuting their statement that human souls are impossible to be annihilated after their existence and that they are eternal, their annihilation is inconceivable. So they demand a proof for it, and they have two proofs: The first of them is their statement that their annihilation is not free from either being by the death of the body, or by a contrary that befalls them, or by the power of the Powerful (al-Qādir). And it is false that they are annihilated by the death of the body, because the body is not a locus (maḥall) for them, but rather it is an instrument (ālah) that the soul uses by means of the powers in the body. The corruption of the instrument does not necessitate the corruption of the user of the instrument, unless it is a state (ḥāl) subsisting in it by imprinting, like animal souls and physical powers. So no. And because the soul has an action without the participation of the body, and an action with its participation. As for the action that belongs to it with the participation of the body: imagination, sensation, appetite, and anger; these are (like) a body that is corrupted by the corruption of the body and strengthens by its strength. And its action by itself without participation of the body is the apprehension of intelligibles abstracted from matters. There is no need, in its being an apprehender of intelligibles, for the body. Rather, occupation with the body hinders it from the intelligibles. And whenever it has an action without the body and an existence without the body, it does not depend on the body for its subsistence.
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage marks the beginning of Mas'ala 19, where Ghazali sets out to refute the philosophers' doctrine that human souls, once they exist, cannot be annihilated and are eternal. He presents their claim and then says they demand a proof, offering two proofs. Here he presents the first proof. The philosophers argue that the soul's annihilation could only occur by one of three causes: death of the body, a contrary that attacks it, or an act of God's power. They then argue that death of the body cannot annihilate the soul because the body is not a locus (maḥall) for the soul; rather, it is an instrument (ālah) used by the soul via the bodily powers. The corruption of an instrument does not entail the corruption of its user, unless the user is a mode (ḥāl) imprinted in the instrument, like animal souls or physical powers. Then Ghazali adds a second argument: the soul has actions without the body's participation—such as apprehending abstract intelligibles—and actions with participation (imagination, sensation, etc.). The latter are like a body that perishes with the body. But the soul's own action (apprehension of intelligibles) does not require the body; in fact, the body hinders it. Since the soul has both an action and an existence independent of the body, it does not depend on the body for its subsistence. Therefore, the body's death does not annihilate the soul.

Key Terms
نفس
عدم
بدن
محل
آلة
معقولات
قادر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The soul is a material substance that perishes with the death of the body.
Correction: Ghazali argues that the soul is not a mode imprinted in the body; rather, it uses the body as an instrument and has actions independent of it, so it does not die with the body.

Checkpoint
How does Ghazali refute the idea that the soul is annihilated by the death of the body?
A) By arguing that the body is the soul's locus.
B) By showing that the soul has actions independent of the body.
C) By claiming the soul is material.
D) By saying the soul is a mode imprinted in the body.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali establishes that the soul's independence from the body is proven by its ability to act and exist without the body, thus refuting the idea that death of the body annihilates the soul.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your soul can truly exist and act without your body, then what does that imply about the way you currently spend your time? Are you feeding your soul's independent capacities or only its bodily functions?
?
Ghazali says that occupation with the body hinders the soul from apprehending intelligibles. What specific bodily preoccupations in your own life are keeping you from deeper understanding? Will you reduce at least one of them this week?
?
When you face the inevitable death of your body, what will remain of you? Based on this lesson, what should you be investing in now that will not perish with your body?

The Unknown Relation and the Soul's Survival
Ghazali critiques the philosophers' proof of soul's immortality using the unknown relation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali uses the concept of an unknown suitability (munasaba) between soul and body to undermine the philosophers' proof of the soul's immortality.

Core Idea
The unknown relation may bind the soul to the body, making its survival uncertain.

Ghazali challenges the philosophers' claim that the rational soul survives the body's death. He argues that the specification of each soul to a particular body relies on an unknown suitability. This unknown factor could mean the soul depends on the body for its existence, weakening the argument for immortality.

Guided Path
1
Read about the soul's longing after death
Begin with the passage describing how the soul's attachment to the body causes pain when the body is lost. This sets the stage for questioning the soul's independence.
Notice the emotional tone: the soul 'is pained by that longing'.
2
Understand the problem of specification
Identify why Zayd's soul is attached to Zayd's body. Ghazali says there is a cause and suitability (munasaba) that determines this attachment, but we cannot perceive it.
This moves from emotion to rational analysis.
3
Grasp the role of the unknown suitability
The unknown suitability might be such that the soul needs the body to remain in existence. If so, the soul would perish with the body, contradicting the philosophers' claim.
This is the core of Ghazali's critique.
4
See how the proof becomes unreliable
Because we do not know the nature of the relation, we cannot be sure the soul survives. Therefore, the philosophers' proof is not trustworthy.
Ghazali concludes: 'there is no confidence in the proof that he mentioned.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذلك لا فسادها بفساد البدن ، يوجب نعم قد يبقى ذلك الشوق التي هي مشتاقة بالحيلة إلى تدبيره ، بعد مفارقة البدن ، إن استحكم في الحياة اشتغالها بالبدن وإعراضها عن كسر الشهوات ، وطلب المعقولات ، فتتأذى بذلك الشوق ، مع فوات الآلة التي يصل بها الشوق إلى مقتضاه (۱) وأما تعين نفس زيد لشخص زيد ، فى أول الحدوث ، فلسبب ومناسبة ، بين البدن والنفس لا محالة ، حتى يكون هذا البدن مثلا ، أصلح لهذه النفس من الأخرى لمزيد مناسبة بينهما ، فيترجح اختصاصه، وليس في القوة البشرية إدراك تلك المناسبات ، وعدم اطلاعنا على تفصيلها ، لا يشككنا في أصل الحاجة إلى مخصص ، ولا يضرنا أيضاً فى قولنا : إن النفس لا تفنى بفناء البدن قلنا : مهما غابت المناسبة عنا ، وهى المقتضية للاختصاص ، فلا يبعد أن تكون تلك المناسبة المجهولة ، على وجه يحوج النفوس في بقائها إلى بقاء البدن ، خصوص فإنهم قالوا : الإنسان بالحقيقة هو النفس الناطقة ، وهى المكلف ، والمطيع ، والعاصي ، والمشاب ، والمعاقب ، والبدن يجرى منها مجرى الآلة ، والنفس باقية بعد فساد البدن ، فإذا أزاد الله تعالى حشر الخلائق ، خلق لكل واحد من الأرواح ، بدناً يتعلق به ، ويتصرف ، كما كان في الدنيا . ( د ) والرابع عدم ثبوت شيء منهما ، وهذا قول القدماء من الفلاسفة الطبيعيين . ( ه ) والخامس التوقف فى هذه الأقسام ، وهو المنقول عن جالينوس ، فإنه قال : لم يتبين لى أن النفس . هل هى المزاج ؟ ! فينعدم عند الموت ، فيستحيل إعادتها ، أو هي جوهر باق بعد فساد البنية ؟ ! [ فيمكن المعاد حينئذ ] . وليس في هذا النص ما يفيد أن جمهرة المتكلمين يقولون بنفس مجردة ، فضلا عن و بين البدن علاقة خاصة .. إلى آخر ما يقوله الغزالى فيه أن نعم · وجسم أن بينها الغزالي وطائفة معه ، يقولون : إن الإنسان مكون من نفس مجردة ، وانه إنسان بهما معاً في الدنيا ، وإنسان بهما معاً في الآخرة ، ولعله وطائفته ، يستطيعون أن يجدوا لذلك مسوغاً من النصوص أما أن النفس المجردة تفنى بموت البدن ، ثم تخلق عند إحيائه بالبعث والنشور ، فهو ما لم يعرف لغيره من هذه الطائفة ، بل ولا له بمقتضى هذا النص ، فضلا عن أن يكون هو ما ورد به الشرع . اللهم إلا بناء على رأى من يرى أن العالم كله سيفنى ثم يعاد خلقه من جديد ، ظاهر قوله تعالى : [ كل شيء هالك إلا وجهه ] أخذا من حتى إذا فسد فسدت ، فإن المجهول لا يمكن الحكم عليه ، بأنه يقتضى التلازم أم لا ، فلعل تلك النسبة ضرورية فى وجود النفس ، فإن انعدمت انعدمت ، فلا ثقة بالدليل الذي ذكر وه .
And that is not its corruption due to the corruption of the body. Yes, that longing may remain, which is desirous by stratagem of its management, after separation from the body, if in life its occupation with the body and its turning away from breaking desires and seeking intelligibles becomes strong, so it is pained by that longing, with the loss of the instrument by which the longing reaches its object. As for the specification of Zayd's soul to Zayd's person, in the first origination, it is due to a cause and suitability between the body and the soul inevitably, so that this body, for example, is more suitable for this soul than another due to increased suitability between them, so its specification is preferred. And it is not within human power to perceive those suitabilities, and our lack of knowledge of their details does not make us doubt the principle of the need for a specifier, nor does it harm us in our statement that the soul does not perish with the perishing of the body. We say: Whenever the suitability is hidden from us, and it is the requisite for specification, it is not unlikely that that unknown suitability is such that it necessitates souls in their remaining to the remaining of the body, especially since they said: Man in reality is the rational soul, and it is the one obligated, the obedient, the disobedient, the rewarded, the punished, and the body is in the position of an instrument for it, and the soul remains after the corruption of the body. So when God Almighty wills the resurrection of creatures, He creates for each of the spirits a body to which it attaches and acts, as was in this world. (D) The fourth: the non-establishment of either of them, and this is the saying of the ancients among the natural philosophers. (H) The fifth: suspension regarding these divisions, and it is transmitted from Galen, for he said: It has not become clear to me whether the soul is the temperament? Then it becomes non-existent at death, so its return is impossible, or it is a substance remaining after the corruption of the structure? Then the resurrection is possible then. And in this text there is nothing that indicates that the majority of the theologians say a simple soul, let alone... and between the body a special relation... to the end of what al-Ghazali says in it, that yes, and a body that between them... al-Ghazali and a group with him say that man is composed of a simple soul, and that he is a man by both together in this world, and a man by both together in the afterlife, and perhaps he and his group can find justification for that from the texts. As for the simple soul perishing by the death of the body, then being created at its revival by resurrection and raising, that is what has not been known from anyone else of this group, nor even for him according to this text, let alone it being what the Law has brought. Except based on the opinion of one who opines that the whole world will perish and then be created anew, according to the apparent meaning of His statement: 'Everything is perishing except His Face' (Quran 28:88) taken from... So that when it corrupts, it corrupts, for the unknown cannot be judged as to whether it necessitates inseparability or not. Perhaps that relation is necessary for the existence of the soul; if it ceases, it ceases, so there is no confidence in the proof that he mentioned.
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This passage presents a critique of the philosophers' argument for the immortality of the soul. Ghazali begins by discussing how the soul's longing for the body continues after death if it was strongly attached in life, causing pain. He then addresses the specification of a particular soul to a particular body, arguing that an unknown suitability (munasaba) between them exists, which we cannot fully perceive. Ghazali argues that this unknown suitability might be such that the soul depends on the body for its continued existence, undermining the philosophers' claim that the soul survives independently. He then presents the philosophers' view that man is essentially the rational soul, which is the subject of obligation, reward, and punishment, and that the body is merely an instrument, with the soul remaining after death and being reunited with a new body at resurrection. Following this, Ghazali lists two additional positions: the fourth view (of ancient natural philosophers) that neither soul nor body persists, and the fifth view (of Galen) expressing doubt whether the soul is the temperament (and thus perishes) or a substance that remains. Ghazali then criticizes the implication that most theologians (mutakallimun) assert a simple soul, pointing out that Ghazali himself and his group hold that man is composed of soul and body together in both worlds. He asserts that the notion of the simple soul perishing at death and being recreated is not found in the group's texts, except on the view that the whole world perishes and is recreated. The passage concludes with the key argument: since the relation between soul and body is unknown, it cannot be assumed that the soul does not depend on the body. Therefore, the proof that the soul survives death is unreliable.

Key Terms
نفس ناطقة (rational soul)
مناسبة (suitability/relation)
اختصاص (specification)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The soul's simplicity (being a simple substance) guarantees its immortality and independence from the body.
Correction: Ghazali argues that even if the soul is simple, its relation to the body is unknown; this unknown relation might still require the body for the soul's existence, so simplicity does not ensure survival.

Checkpoint
Why does Ghazali argue that the proof for the soul's immortality is unreliable?
A) Because the soul is a simple substance.
B) Because the relation between soul and body is unknown and may require the body for the soul's existence.
C) Because the philosophers agree the soul perishes.
D) Because Galen said so.

Key Takeaway
The unknown suitability (munasaba) between soul and body casts doubt on the philosophers' claim that the soul survives the body's death.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have heard since childhood that your soul lives on after death. But if the unknown suitability might tie your soul so tightly to your body that it perishes, what then? Does that possibility shake your confidence, or can you live with the uncertainty? What are you actually building for a life you may not even have after death?
?
Ghazali shows that even the philosophers' clever proof fails because of one unknown factor. Think about your own faith: is there any belief you hold simply because it is common, without having examined its evidence? Are you willing to dig into what the Quran and authentic texts really say about the soul, even if it unsettles your assumptions?
?
If the soul's survival beyond the grave is not a certainty we can prove through human intellect, then on what ground do you place your hope for the Hereafter? Is your hope anchored in rational demonstration or in the mercy of the One who creates and recreates? And does that anchor hold when you face the unknown?

Galen's Doubt and Ghazali's Resolution
Ghazali enumerates positions on the soul and defends his own view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth and fifth positions on the soul's fate after death and how Ghazali resolves the issue by affirming the soul-body composite.

Core Idea
Ghazali affirms that man is a composite of soul and body in both worlds, rejecting the view of the soul as a surviving simple substance.

After critiquing the philosophers' proof, Ghazali enumerates other views: the ancient naturalists who deny persistence of both, and Galen who suspends judgment. He then clarifies his own position—that man is soul and body together—and argues that the unknown relation does not allow a confident proof of the soul's independence.

Guided Path
1
Read the fourth and fifth positions
Examine the enumerated positions: the fourth (ancient naturalists) says neither soul nor body persists; the fifth (Galen) is uncertain whether the soul is the temperament or a substance.
Pay attention to how Galen's doubt connects to Ghazali's earlier critique.
2
Understand Ghazali's criticism of the simple soul view
Ghazali argues that the text does not support the claim that most theologians affirm a simple soul independent of the body. He states his own view: man is a composite of soul and body in this world and the next.
This is a direct response to the philosophers' position.
3
See the final argument
Ghazali concludes that because the relation between soul and body is unknown, we cannot be sure the soul does not depend on the body. Therefore, the philosophers' proof is unreliable.
The unknown relation is key throughout.

According to Ghazali, what does Galen's doubt about the soul's nature show?
A) That the soul is definitely the temperament.

Ghazali's own position is that man is soul and body together in both worlds, and the unknown relation prevents a definitive proof of the soul's survival.

?

?
Ghazali says the relation between your soul and your body is unknown. If something so intimate to you remains a mystery, how does that challenge your pride in your own understanding? Will you accept the limits of your knowledge and turn to the One who knows?
?
The philosophers claimed certainty, but Ghazali shows they had no trustworthy proof. Are there areas of your faith where you have accepted claims without real evidence from revelation? How will you seek the truth from the Qur'an and Sunnah, even when it humbles your intellect?

Logic and Technical Terms

Fourth Objection: The Three Ways of Non-Existence Are Not Exhaustive
Ghazali objects that the philosophers' enumeration of ways of non-existence is not proven exhaustive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's objection that the philosophers' claim of three exhaustive ways of non-existence lacks demonstrative proof.

Core Idea
A classification is not proven exhaustive unless it is a true dichotomy.

Ghazali has already conceded the three ways of non-existence as possibilities. Now he challenges the philosophers' assertion that these are the only possibilities. He argues that without a rigorous proof that the classification is exhaustive, one cannot rule out additional ways. This objection strikes at the logical foundation of the philosophers' argument for the soul's immortality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the English translation. Allow the objection to sink in before interpreting.
2
Identify Ghazali's concession and challenge
Notice that Ghazali first concedes the three ways, then asks for proof that these are the only possible ways of non-existence.
This is the core of the objection: the philosophers' classification may be incomplete.
3
Understand key terms
Focus on the terms 'taqsim' (division), 'hasr' (restriction), and 'burhan' (demonstration). How do these relate to the argument?
Burhan is a logical demonstration, not just empirical observation.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this objection matters: if the three ways are not exhaustive, the philosophers' argument for the soul's immortality collapses.
This shows how Ghazali uses rigorous logical scrutiny.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاعتراض الرابع ، هو أن يقال : ذكرتم أن هذه الطرق الثلاث في العدم ، وهو مسلم ، فما الدليل على أن عدم الشيء ، لا يتصور إلا بطريق تنحسم من هذه الطرق الثلاث ؟ ! فإن التقسيم إذا لم يكن دائراً بين النفى والإثبات فلا يبعد أن يزيد على الثلاث والأربع، فلعل للعدم طريقماً رابعاً ، وخامساً، سوى ما ذكرتموه ، فحصر الطرق فى هذه الثلاث غير معلوم بالبرهان .
The fourth objection is that it be said: You mentioned that these three ways of non-existence, and that is conceded, but what is the proof that non-existence of a thing is not conceived except by a way that is encompassed by these three ways? For if the division is not exhaustive between affirmation and negation, it is not unlikely that it increases beyond three and four. Perhaps non-existence has a fourth and a fifth way besides what you mentioned. So the restriction of the ways to these three is not known by demonstration.
Source pages p. 279

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth objection to the philosophers' argument that the soul cannot be annihilated because only three ways of non-existence are possible. Ghazali concedes that the three ways (which were presumably listed earlier) are accepted, but he challenges the claim that these three are exhaustive. He argues that the classification (taqsim) is not necessarily a complete dichotomy between existence and non-existence, so there could be additional ways beyond three or four. Therefore, the restriction to three ways is not proven by demonstration (burhan). This objection undermines the philosophers' reasoning that the soul's annihilation would have to occur through one of those three ways, and since none apply, the soul is immortal.

Key Terms
طرق العدم (ways of non-existence)
تقسيم (division/classification)
حصر (restriction)
برهان (demonstration)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A simple division into yes/no is always exhaustive.
Correction: Ghazali shows that without a proof that the division covers all possibilities, it may not be exhaustive.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the fourth objection?
A) The three ways of non-existence are proven by demonstration.
B) The soul cannot be annihilated at all.
C) The philosophers' enumeration of three ways of non-existence may not be exhaustive.
D) Non-existence is impossible for substances.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' restriction of non-existence to three ways is not proven by demonstration, so their argument for the soul's immortality fails.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you accepted someone's argument as complete and final simply because it was presented confidently? What might you be missing by not demanding a similar proof (burhan) for its exhaustiveness?
?
Consider your own reasoning in matters of belief: are there areas where you assume you have covered all possibilities? How can you guard against the assumption of completeness in your understanding of faith?
?
Ghazali challenges the philosophers to provide a proof for their classification. In your life, do you hold your own convictions to the same standard of proof? Where might you be content with an unproven assumption?

Second Proof: Simple Substances Cannot Be Annihilated
Ghazali presents the philosophers' second proof for the soul's immortality based on the impossibility of annihilating simple substances.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophical argument that a simple substance not in a subject cannot undergo annihilation, because non-existence requires a potentiality that must inhere in a material substrate.

Core Idea
Annihilation requires a substrate; simple substances lack one, so they are indestructible.

This passage presents the second and more elaborate proof that the philosophers rely on for the soul's immortality. Ghazali here explains their reasoning: if a substance is not in a subject (mahall), it cannot have the potentiality for non-existence, which must inhere in something. The proof builds on the analogy with change and the eternity of the world, drawing a parallel between the possibility of existence and non-existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the claim carefully
Start by reading the passage and its translation. Notice the central claim: 'Every substance not in a subject is impossible for it to become non-existent.' This is the foundation of the proof.
The term 'substance' (jawhar) here means a simple, indivisible entity like the soul.
2
Understand the analogy with existence
The proof uses an analogy: just as the possibility of existence must precede existence and inhere in a pre-existing matter, similarly the possibility of non-existence must precede non-existence and inhere in something that remains.
This is derived from the earlier discussion on the eternity of the world, where change requires a substrate.
3
Apply to the soul
Recognize that the soul is a simple substance not in a subject (it is not located in matter). Therefore, it has no substrate to receive the potentiality of non-existence, and thus it cannot be annihilated.
This supports the philosophers' claim of the soul's immortality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دليل ثان ، وعليه تعويلهم وعليه تعويلهم ، أن قالوا: كل جوهر ليس في محل فيستحيل عليه العدم ، بل البسائط لا تنعدم قط ، وهذا الدليل، يثبت فيه أولا أن انعدام البدن ، لا يوجب انعدام النفس لما سبق ، فبعد ذلك يقال : يستحيل أن ينعدم بسبب آخر ، لأن كل ما ينعدم بسبب ما ، ــ أي سبب كان ــ ففيه قوة الفساد ، قبل الفساد ، أى إمكان العدم سابق على العدم ، كما أن ما يطرأ وجوده من الحوادث ، فيكون إمكان الوجود سابقاً على الوجود ، ويسمى إمكان الوجود قوة الوجود ، وإمكان العدم قوة الفساد ، وكما أن إمكان الوجود وصف إضافي ، لا يقوم إلا بشيء ، حتى يكون إمكاناً بالإضافة إليه ، فكذلك إمكان العدم ؛ ولذلك قيل : إن كل حادث ، مفتقر إلى مادة سابقة ، يكون فيها إمكان وجود الحادث وقوته ، كما سبق في مسألة قدم العالم ، فالمادة التي فيها قوة الوجود ، قابلة للوجود الطارئ ، والقابل غير المقبول ، فيكون القابل موجوداً مع المقبول عند طريانه ، وهو غيره ، فكذلك قابل العدم ، ينبغي أن يكون كما وجد فيه شيء ، ويكون موجوداً عند طريان العدم ، حتى يعدم منه شيء ، ما عدم غير ما بقى ، ويكون ما بقى هو الذى فيه قوة العدم ، وقبوله وإمكانه .
A second proof, and upon it they rely, that they said: Every substance not in a subject is impossible upon it non-existence; rather, simple substances are never annihilated. And this proof establishes first that the annihilation of the body does not necessitate the annihilation of the soul due to what preceded. Then after that it is said: It is impossible for it to be annihilated due to another cause, because everything that is annihilated due to something—whatever cause it may be—has in it the potentiality of corruption before corruption, i.e., the possibility of non-existence precedes non-existence, just as what happens to existence from events, so the possibility of existence precedes existence, and it is called the potentiality of existence, and the possibility of non-existence is the potentiality of corruption. And just as the possibility of existence is a relative attribute, subsisting only in something, so that it is possibility in relation to it, likewise the possibility of non-existence. Therefore it is said: Every originated thing is dependent on a prior matter in which is the possibility of the existence of the originated thing and its potentiality, as preceded in the issue of the eternity of the world. So the matter in which is the potentiality of existence is receptive to the adventitious existence, and the receptor is not the received, so the receptor exists with the received at its advent, and it is other than it. Likewise, the receptor of non-existence should be as something exists in it, and it exists at the advent of non-existence, so that something becomes non-existent from it; what becomes non-existent is other than what remains, and what remains is that in which is the potentiality of non-existence, its receptivity and its possibility.
Source pages p. 279

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' second proof for the immortality of the soul. The proof argues that any substance (jawhar) that is not in a subject (mahall) cannot undergo non-existence; simple substances (al-basa'it) are never annihilated. The proof proceeds in two steps: First, it establishes that the annihilation of the body does not cause the soul's annihilation, as already argued. Second, it argues that the soul cannot be annihilated by any other cause because annihilation requires a prior potentiality (quwwat al-fasad) in a substrate. The proof draws an analogy with originated events: the possibility of existence must precede existence, and that possibility inheres in a pre-existing matter. Similarly, the possibility of non-existence must inhere in something that remains when another thing becomes non-existent. Thus, if the soul is a simple substance with no substrate, it cannot have such potentiality and therefore cannot be annihilated. The proof references the earlier discussion on the eternity of the world, where the need for a material substrate for change was established.

Key Terms
جوهر (substance)
محل (subject/locus)
بسائط (simple substances)
قوة الفساد (potentiality of corruption)
إمكان العدم (possibility of non-existence)
مادة (matter)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Anything can be annihilated by God's power directly without need for a substrate.
Correction: This proof argues that annihilation is not a direct act but requires a potentiality in a substrate; simple substances lack such a substrate, so even God cannot annihilate them in this framework.

Checkpoint
Why does the second proof claim that simple substances cannot be annihilated?
A) Because they are eternal.
B) Because they have no prior matter to receive the possibility of non-existence.
C) Because God cannot destroy them.
D) Because they are composite.

Key Takeaway
The soul, as a simple substance not in a subject, cannot cease to exist because annihilation requires a potentiality that must inhere in a material substrate, which the soul lacks.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your soul, this simple substance, cannot die. Yet you live as though death ends everything. What would it mean for you to truly believe that your soul is indestructible? How would your daily choices shift if you saw every moment as an investment in something eternal?
?
You have placed your trust in things that will pass — your body, your reputation, your wealth. But if the soul is simple and indestructible, what are you feeding it today that will survive your body's death? Will it be nourished or starved when you stand before your Maker?
?
The philosophers argue that the soul cannot be annihilated by any cause. Yet you fear loss, decay, and the dissolution of your identity. Do you fear the death of your body or the state of your soul? If the soul is imperishable, what truly should you fear?

Each Psychic Power Has Its Own Pleasure and Pain
Ibn Sina enumerates pleasures specific to psychic powers and states their common principle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's principle that each psychic power has a unique pleasure and pain, and the common feature of awareness of what is agreeable leading to perfection.

Core Idea
Every psychic faculty has its own unique pleasure and pain.

This lesson introduces Ibn Sina's foundational principle in the psychology of pleasures. He argues that sensory pleasures are just one type; each inner faculty has its own distinct delight. Understanding this is key to his theory of happiness and perfection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the examples: appetite, anger, estimation, memory.
2
Identify each faculty and its pleasure
The passage lists four psychic powers and their specific pleasures. Find each one and note how Ibn Sina defines the pleasure for each.
Appetite: suitable sensory quality; Anger: victory; Estimation: hope; Memory: recollection.
3
Grasp the common principle
Ibn Sina then points out a shared characteristic: the awareness of what is agreeable is the good and pleasure specific to each. The ultimate agreeable for each is the attainment of its proper perfection.
This connects the specific pleasures to a universal idea of perfection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يجب أن تعلم أن لكل قوة نفسانية ، لذة وخيراً يخصها ، وأذى وشراً يخصها . مثاله : أن لذة الشهوة وخيرها ، أن يتأدى إليها كيفية محسوسة ملائمة ، ولذة الغضب الظفر ، ولذة الوهم الرجاء ، ولذة الحفظ تذكر الأمور الموافقة الماضية ، وأذى كل واحد منها ما يضاده . وتشترك كلها نوعاً من الشركة ، في أن الشعور بموافقها وملائمها ، هو الخير واللذة الخاصة بها ، والموافق لكل واحد منها بالذات والحقيقة ، هو حصول الكمال ، الذي هو بالقياس إليه كمال بالفعل . فهذا أصل .
You should know that for every psychic power there is a pleasure and good specific to it, and a harm and evil specific to it. Example: the pleasure of the appetite and its good is that a suitable sensory quality reaches it; the pleasure of anger is victory; the pleasure of the estimative faculty is hope; the pleasure of memory is recollection of agreeable past matters; and the harm of each one is its opposite. And they all share a kind of commonality in that the awareness of what is agreeable and suitable is the good and pleasure specific to it, and the agreeable for each one in itself and in truth is the attainment of the perfection that is, relative to it, perfection in actuality. This is a principle.
Source pages p. 288

Commentary
This passage presents a foundational principle: each psychic power (quwwa nafsāniyya) has its own specific pleasure and pain. Ibn Sina gives examples: the appetite (shahwa) delights in reaching a suitable sensory quality; anger (ghadab) in victory; the estimative faculty (wahm) in hope; memory (hifz) in recalling agreeable past events. The harm for each is its opposite. He then notes that despite diversity, they share a common feature: the awareness of what is agreeable is the good and pleasure specific to that faculty. The ultimate agreeable for each is the attainment of its proper perfection (kamāl bi'l-fi'l). This is the first principle of his psychology of pleasures.

Key Terms
قوة نفسانية
الشهوة
الغضب
الوهم
الحفظ
الكمال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Pleasure is only sensory or physical.
Correction: Ibn Sina shows that each psychic faculty has its own non-sensory pleasure, such as hope for the estimative faculty.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Sina, what is the pleasure of the estimative faculty (al-wahm)?
A) Victory.
B) A suitable sensory quality.
C) Hope.
D) Recollection of past agreeable matters.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Sina establishes that each psychic power has its own distinct pleasure and pain, and the common ultimate good is the attainment of its proper perfection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Which of these psychic powers—appetite, anger, hope, or memory—do you find yourself serving most often? How does that pleasure actually move you closer to or further from your ultimate perfection?
?
Ibn Sina says each power's pleasure is the awareness of what suits it. When you feel anger's pleasure in victory, can you pause to notice that it is a specific good? How does that compare to the pleasure of knowing truth or loving God?
?
The passage ends by stating that true good is the attainment of each power's perfection in actuality. Which of your faculties is most underdeveloped? What concrete step could you take today to move it from potential to actual perfection?

Ranks of Pleasures According to Perfection
Ibn Sina lists six criteria for ranking pleasures based on perfection of the power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's principle that the rank of a pleasure depends on the perfection of the power that experiences it, and you will be able to identify the six criteria used to compare pleasures.

Core Idea
Pleasure is ranked by the perfection of the power, not by worldly standards.

Ibn Sina builds on the earlier definition of pleasure as perception of perfection. Now he introduces a crucial refinement: not all pleasures are equal. Although every power experiences pleasure when it attains its perfection, the rank of that pleasure depends on the rank of the power itself. He lists six criteria to compare these ranks, preparing the ground for his conclusion that intellectual pleasures are supreme.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get a sense of Ibn Sina's concise style and the six criteria he lists.
The passage is a single sentence with a list.
2
Identify the six criteria
Ibn Sina mentions six ways in which perfections can differ: completeness, quantity, duration, attainability, intrinsic perfection in act, and intensity of perception. Locate each in the text.
The Arabic uses 'alladhī' clauses: والذي كماله أتم ...
3
Understand the principle
Grasp the overarching claim: the pleasure of a power is more intense and complete when its perfection excels in these six aspects. This is stated as 'a principle' (asl).
Ibn Sina will use this principle later to argue for the superiority of intellectual pleasures.
4
Reflect on a common misunderstanding
Many might think this passage is about ranking worldly pleasures (e.g., sensory vs. sensory). But Ibn Sina is ranking the faculties themselves by their perfection.
This distinction is crucial for his larger argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً فإن هذه القوى ، وإن اشتركت فى هذه المعانى ، فإن مراتبها فى الحقيقة مختلفة ، فالذي كماله أتم وأفضل ، والذي كماله أكثر ، والذى كماله أدوم ، والذى كماله أوصل إليه ، وأحصل له ، والذي هو في نفسه أكمل فعلا وأفضل ، والذي هو في نفسه أشد إدراكاً ، فاللذة له أبلغ وأوفى لا محالة . وهذا أصل .
And also, although these powers share in these meanings, their ranks in reality are different. So that whose perfection is more complete and better, that whose perfection is more, that whose perfection is more enduring, that whose perfection is more attainable and more acquired, that which is in itself more perfect in act and better, and that which is in itself more intense in perception—the pleasure for it is more intense and more complete without doubt. This is a principle.
Source pages p. 288

Commentary
This passage establishes a second principle: although all psychic powers share the feature of having pleasures based on perfection, the ranks of those perfections differ. Six criteria are mentioned for ranking: completeness, quantity, duration, attainability, intrinsic perfection in act, and intensity of perception. The power that excels in these criteria will have a more intense and complete pleasure. This prepares for Ibn Sina's argument that the intellectual soul's pleasure is superior to all other pleasures.

Key Terms
مراتب
الكمال
اللذة
القوى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This passage ranks pleasures by worldly standards (e.g., sensory pleasure vs. sensory pleasure).
Correction: Ibn Sina is ranking the faculties themselves by their perfection, not the pleasures directly. The pleasure follows from the perfection of the power.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Sina, which factor contributes to a greater pleasure?
A) The power being less perfect in itself.
B) The perfection being less enduring.
C) The power being more intense in perception.
D) The perfection being less attainable.

Key Takeaway
Pleasure is more intense and complete when the power's perfection excels in completeness, quantity, duration, attainability, intrinsic perfection, and intensity of perception.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Using the six criteria from this passage, honestly examine the pleasures you pursue most. Which of them are most complete, enduring, and intrinsically perfect? What does your real behavior reveal about what you truly value as perfection?
?
Ibn Sina argues that pleasure's intensity depends on the perfection of the power, not just the object. Think of a time you were deeply satisfied by something intellectual versus something physical. Which gave you a more lasting, 'complete' pleasure? Will you reorganize your life to pursue the higher perfections?
?
If you truly believed that the most perfect pleasure comes from the most perfect power—your intellect—what would you change today about how you spend your time, attention, and energy? What specific step will you take to cultivate that intellectual perfection?

Analogy of the Impotent and the Blind Man
Ibn Sina uses analogies to show that unexperienced pleasures are not desired.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's argument that desire for a pleasure requires prior experience or imagination, and why this supports the necessity of revelation for describing spiritual pleasures.

Core Idea
You cannot desire what you cannot imagine.

Ibn Sina uses two vivid analogies to make a subtle point about human psychology: knowing about a pleasure intellectually is not the same as desiring it. The impotent man knows intercourse is pleasurable but feels no specific longing, just as the blind man cannot desire beautiful forms. This lays the groundwork for the claim that spiritual pleasures of the afterlife must be described through familiar images in revelation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing it, then read the literal translation. Notice the two analogies and the incomplete ending (likely a textual corruption).
The OCR has 'والإسم' twice; it is likely 'والأصم' (the deaf) intended for the analogy with melodies.
2
Analyze the impotent man analogy
Identify how Ibn Sina distinguishes between intellectual knowledge of pleasure and specific desire. The impotent man knows intercourse is pleasurable but does not feel the yearning specific to it.
This shows that desire requires more than abstract knowledge.
3
Analyze the blind man analogy and the implied deaf man
The blind man cannot desire beautiful forms because he cannot see them. Similarly, the deaf man (from the corrected text) cannot desire well-ordered melodies. Both analogies reinforce the same point.
The text likely originally had 'الأصم' (the deaf) for the second analogy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً فإنه قد يكون الخروج إلى الفعل ، في كمال ما ، بحيث يعلم أنه كائن ولذيد ، ولا يتصور كيفيته ، ولا يشعر باللذاذة ما لم يحصل ؛ وما لم يشعر به ، لم يشتق إليه ولم ينزع نحوه ، مثل العنين ، فإنه متحقق أن للجماع لذة ، ولكنه لا يشتهيه ولا يحن نحوه ، الاشتهاء والحنين ، اللذان يكونان مخصوصين به ، بل شهوة أخرى ، كما يشتهى من يجرب ، من حيث يحصل به إدراك ، وإن كان مؤذياً ، وفى الجملة فإنه لا يتخيله . وكذلك حال الأكمه ، عند الصور الجميلة ، والإسم ، عند الصور الجميلة ، والإسم عند الألحان المنتظمة
And also it may be that the actualization of a certain perfection occurs such that one knows it exists and is pleasurable, but one does not conceive its modality nor feel its pleasure until it is obtained; and as long as one has not felt it, one does not long for it nor incline towards it, like the impotent man (al-‘innīn): he is certain that intercourse has pleasure, but he does not desire it nor yearn for it—the desire and yearning that are specific to it—but rather another desire, as one who has experienced desires [differently], from the perspective that perception occurs through it, even if it is harmful. In short, he does not imagine it. And similarly the case of the blind man (al-akmah) regarding beautiful forms, and the [؟] name (al-ism) regarding beautiful forms, and the [؟] name regarding well-ordered melodies.
Source pages p. 288

Commentary
This passage uses analogies to illustrate a key psychological point: one cannot desire a pleasure one has never experienced or even imagined. The first analogy is the impotent man (al-‘innīn) who knows intellectually that intercourse is pleasurable but does not feel the specific desire because he lacks the experience and imagination of that pleasure. He may have a different kind of desire. The second analogy is the blind man (al-akmah) who cannot desire beautiful forms because he cannot see them. The text then mentions 'al-ism' twice, which is likely a corruption for 'al-asamm' (the deaf) regarding well-ordered melodies. The point is that spiritual pleasures of the afterlife cannot be properly desired or understood by those who have not experienced them; hence the necessity of revelation to describe them in familiar terms.

Key Terms
العنين
الأكمه
الأصم (المصحح)
تصور اللذة
الاشتهاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ibn Sina claims that unknown pleasures can never be desired at all.
Correction: He distinguishes between general desire and specific desire; the impotent man still has a different desire, but not the specific one tied to the imagined pleasure.

Checkpoint
Why does the impotent man (al-‘innīn) not desire intercourse according to Ibn Sina?
A) He knows it is not pleasurable.
B) He cannot imagine or feel its specific pleasure.
C) He has no sexual drive at all.
D) He has experienced it and dislikes it.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Sina's analogies show that specific desire depends on experiential or imaginative acquaintance, implying that spiritual pleasures must be depicted through familiar terms in revelation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Ibn Sina says the impotent man knows intercourse is pleasurable but does not desire it. Are there spiritual pleasures—like the vision of Allah or the company of the prophets—that you intellectually affirm but do not truly long for? What would it take for you to genuinely desire them?
?
The blind man cannot desire beautiful sights. What 'blindness' do you have regarding the descriptions of Jannah in the Quran? Are there descriptions that leave you cold because you cannot imagine them? How can you actively cultivate imaginative acquaintance through reflection and dua?
?
If specific desire requires prior experience or imagination, are you relying only on rational acceptance of the afterlife without striving to make it real in your heart? What step will you take today to move from knowing to longing?

Superiority of Intellectual Perception and Pleasure
Ghazali argues for the superiority of intellectual perception and pleasure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's argument that intellectual perception is more perfect and its pleasure far exceeds sensory pleasures.

Core Idea
Intellectual pleasure is to sensory pleasure as tasting is to smelling.

Ghazali presents a powerful comparison between intellectual and sensory perception. He argues that the rational soul perceives more deeply and abstractly, and its pleasure is vastly superior. However, we are often distracted by worldly vices from experiencing it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice Ghazali's two main claims: the perceiver is more perfect, and its perception is more intense.
Focus on the tone of rhetorical questions.
2
Examine the description of the rational soul
The rational soul has more objects, penetrates deeper, and abstracts essences from accidents. Compare this to sensory perception.
3
Analyze the analogy at the end
Ghazali compares intellectual pleasure to sensory pleasure like tasting to smelling, emphasizing an infinite distance. Reflect on why this analogy is powerful.
This is the core of the lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما أن المدرك فى نفسه أكمل ، فأمر لا يخفى ، وأما أنه أشد إدراكاً ، فأمر أيضاً تعرفه بأدنى تذكر لما سلف بيانه . فإن النفس النطقية أكثر عدد مدركات ، وأشد تقصياً للمدرك ، وتجريداً له عن الزوائد ، غير الداخلة في معناه ، إلا بالعرض ، ولها الخوض في باطن المدرك وظاهره . بل كيف يقاس هذا الإدراك ، بذلك الإدراك ؟ ! أو كيف تقاس هذه اللذة ، باللذة الحسية ، والهيمية ، والغضبية ، ولكننا في عالمنا و بدننا وانغماسنا فى الرذائل ، لا نحس بتلك اللذة ، إذا حصل عندنا شيء من أسبابها ، كما أومأنا إليه في بعض ما قدمناه ، من الأصول ، ولذلك لا نطلبها ولا نحن إليها ، اللهم إلا أن نكون قد خلعنا ، ربقة الشهوة والغضب وأخواتها من أعناقنا ، وطالعنا شيئاً من تلك اللذة ، فحينئذ ربما تخيلنا منها ، خيالا طفيفاً ضعيفاً ، وخصوصاً عند انحلال المشكلات ، واستيضاح المطلوبات النفيسة . ونسبة التذاذنا هذا ، إلى التذاذنا ذلك ، نسبة الالتذاذ الحسى ، بتنشق روائح المذوقات اللذيدة ، إلى الالتذاذ بتطعمها ، بل أبعد من ذلك بعداً غير محدود .
As for the fact that the perceiver is more perfect in itself, it is not hidden. And as for the fact that it is more intense in perception, it is also something you know with the slightest recollection of what has been previously explained. For the rational soul has a greater number of perceivables, is more penetrating in investigating the object of perception, and more abstracting it from accidents that are not part of its meaning except by accident, and it can delve into the inner and outer of the perceived object. Indeed, how can this perception be compared to that perception?! Or how can this pleasure be compared to sensory, animalistic, and irascible pleasure?! But we, in our world and our body and our immersion in vices, do not feel that pleasure when some of its causes occur to us, as we have indicated in some of what we have previously presented of the principles. That is why we do not seek it nor incline towards it, unless we have cast off the yoke of lust, anger, and their sisters from our necks, and we have glimpsed something of that pleasure. Then perhaps we imagine from it a slight, weak imagination, especially at the resolution of difficulties and the clarification of precious objects of inquiry. The ratio of this pleasure of ours to that pleasure is like the ratio of sensory pleasure from smelling the aromas of delicious foods to the pleasure of tasting them, rather farther than that by an unbounded distance.
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage is an argument for the superiority of intellectual perception and pleasure. The author states two points: (1) the perceiver (the rational soul) is more perfect in itself, and (2) it is more intense in perception. He explains that the rational soul (al-nafs al-nāṭiqa) has more objects of perception, investigates more deeply, and abstracts the essence from accidental attributes. It can grasp both the inner and outer aspects of the perceived. Then he asks rhetorical questions: how can this perception be compared to sensory perception? How can this pleasure be compared to sensory, animalistic (bahīmiyya? but text has 'هيمية' which may be a typo), and irascible (ghaḍabiyya) pleasure? He then laments that due to our immersion in bodily vices, we do not feel that intellectual pleasure even when its causes are present, as we are distracted. Only when we free ourselves from lust and anger do we get a faint glimpse, especially during solving difficult problems. The analogy: our current intellectual pleasure compared to the full intellectual pleasure is like smelling food compared to tasting it, and even farther.

Key Terms
النفس النطقية
المدرك في نفسه أكمل
اللذة الحسية والهيمية والغضبية
تجريد المعقول عن الزوائد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Ghazali denies any value to sensory pleasure.
Correction: Ghazali only says intellectual pleasure is far superior, not that sensory pleasure is worthless.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, why do we not feel intellectual pleasure even when its causes are present?
A) Because intellectual pleasure does not exist
B) Because we are immersed in vices and distracted by the body
C) Because it is weaker than sensory pleasure
D) Because we lack the rational soul

Key Takeaway
Intellectual perception is more perfect and its pleasure is incomparably greater than sensory pleasure.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Which vice—lust, anger, or something else—is currently muffling the intellectual pleasure you could be tasting in your pursuit of knowledge? What will you do this week to loosen its yoke from your neck?
?
Recall a moment when a difficult question became clear and you felt a flash of joy. Did you recognize that as a glimpse of the intellectual pleasure Ghazali describes? How can you seek more of those moments through deliberate study and reflection?
?
Ghazali says we do not seek intellectual pleasure because we are immersed in vices. Is your daily routine truly oriented toward that higher pleasure, or are you settling for the faint smell when you could be tasting? Write down one immediate change you will make to prioritize the nourishment of your rational soul.

Philosophers on Power and Will: Claim and Refutation
Philosophers' claim and Ghazali's refutation, cut off

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' argument that God's power does not require actual will, and Ghazali's critique that this argument presupposes the eternity of the world.

Core Idea
Power is conditional; will is not required for capacity.

The philosophers argue that saying 'God is powerful over everything' does not mean He must actually will or do everything. They use a logical distinction between conditional and categorical statements. Ghazali replies that this argument illegitimately assumes the world's eternity. Let's examine their reasoning carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the Passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the philosophers' claim and logical argument.
The passage ends abruptly; we have only the philosophers' claim and the start of Ghazali's answer.
2
Understand the Logical Distinction
The philosophers distinguish between conditional propositions (e.g., 'if He willed, He would do') and categorical propositions (e.g., 'He did not will'). They claim a negative categorical does not contradict a positive conditional.
This is a key point in logic used to separate power from actual will.
3
Examine the Analogy
They give the example of a person able to harm himself but not actually doing so. This illustrates that power can exist without will or action.
The analogy is meant to be intuitive.
4
Identify Ghazali's Refutation
Ghazali responds that this argument relies on the presupposition of the world's eternity and the will being eternal. He claims this has already been refuted and says it is not unreasonable to posit three divisions (though the text cuts off).
Ghazali does not fully elaborate here due to the page break.
5
Reflect on the Implication
Consider how the philosophers' logical move is used to defend the eternity of the world, and why Ghazali sees it as a flawed premise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وزعموا أن هذا لا يناقض قولنا : إن الله تعالى قادر على كل شيء ، فإنا نقول : إن الله تعالى قادر على البعث والنشور ، وجميع الأمور الممكنة ، على معنى أنه لو شاء ، لفعل ، وليس من شرط صدق قولنا هذا ، أن يشاء ولا أن يفعل ، وهذا كما أنا نقول : إن فلاناً قادر على أن يجز رقبة نفسه ، ويبعج بطن نفسه ، ويصدق ذلك ، على معنى أنه لو شاء لفعل ، ولكنا نعلم أنه لا يشاء ولا يفعل ، وقولنا : لا يشاء ، ولا يفعل ، لا يناقض قولنا : إنه قادر ، أنه لو شاء لفعل ؛ فإن الحمليات لا تناقض الشرطيات ، كما ذكر في المنطق إذ قولنا : لو شاء لفعل ، شرطى موجب ، وقولنا : ما شاء ، وما فعل ، حمليتان سالبتان ، والسالبة الحملية ، لا تناقض الموجبة الشرطية . فإذن الدليل ، الذي دلنا على أن مشيئته أزلية ، وليست متغيرة ، يدلنا على أن مجرى الأمر الإلهى ، لا يكون إلا على انتظام واتساق بالتكرر والعود ، وإن اختلف في آحاد الأوقات ، فيكون اختلافه أيضاً على انتظام واتساق بالتكرر والعود ، وأما غير هذا ، فلا يمكن . والجواب ، أن هذا استمداد مسألة قدم العالم ، وأن المشيئة قديمة ، من فليكن العالم قديماً ، وقد أبطلنا ذلك وبينا أنه لا يبعد في العقل ، وضع ثلاثة أقسام ، وهى :
And they claimed that this does not contradict our statement: that God is powerful over everything. For we say: God is powerful over resurrection and the gathering and all possible things, meaning that if He willed, He would do. It is not a condition of the truth of this statement that He wills nor that He does. This is like our saying: So-and-so is able to cut off his own neck and rip open his own belly. That is true meaning that if he willed, he would do, but we know that he does not will and does not do. Our saying: he does not will and does not do does not contradict our saying: he is able, that if he willed, he would do. For categorical (propositions) do not contradict conditional propositions, as mentioned in logic. Indeed, our saying 'if he willed, he would do' is a positive conditional, and our saying 'he did not will and he did not do' are two negative categoricals. A negative categorical does not contradict a positive conditional. So then, the proof that has shown us that His will is eternal and not changing shows us that the course of the divine command can only be with order and consistency through repetition and return, even if it differs in individual times; its difference would also be with order and consistency through repetition and return. As for other than this, it is not possible. And the answer: this is drawing upon the issue of the eternity of the world, and that the will is eternal, so let the world be eternal. And we have refuted that and shown that it is not far in reason to posit three divisions, namely:
Source pages p. 305

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' claim and Ghazali's answer. The philosophers argue that God's power over all things does not require that He actually wills or performs every possible act; it is enough that if He willed, He would do. They draw an analogy with a person who is able to harm himself but does not. They then make a logical point: a conditional statement ('if he willed, he would do') is not contradicted by negative categorical statements ('he did not will, he did not do'). From this, they conclude that since the proof shows the will is eternal and unchanging, the divine command must proceed with order and consistency through repetition and return. Ghazali's answer is that this argument depends on the issue of the eternity of the world—if the will is eternal, then the world would be eternal, which has already been refuted. He then says it is not unreasonable to posit three divisions, but the text cuts off before listing them.

Key Terms
قادر على كل شيء
الحمليات
الشرطيات
قدم العالم
المشيئة الأزلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's power being universal means He must actualize every possible thing.
Correction: Power is conditional: if He wills, He acts. Not willing or acting does not contradict that capacity.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers in this passage, why does the statement 'God is powerful over everything' not require that He actually wills every possible thing?
A) Because God's will is eternal and unchanging.
B) Because power is a conditional: if He willed, He would do, and not willing does not contradict that.
C) Because categorical propositions always contradict conditionals.
D) Because God's action has one fixed course.

Key Takeaway
God's power is conditional on will, but the philosophers' argument fails because it presumes the world's eternity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The philosophers argue that power does not require actual will, using the example of a person who can harm himself but refrains. In your own life, when you say 'I could do this good deed if I wanted to,' but you don't, what stops you? Is it a lack of will, or are you hiding behind conditional power? How can you move from 'if I willed' to 'I will' for the sake of Allah?
?
Ghazali refutes the philosophers by pointing out their hidden assumption. What assumption about Allah's attributes might you be carrying that is not actually from revelation? How will you test that assumption against the Qur'an and Sunnah today?
?
The passage ends abruptly with a mention of 'three divisions' that are not out of reason. When you face a difficult theological question, are you quick to accept a single answer, or do you remain open to multiple valid possibilities? What does that say about your trust in Allah's wisdom versus your own need for certainty?

The Five Modes of Existence According to Ghazali
The editor enumerates Ghazali's five modes of existence and provides a brief definition for each.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's five modes of existence and how they serve as interpretive categories for understanding scriptural descriptions.

Core Idea
Ghazali's five modes show how scriptural statements can be true in different senses.

In his work Faysal al-tafriqa, Ghazali introduces a framework of five modes of existence to resolve apparent conflicts between reason and revelation. These modes are not metaphysical realities but interpretive categories that allow us to affirm the truth of scriptural descriptions in various ways. This passage from the editor summarizes Ghazali's classification, which is essential for understanding his approach to allegorical interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the full Arabic passage and its translation to get an overview of the five modes. Note the structure: each mode begins with 'أما' and is defined.
The passage includes editorial comments at the end.
2
Identify each mode and its definition
Go through each mode one by one. Underline the key phrase that defines the mode. Notice how they differ from each other.
Pay special attention to the example for intellectual existence.
3
Understand the purpose of this classification
Reflect on why Ghazali needed these categories. The framework allows him to interpret scriptural descriptions non-literally while still affirming their truth.
This is an interpretive tool, not a metaphysical doctrine.
4
Complete the checkpoint and activity
Test your understanding with the multiple-choice question, then create a chart summarizing the five modes.
The activity will help solidify the distinctions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الوجود الذاتي فهو الوجود الحقيق الثابت خارج الحس والعقل وأما الوجود الحسى، فهو ما يتمثل في القوة الباصرة من العين مما لا وجود له خارج العين، وذلك كما يشاهد الغانم [؟] . ... إلخ . وأما الوجود الخيالى، فهو صورة هذه المحسوسات إذا غابت عن حسك . . . إلخ . وأما الوجود العقلى فهو أن يكون للشيء روح وحقيقة ومعنى، فيتلقى العقل مجرد معناه، دون أن يثبت صورته في خيال أو خارج، كاليد مثلا، فإن لها صورة محسوسة ومتخيلة، ولهما معنى هو حقيقتها وهو القدرة على البطش، والقدرة على البطش، هي اليد العقلية . . . إلخ . وأما الوجود الشبهى، فهو ألا يكون نفس الشيء موجوداً، لا بصورته، ولا بحقيقته، لا في الخارج، ولا في الحس، ولا في الخيال، ولا فى العقل، ولكن يكون الموجود شيئاً آخر يشبهه، في خاصة من خواصه، وصفة من صفاته . . . إلخ ] . ولا أحب أن أطيل باستيفاء هذه الأقسام وشرحها فإنه يطول، بل ربما جر إلى نقل الكتاب برمته، فارجع إليه، فهو صغير وجيز
As for essential existence (al-wujud al-dhati), it is the true existence established outside sense and intellect. As for sensory existence (al-wujud al-hissi), it is what is represented in the visual faculty of the eye which has no existence outside the eye, such as what the [؟] sees... etc. As for imaginal existence (al-wujud al-khayali), it is the image of these sensed things when they disappear from your sense... etc. As for intellectual existence (al-wujud al-'aqli), it is that a thing has a spirit, reality, and meaning, so the intellect receives its abstract meaning, without its image being established in imagination or outside, like the hand for example, for it has a sensible and imagined form, and both have a meaning which is its reality, namely the ability to strike, and the ability to strike is the intellectual hand... etc. As for metaphorical existence (al-wujud al-shabahi), it is that the thing itself is not existent, neither in its form nor in its reality, neither externally, nor in sense, nor in imagination, nor in intellect, but rather something else resembling it exists, in one of its properties and a description of its attributes... etc. And I do not wish to prolong by covering all these divisions and explaining them, for it would be lengthy; rather it might lead to transferring the entire book. So refer to it, for it is small and concise.
Source pages p. 311

Commentary
This passage is a definition and categorization of the five modes of existence as presented in Ghazali's 'Faysal al-tafriqa'. The editor quotes Ghazali to illustrate the expansive framework within which prophetic reports about existence can be affirmed without literal falsehood. The five modes are: (1) Essential existence (al-wujud al-dhati): true existence independent of perception. (2) Sensory existence (al-wujud al-hissi): what appears in the sense faculty with no external reality, e.g., an optical illusion. (3) Imaginal existence (al-wujud al-khayali): the mental image of a previously sensed object when absent. (4) Intellectual existence (al-wujud al-'aqli): the abstract meaning or quiddity of a thing, like 'ability to strike' as the intellectual hand. (5) Metaphorical existence (al-wujud al-shabahi): when a thing is not existent in any of these forms, but something else resembling it exists. This framework allows for allegorical interpretation of texts. The editor refrains from fully elaborating, redirecting the reader to the original source.

Key Terms
الوجود الذاتي
الوجود الحسي
الوجود الخيالي
الوجود العقلي
الوجود الشبهي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The five modes of existence are distinct metaphysical realities.
Correction: They are interpretive categories for understanding scriptural descriptions, not independent metaphysical domains.

Checkpoint
According to the five modes of existence, which mode applies to the abstract meaning of a thing, such as the 'ability to strike' of the hand?
A) Essential existence
B) Sensory existence
C) Intellectual existence
D) Metaphorical existence

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's five modes of existence provide a nuanced interpretive framework for affirming the truth of scriptural descriptions without requiring literal correspondence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a verse like 'The Hand of Allah is above their hands' (48:10), which mode of existence do you instinctively assume? What would change in your relationship with Allah if you considered it an intellectual or metaphorical existence instead of a sensory one? Commit to re-reading one such verse this week with Ghazali's framework in mind.
?
Think of a time you rejected a spiritual truth because it didn't fit your literal reading of scripture. Could it be that the truth belongs to a different mode of existence? Are you willing to admit that your previous certainty might have been misplaced, and ask Allah to guide you to the correct understanding? This week, review one belief you hold strongly and test it against Ghazali's five modes.
?
If the intellectual existence of a thing is its true reality (like the ability to strike as the 'hand'), then what is the intellectual existence of your own soul? In what mode does your faith in the unseen truly exist—merely as a mental image, or as a living reality that transforms your actions? How can you move it from the imaginal to the essential today?

The Philosophers' Claim on Self-Subsistent Beings
A commentator reflects on the philosophers' claim about self-subsistent beings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the commentator's critique that the philosophers' claim about self-subsistent beings may only be dialectical, not investigative, and how it contradicts the nature of a possible being.

Core Idea
A claim true in debate may be false in reality.

In this passage, a commentator reflects on a philosophical claim: that every self-subsistent being cannot become non-existent after existence. He cautions that this may only be a dialectical statement used for argument, not a serious investigative truth. By examining the definition of a 'possible' being, he reveals a logical contradiction if the claim is taken as certain. This lesson explores that distinction and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and then the literal translation. Notice the commentator's cautious tone and the key terms 'jadali' (dialectical) and 'taḥqīqī' (investigative).
This passage is a footnote, so it reflects a personal reflection rather than a definitive ruling.
2
Distinguish the two contexts
Identify why the commentator accepts the claim if it is made dialectically (for argument) but questions it if made as an investigative truth. What is the difference between these two contexts?
Dialectical statements are used to persuade or win a debate; investigative statements seek truth.
3
Understand the contradiction
The commentator argues that a possible being (mumkin) by definition does not deserve existence or non-existence in itself. So how can it be said that its non-existence after existence is impossible? This would imply it deserves existence, contradicting its possibility.
This is the core logical problem that the commentator raises.
4
Reflect on the promised research
The commentator promises to return to this topic to investigate the philosophers' concept of the possible and whether the claim was made in a dialectical or investigative context. Consider why such an investigation is important.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عندى أن القول على لسان الفلاسفة بأن كل قائم بنفسه لا يتصور انعدامه بعد وجوده ؛ إن كان قولا جدليا في معرض الحجاج والمغالبة ، فلا بأس : أما إن كان قولا تحقيقيا ففي النفس منه شيء ؛ إذ كيف يكون الشيء الممكن هو ما لا يستحق الوجود ولا العدم لذاته ؟ ثم يقال : إنه عدمه بعد وجوده مستحيل ؟ ولعلى أعود إلى هذا القول في بحث مستقل إن شاء الله أتقصى فيه أحكام الممكن عند الفلاسفة ، لأتبين مدى صحة نسبة هذا القول إليهم ، فإن صح نسبته إليهم ، تبينت هل قالوه في مقام الجدل ، أم في مقام التحقيق ؟ فإن كان في مقام التحقيق ، تبينت هل يتفق هذا القول طبيعة الممكن ؟ أم يتعارض معها ولا يلائمها إنه بحث شيق ودقيق في الوقت ذاته ، أرجو أن أفرغ له قريباً .
In my view, the statement attributed to the philosophers that every self-subsistent not in a locus cannot be conceived to become non-existent after its existence: if it is a dialectical statement in the context of argumentation and sophistry, then there is no harm; but if it is an investigative (taḥqīqī) statement, then there is something in my soul about it. For how can a possible thing (al-mumkin) be that which does not deserve existence nor non-existence in itself, and then it is said that its non-existence after existence is impossible? And perhaps I will return to this statement in an independent research, God willing, in which I will investigate the judgments of the possible according to the philosophers, to ascertain the extent of the correctness of attributing this statement to them. If its attribution to them is correct, it will become clear whether they said it in the context of dialectic or in the context of investigation. If it is in the context of investigation, it will become clear whether this statement agrees with the nature of the possible or contradicts it and is incompatible with it. It is an interesting and precise research at the same time; I hope to find time for it soon.
Source pages p. 335

Commentary
This passage is a footnote by a commentator (possibly the editor or a later scholar) reflecting on the philosophers' claim that every self-subsistent being not in a locus cannot become non-existent after existence. The commentator distinguishes between two contexts: if the statement is made dialectically (jadaliyyan) for the sake of argument and sophistry, it is acceptable; but if it is made as a serious investigative (taḥqīqiyyan) claim, then it is problematic. The reason is that a possible thing (mumkin) is defined as that which does not deserve either existence or non-existence in itself. So how can it be said that its non-existence after existence is impossible? That would imply that it deserves existence, which contradicts its possiblity. The commentator promises to return to this topic in a separate research to investigate the philosophers' concept of the possible and to determine whether this statement was made in dialectical or investigative context, and whether it is consistent with the nature of the possible.

Key Terms
جدلي (jadali)
تحقيقي (taḥqīqī)
ممكن (mumkin)
قائم بنفسه (qā’im bi-nafsihī)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the philosophers' claim that self-subsistent beings cannot become non-existent is an absolute truth.
Correction: The commentator shows that this claim may be only dialectical, not investigative, and if taken as investigative, it contradicts the definition of a possible being.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what problem arises if the philosophers' statement is taken as a serious (taḥqīqī) claim?
A) It leads to an infinite regress.

C) It is unsupported by evidence.
D) It is a denial of God's power.

Key Takeaway
The context of a philosophical claim—whether dialectical or investigative—is essential for evaluating its truth and coherence with foundational concepts like possibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
