Ghazali's Definition of the Impossible
Ghazali defines the impossible as logical contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's precise logical definition of the impossible and why it is limited to logical contradictions.

Core Idea
The impossible is only a logical contradiction, not a habitual break.

Imam Ghazali establishes a crucial distinction in this short definition: the impossible is not whatever is unusual or beyond normal experience. Instead, it is strictly the conjunction of affirmation and negation, a logical self-contradiction. This principle frees God's power from the limitations of human habit while preserving logical consistency.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Read Ghazali's one-sentence definition of the impossible in Arabic and then in English.
2
Understand the key terms
Focus on the phrase 'conjunction of negation and affirmation.' What does this mean in logic?
It means a statement that is both true and false at the same time.
3
Apply to miracles
Based on this definition, why can miracles not be considered impossible?
Miracles do not involve a logical contradiction; they are merely unusual.
4
Avoid the common error
Some might think impossible means 'never happens.' See how Ghazali corrects this.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمحال : هو ما يرجع إلى الجمع بين النفى والإثبات
And the impossible: it is what reduces to the conjunction of negation and affirmation.
Source pages p. 361

Commentary
This passage provides a definition of the impossible (المحال). Ghazali states that the impossible is that which involves the combination of negation and affirmation, i.e., a logical contradiction. This is a fundamental principle in kalam: miracles are not impossible because they do not involve contradiction; they are merely habitual interruptions. Thus, the impossible is limited to logical absurdities like 'a thing can be and not be at the same time.' This definition clarifies that what is not logically contradictory is possible for God.

Key Terms
المحال (the impossible)
الجمع بين النفى والإثبات (conjunction of negation and affirmation)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people think that miracles are impossible because they violate natural laws.
Correction: Ghazali restricts impossibility to logical contradictions only; miracles are possible for God because they do not involve self-contradiction.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what is the definition of the impossible?
A) Whatever never occurs
B) The conjunction of negation and affirmation
C) Whatever God does not do
D) Any unusual event

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's definition narrows impossibility to logical contradictions, affirming God's power over all that is not self-contradictory.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a seemingly impossible situation in your life, do you immediately despair, forgetting that the only true impossibility is a logical contradiction? How can this distinction between habit and logic transform your reliance on God?
?
Have you ever limited God's power by calling something 'impossible' that is merely difficult or unusual? What would it look like to trust that God can do anything that is not a contradiction—even if it defies your expectations?
?
Imagine you are tempted to say 'God cannot forgive me for this sin.' According to Ghazali, is that a true impossibility? What does this mean for your repentance and hope in God's mercy?

Classification of Animal and Human Powers
Ghazali classifies animal powers and rational soul powers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' detailed classification of animal powers (moving and perceiving) and the two powers of the rational human soul (theoretical and practical).

Core Idea
The soul's powers are hierarchically classified: animal powers are bodily, rational powers are intellectual.

In this passage, al-Ghazali presents the philosophers' detailed classification of the powers that animate living beings. He distinguishes between animal powers—both moving and perceiving—and the human rational soul, which has theoretical and practical capacities. This taxonomy sets the stage for his critique of their proof regarding the soul's nature.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the English translation to understand the full classification.
Pay attention to the technical terms in Arabic; they will reappear in later discussions.
2
Identify the two main categories
Note that Ghazali first mentions the animal powers (القوى الحيوانية) and then the rational soul (النفس العاقلة). Each has its own subdivisions.
3
Subdivide animal powers
Animal powers are divided into perceiving (مدركة) and moving (محركة). Perceiving has external (five senses) and internal (three: imagination, estimation, thought). Moving has inciting (appetitive/irascible) and agent (nerves/muscles).
The internal perceiving power called 'mutakhayyila' in animals becomes 'mufakkira' in humans.
4
Analyze the rational soul
The rational soul has two powers: practical (عاملة) which moves the body toward crafts, and theoretical (عالِمة/نظرية) which perceives intelligibles.
5
Reflect on the purpose
Consider why Ghazali presents this classification: it prepares his argument that the philosophers cannot prove the soul is a spiritual substance solely through reason.
The classification shows that both animal and human powers are tied to the body, except the theoretical power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٢٦ - مسألة تعجيزهم عن إقامة البرهان العقلى على أن النفس الإنسانية جوهر روحانى قائم بنفسه ، لا يتميز وليس بجسم ، ولا منطبع في جسم ولا هو متصل بالبدن ولا منفصل عنه قال الغزالي : يرى الفلاسفة أن القوى الحيوانية تنقسم إلى قسمين : محركة ومدركة والمدركة قسمان : ظاهرة ، وباطنة والظاهرة هي الحواس الخمس ، وهي معان منطبعة في الأجسام وأما الباطنة فثلاث : إحداها : القوة الخيالية ، في مقدم الدماغ الثانية : القوة الوهمية الثالثة : القوة التى تسمى فى الحيوان ) متخيلة ) وفى الإنسان ( مفكرة ) وأما المحركة فتنقسم : إلى محركة على معنى أنها باعثة على الحركة و إلى محركة على معنى أنها مباشرة للحركة . . . فاعلة والباعثة : هى القوة النزوعية الشوقية ولها شعبتان : شعبة تسمى قوة شوقية وشعبة تسمى قوة غضبية والفاعلة : قوة منبثة فى الأعصاب والعضلات تجذب الأوتار والرباطات المتصلة بالأعضاء وأما النفس العاقلة الإنسانية ؛ فلها قوتان : قوة عالمة . وقوة عاملة فالعاملة : قوة هى مبدأ محرك لبدن الإنسان ، إلى الصناعات المرتبة الإنسانية المستنبط ترتيبها بالروية الخاصة بالإنسان أما العالمة : فهى التى تسمى النظرية ، وهي قوة من شأنها أن تدرك حقائق المعقولات
26th issue: Incapacitating them from establishing a rational proof that the human soul is a spiritual substance subsisting by itself, not divisible, not a body, not imprinted in a body, nor is it connected to the body nor separate from it. Al-Ghazali said: The philosophers see that the animal powers are divided into two divisions: moving and perceiving. The perceiving is two kinds: external and internal. The external are the five senses, and they are meanings imprinted in bodies. As for the internal, they are three: one of them is the imaginative power (al-quwwa al-khayaliyya) in the front of the brain. The second: the estimative power (al-quwwa al-wahmiyya). The third: the power called in animals 'mutakhayyila' and in humans 'mufakkira' (thought). As for the moving, it is divided: into a mover meaning that it incites to motion, and into a mover meaning that it directly performs the motion, an agent. The inciter is the appetitive-desiderative power, and it has two branches: a branch called the appetitive power, and a branch called the irascible power. The agent is a power diffused in the nerves and muscles that attracts the tendons and ligaments connected to the limbs. As for the rational human soul, it has two powers: a theoretical power and a practical power. The practical is a power that is a motive principle for the human body towards the ordered human crafts, their order being derived through the special deliberation of the human. As for the theoretical, it is called the speculative power, and it is a power whose function is to perceive the realities of intelligibles.
Source pages p. 361

Commentary
This passage begins with the heading of the twenty-sixth issue, which aims to incapacitate the philosophers from providing a rational proof that the human soul is a spiritual substance. Ghazali then presents the philosophers' classification of powers. The animal powers are divided into moving and perceiving. The perceiving powers are external (the five senses, which are imprinted in bodies) and internal. The internal perceiving powers are three: the imaginative power located in the front of the brain, the estimative power, and the power called 'mutakhayyila' in animals and 'mufakkira' (thought) in humans. The moving powers are divided into those that incite motion and those that directly perform the motion. The inciters are the appetitive (desire) and irascible (anger) powers. The direct agent is a power diffused in the nerves and muscles. Then Ghazali turns to the rational human soul, which has two powers: a theoretical (scientific) power that perceives intelligibles, and a practical power that moves the body towards crafts derived by human deliberation. This classification sets the stage for Ghazali's objection to the philosophers' claim that the soul can be proved as a substance by reason alone.

القوة الوهمية (estimation)

القوة النزوعية (appetitive)
القوة الغضبية (irascible)
القوة العاملة (practical power)

Misconception: One might think that the internal perceiving powers (imagination, estimation, thought) are part of the rational soul.
Correction: Ghazali clearly classifies them as animal powers, not rational. The rational soul has only practical and theoretical powers, which are distinct from these bodily-based internal senses.

The philosophers classify all powers of living beings into animal (bodily) and rational (soul) categories, with detailed subdivisions of perceiving, moving, and intellectual powers.

?

?
The philosophers locate imagination and estimation in the brain as animal powers—do you ever let your imagination create false images that lead you away from the truth? How can you discipline your mind to let your theoretical power rule instead?

The First Proof for the Soul's Immateriality and Its Refutation
Ghazali critiques the first philosophical proof for the soul's immateriality via two objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Ghazali refutes the first philosophical proof for the immateriality of the soul by exposing its flawed assumptions.

Core Idea
Ghazali deconstructs the proof by showing it confuses knowledge with physical properties.

The philosophers argue that because knowledge contains indivisible concepts, its locus must be indivisible—hence the soul is not a body. Ghazali challenges this on two fronts: first, why reject an indivisible atomic substance? Second, the sheep's perception of wolf enmity is indivisible, contradicting the claim that all states in a body are divisible. He then identifies the real problem: the assumption that knowledge is impressed in the body like color.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the structure: proof, then two objections, then a counter and final analysis.
2
Identify the philosophers' proof
Find the two premises: rational sciences contain indivisible units, and every body is divisible. The conclusion is that the soul is not a body.
3
Understand Ghazali's first objection
Ghazali asks: why reject the possibility that the locus of knowledge is an indivisible atomic substance? This undermines the need for a non-body.
4
Understand Ghazali's second objection
The sheep's estimative faculty perceives wolf enmity as a single indivisible judgment, showing that some states in a body are not divisible.
This refutes the claim that everything in a body is divisible.
5
Reflect on the final equivocation
Ghazali points out that the philosophers assumed knowledge is like color impressed in the body, but this is the point of confusion.
He exposes the flaw rather than denying the soul's immateriality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الدليل الأول : أن العلوم العقلية تحل النفوس الإنسانية وفيها آحاد لا تنقسم فلابد أن يكون محلها لا ينقسم . وكل جسم فمنقسم . قال الغزالي : والاعتراض على مقامين : المقام الأول : أن يقال : بم تنكرون على من يقول : محل العلم جوهر فرد متحيز لا ينقسم . [جسم فينبغي] . المقام الثانى : أن نقول : ما زعمتموه من أن كل حال في أن ينقسم، باطل عليكم بما تدركه القوة الوهمية التي في الشاة ، من عداوة الذئب ؛ فإنها في حكم شيء واحد لا ينقسم . قال الغزالى : فإن قيل : هذه مناقضة فى المعقولات ، والمعقولات لا تنقض ؛ فإنكم مهما لم تقدروا على الشك في المقدمتين وهو أن العلم الواحد لا ينقسم ، وأن ما لا ينقسم لا يقوم بجسم منقسم ، لم يمكنكم الشك في النتيجة قلنا : إن هذا الكتاب ما صنفناه إلا لبيان التهافت والتناقض في كلام الفلاسفة وقد حصل . قال الغزالي : ثم نقول : هذه المناقضة تبين أنهم غفلوا عن موضع تلبيس في القياس ، ولعل موضع الالتباس قولهم : إن العلم منطبع فى الجسم ، انطباع اللون فى محله ، فينقسم العلم بانقسام الجسم مثل ما ينقسم اللون بانقسام محله
The first proof: that the rational sciences inhere in human souls, and in them there are indivisible units, so their locus must be indivisible. And every body is divisible. Al-Ghazali said: The objection is in two stations: The first station: that it is said: how do you reject the one who says: the locus of knowledge is an indivisible atomic substance that occupies space? [A body accordingly?]. The second station: that we say: what you claim that every state [??] is divisible is refuted by what the estimative faculty in the sheep perceives, of enmity of the wolf; for it is, in terms of judgment, one indivisible thing. Al-Ghazali said: If it is said: this is a contradiction in rational matters, and rational matters cannot be contradicted; for as long as you are unable to doubt the two premises—that knowledge is indivisible, and that what is indivisible does not subsist in a divisible body—you cannot doubt the conclusion. We say: We have only composed this book to demonstrate the incoherence and contradiction in the speech of the philosophers, and it has been achieved. Al-Ghazali said: Then we say: this contradiction shows that they overlooked a point of equivocation in the syllogism; perhaps the point of equivocation is their statement that knowledge is impressed in the body, like the impression of color in its locus, so knowledge divides by the division of the body just as color divides by the division of its locus.
Source pages p. 363

Commentary
This passage is a objection-and-answer passage. It presents the first philosophical proof for the immateriality of the soul and Ghazali's refutation. The philosophers argue: rational sciences (concepts) contain indivisible units (e.g., the concept of unity), so their locus must be indivisible; the body is divisible, so the soul is not a body. Ghazali objects in two ways: First, why reject the possibility that the locus is an indivisible atomic substance? Second, the claim that every state is divisible is refuted by the sheep's estimative faculty, which perceives the wolf's enmity as a single indivisible judgment. The philosophers counter that this is a contradiction in rational matters, but Ghazali responds that his book is specifically written to expose such contradictions. He then identifies the true equivocation: the philosophers assume knowledge is like color—impressed in the body and divisible with it—but this is the point of confusion.

Key Terms
العلم
النفس
الجسم
الانقسام
القوة الوهمية
المناقضة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Ghazali is rejecting all rational proofs for the soul.
Correction: Rather, he is exposing flawed assumptions in the philosophers' argument, not denying the soul's immateriality.

Checkpoint
What is the second objection of Imam al-Ghazali against the first proof?
A) The proof is self-contradictory.
B) The estimative faculty in a sheep perceives enmity as one indivisible thing, refuting the claim that everything in a body is divisible.
C) Knowledge cannot be impressed in the body at all.
D) The locus of knowledge must be a body.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali demonstrates that the philosophers' proof fails because it mistakenly equates knowledge with physical impressions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hold a belief that seems logically proven, are you willing to examine the hidden assumptions it rests on? What assumption in your own life might be like the philosophers' assumption that knowledge is like color—something you've accepted without question?
?
The sheep's instinctive perception of the wolf's enmity is used to challenge a logical proof. When your heart or intuition contradicts a 'rational' argument, do you trust your instinct or the argument? How do you discern which is right?
?
Ghazali wrote this book to expose incoherence in the philosophers' claims. Are you willing to subject your own most cherished beliefs to honest critique, even if it is uncomfortable? What would it take for you to change your mind on an issue you feel strongly about?

The Second Proof: Identical and Already Refuted
Ghazali dismisses the second proof as identical to the first and already refuted.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali dismisses the philosophers' second proof as a mere variation of the first, requiring no new refutation.

Core Idea
A repeated proof needs no new refutation.

Ghazali now turns to the second proof presented by the philosophers. Rather than repeating the same refutation, he points out that this proof is merely a rephrasing of the first. His response, therefore, is identical, saving time and demonstrating the weakness of the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read Ghazali's concise dismissal of the second proof, noting his claim that it reduces to the same premise as the first.
The passage is very short; pay attention to the key term 'انطباع' (impression).
2
Understand the shared premise
Both proofs rely on the idea that knowledge is impressed in a divisible body and would thus divide with it. This is the core assumption Ghazali refuted earlier.
3
Grasp the dismissal
Ghazali states that the answer to the second proof is the same as the first. This is a valid rhetorical strategy: a proof that adds nothing new does not require a new rebuttal.
Consider why repeating the same refutation would be redundant.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الدليل الثانى : وهو يؤول إلى انطباع العلم ، وانقسامه بانقسام محله على ما مر فى الدليل الأول . . . وجوابه هو نفس الجواب.
The second proof: it reduces to the impression of knowledge and its division by the division of its locus as has passed in the first proof... and its answer is the same answer.
Source pages p. 364

Commentary
This passage is a brief dismissal. The philosophers' second proof is essentially the same as the first: it relies on the idea that knowledge is impressed in the body and divides with it. Ghazali states that the answer to it is the same as the answer already given to the first proof, so no new objection is needed.

Key Takeaway

Ghazali's Refutation of the Third Proof
Ghazali refutes the third proof using the analogy of sense perception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali refutes the philosophers' third proof about knowledge and the locus of knowing, using the analogy of sense perception.

Core Idea
Attribution to the whole does not require the attribute to be in every part.

This passage presents the third philosophical proof that knowledge cannot be in a physical part, and Ghazali's sharp refutation. The philosophers argue that if knowledge were in a part, only that part would be called knower, but we call the whole man knowing. Ghazali calls this nonsense and gives a simple analogy: we say man sees, hears, and tastes even though these are localized in specific organs.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the two parts: the philosophers' proof and Ghazali's response.
The philosophers' argument is short and Ghazali calls it 'haws' (nonsense).
2
Understand the philosophers' reasoning
Identify their premise: if knowledge were in a part, only that part would be the knower. Their conclusion: therefore knowledge cannot be in a part because we call the whole person 'knower'.
They assume that the attribute must apply to the specific locus.
3
Follow Ghazali's refutation
Ghazali provides a counterexample: we call a person 'seeing', 'hearing', 'tasting' even though these functions are performed by specific parts (eyes, ears, tongue). So the assumption is false.
This shows that attribution to the whole is common for localized faculties.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الدليل الثالث : قولهم : إن العلم لو كان في جزء من الجسم ، لكان العالم ذلك الجزء ، دون سائر أجزاء الإنسان ، والإنسان يقال له عالم ، والعالمية صفة له على الجملة، من غير نسبة إلى محل مخصوص. قال الغزالي : هذا هوس : فإن الإنسان يسمى مبصراً ، وسامعاً ، وهو يسمع ، ويبصر ، ويذوق ، بأجزاء من جسمه، لا به كله.
The third proof: their statement: if knowledge were in a part of the body, then that part would be the knower, without the other parts of man, while man is called 'knower', and 'knowing' is an attribute of him in a general way, without relation to a particular locus. Al-Ghazali said: This is nonsense: for man is called 'seeing' and 'hearing', and he hears, sees, and tastes by parts of his body, not by all of it.
Source pages p. 364

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof and its refutation. The philosophers argue that if knowledge were located in a physical part, only that part would be the knower, but we attribute being a knower to the whole human. Ghazali calls this 'nonsense' and gives a counterexample: we say 'man sees' even though sight occurs only in the eye, not the whole body. Similarly, knowledge can be attributed to the whole person despite being in a specific part.

Key Terms
العالم
العالمية
محل
مبصر
سامع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Ghazali denies the unity of the knower by allowing knowledge to be in a part.
Correction: Ghazali does not deny unity; he shows that attributing an ability to the whole is common even when the function is localized, as with sight and hearing.

Checkpoint

C) The analogy of a stone being heavy.
D) The analogy of the soul being immaterial.

Key Takeaway
We can attribute a capacity to a person as a whole even if it originates in a specific body part.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The philosophers argued that attribution to the whole requires the attribute in every part. Do you ever expect your faith to feel the same in every moment, and if so, how does Ghazali's refutation challenge that expectation and free you to accept the unevenness of your spiritual state?

Ghazali's fourth proof: knowledge, ignorance, and the turning of the argument
Ghazali turns the fourth proof against the philosophers using the example of conflicting desires.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Ghazali refutes the philosophers' fourth proof by showing that their own theory of desire leads to the same contradiction.

Core Idea
Ghazali turns the philosophers' own argument against them using desire and will.

In this passage, Ghazali presents the fourth proof of the philosophers, who argue that knowledge and ignorance cannot coexist in different parts of the heart or brain. Then he turns the argument back on them, showing that their own account of desires and will suffers from the same logical problem. This is a classic example of Ghazali's method of refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' proof
Carefully read the first part where the philosophers argue that if knowledge inheres in a part of the heart/brain, then ignorance could inhere in another part, leading to the impossibility of simultaneous knowledge and ignorance of the same thing.
Notice the logical structure: if premise A (knowledge in a part), then possibility of opposite in another part, leading to contradiction.
2
Identify Ghazali's counter
Read Ghazali's response: he says this argument turns against them when applied to desire, longing, and will. These are also 'meanings impressed in the body' and yet it is impossible for aversion and inclination to coexist in the same subject at the same time.
Ghazali is not denying the impossibility; he is showing the same reasoning applies to the philosophers' own concepts.
3
Reflect on the refutation strategy
Think about why Ghazali's refutation is effective. He uses the philosophers' own principle (impossibility of opposites in the same subject at the same time) to challenge their account of emotions, which they also consider bodily.
This is a typical reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الدليل الرابع : قالوا : إن كان العلم يحل جزءاً من القلب أو الدماغ مثلا ، فالجهل ضده ، فيجوز أن يقوم بجزء آخر من القلب أو الدماغ ، ويكون الإنسان في حال واحدة عالماً وجاهلا بشيء واحد . . . وذلك محال. قال الغزالي : هذا ينقلب عليهم فى الشهوة والشوق والإرادة فإن هذه أمور تثبت للبهائم وهى معان تنطبع في الجسم ، ومع يستحيل أن ينفر الإنسان عما يشتاق إليه، حتى يجتمع فيه النفرة والميل إلى شيء واحد، في وقت واحد.
The fourth proof: they said: if knowledge inheres in a part of the heart or brain for example, then ignorance is its opposite, so it is possible that it occurs in another part of the heart or brain, and man would be in one state knowing and ignorant of one thing... and that is impossible. Al-Ghazali said: This turns against them regarding desire, longing, and will: for these are things established in beasts, and they are meanings impressed in the body, and it is impossible for man to dislike what he longs for, so that aversion and inclination towards one thing unite in him at one time.
Source pages p. 364

Commentary
This passage contains the fourth proof and Ghazali's refutation. The philosophers argue: if knowledge inheres in a part of the heart or brain, then its opposite, ignorance, could inhere in another part, making the same person simultaneously knowing and ignorant of the same thing—which is impossible. Ghazali turns the argument back: the same problem applies to the philosophers' own account of desire, longing, and will. These are also 'meanings impressed in the body', and yet it is impossible for a person to simultaneously have aversion and inclination toward the same thing. So if they accept that these opposites cannot coexist, they must admit that desire is not in a divisible part, which undermines their premise.

Key Terms
الجهل
الضد
الشهوة
الشوق
الإرادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think Ghazali is proving the soul's immateriality.
Correction: Ghazali is not proving immateriality; he is showing a flaw in the philosophers' reasoning about how opposites can or cannot coexist in divisible parts.

Checkpoint
How does al-Ghazali refute the fourth proof?
A) He denies that knowledge and ignorance are opposites.
B) He argues that desire, longing, and will also cannot exist in different parts simultaneously, so the same problem applies to the philosophers.
C) He says the heart and brain are not divisible.
D) He accepts the proof as valid.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali refutes the philosophers' proof by applying their own logic to desires, showing that if knowledge and ignorance cannot coexist in different parts, then neither can desire and aversion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you currently holding onto a belief or desire that directly contradicts another belief you claim to hold? What is one specific instance where your actions or feelings oppose what you know to be true, and what will you do this week to resolve that contradiction?
?
Ghazali uses someone's own standard against them. Is there a standard you apply to others but fail to apply to yourself? Name one area and describe how you will begin holding yourself to that same standard today.
?
The philosophers assumed knowledge and ignorance cannot coexist, yet we often simultaneously know something is wrong and desire it. How has this internal contradiction affected your relationship with Allah, and what is one concrete step you can take to align your heart with what you know to be true?

Fifth Proof: Intellect and Bodily Instrument
Ghazali challenges the philosophers' analogy between intellect and sense perception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's critique of the philosophers' argument that the intellect cannot know itself if it uses a bodily instrument.

Core Idea
Ghazali challenges the analogy between sense perception and intellect.

In this passage, Ghazali presents the fifth proof of the philosophers and begins his rebuttal. The philosophers claim that if the intellect perceives through a bodily instrument, it cannot perceive itself—but since it does, it must be immaterial. Ghazali questions the premise and offers two objections, though the text cuts off during the second. This lesson unpacks the debate and shows Ghazali's method of challenging analogical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get the overall flow of the argument and counterargument.
Notice that the passage is incomplete—the second objection is cut off.
2
Identify the philosopher's argument
Trace the conditional argument: if intellect uses a bodily instrument → it cannot know itself. Its impossibility → the antecedent is false.
The philosophers support this by analogy to sight and hearing.
3
Examine Ghazali's first objection
Ghazali says that sight could potentially see itself, thereby breaking the analogy. Understand why this matters for the argument.
He claims this is his own view (عندنا), not universally accepted.
4
Consider the incomplete second objection
Even granting the analogy for senses, why must the intellect behave the same way? The text cuts off, but think about why Ghazali would challenge the transfer of a rule from senses to intellect.
This is a classic move against analogical arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الدليل الخامس : قولهم : إن كان العقل يدرك المعقول بآلة جسمانية ، فهو لا يعقل نفسه ، والتالى محال، لأنه يعقل نفسه، فالمقدم مثله. قال الغزالي : ما الدليل على لزوم التالى للمقدم ؟ قالت الفلاسفة : الدليل عليه أن الإبصار لما كان بجسم، فالإبصار لا يتعلق بالإبصار، والسمع لما كان بجسم، فالسمع يتعلق بالسمع. قال الغزالي : ما ذكر وه فاسد من وجهين : الوجه الأول : أن الإبصار عندنا يجوز أن يتعلق بنفسه، فيكون إبصاراً لغيره ولنفسه. الوجه الثاني : أنا نسلم هذا في الحواس، ولكن لم قلتم : إذا امتنع
The fifth proof: their statement: if the intellect perceives the intelligible by a bodily instrument, then it does not perceive itself, and the consequent is impossible because it perceives itself, so the antecedent is likewise. Al-Ghazali said: What is the proof of the necessitation of the consequent from the antecedent? The philosophers said: The proof of that is that since sight is by a body, sight does not relate to sight, and since hearing is by a body, hearing does not relate to hearing. Al-Ghazali said: What they mentioned is invalid in two ways: the first way: that sight in our view may relate to itself, so it is seeing of other and of itself. The second way: we grant this for the senses, but why did you say: if it is impossible
Source pages p. 364

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth proof and its refutation, though the text cuts off. The philosophers argue: if the intellect uses a bodily instrument to perceive, it cannot perceive itself; but we know the intellect does perceive itself, so it must not use a bodily instrument. Ghazali first questions the premise: what is the proof that if something uses a physical instrument it cannot know itself? The philosophers respond by analogy: sight (by the eye) does not see itself, hearing does not hear itself. Ghazali gives two objections: First, he says that in his view, sight can actually see itself—it can be both seeing other things and itself. Second, even if we grant that sense faculties cannot grasp themselves, why should the intellect follow the same rule? The text breaks off, but Ghazali is questioning the analogy.

Key Terms
العقل
المعقول
آلة جسمانية
الإبصار
السمع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Ghazali accepts the philosophers' analogy between intellect and senses.
Correction: Ghazali challenges the analogy directly, first by denying that sight cannot see itself, and then by rejecting the transfer of the rule from senses to intellect.

Checkpoint
What is the first objection of al-Ghazali against the philosophers' analogy in the fifth proof?
A) The philosophers have no proof for their premise.
B) Sight can see itself according to Ghazali.
C) The intellect is not a sense.
D) The soul is not a body.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali undermines the philosophers' argument by breaking the analogy between sense perception and intellect, showing that the intellect's self-knowledge does not require it to be immaterial.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your intellect can perceive itself, why do you avoid examining your own hidden motives and flaws, preferring to scrutinize others instead? What would it take for you to turn that gaze inward today?
?
The philosophers assumed that what is true of the body is true of the mind. Where in your life have you wrongly assumed that a limitation of your physical nature applies to your soul, such as when you make excuses for your spiritual laziness by saying 'I'm only human'?
?
Ghazali insists that the intellect is not bound by the same rules as the senses. When you argue about matters of faith, do you treat your reasoning as if it were free from all bodily influences, or do you admit that your desires and habits dull your perception? Will you ask Allah to purify your intellect before your next debate?

The Philosophers' Second Proof for the Soul's Eternity
The philosophers present their second proof for the soul's eternity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' second argument for the soul's eternity, which claims that any substance not in a subject cannot perish.

Core Idea
The second proof: what has no location cannot be destroyed.

The philosophers propose a logical argument: if the soul is a substance without a physical location, it cannot undergo annihilation. This lesson presents that proof before Ghazali's refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof statement
Read the Arabic and translation of the philosophers' proof. Let the directness of the claim sink in.
2
Analyze the key terms
Understand 'substance' (jawhar) and 'locus' (mahall). How does location relate to perishability?
The philosophers define substance as something that does not inhere in another.
3
Reflect on the implication
Why does the philosophers' proof depend on the idea that only things in a subject can perish? Consider examples from physical objects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الدليل الثاني : قولهم : كل جوهر ليس في محل فيستحيل عليه العدم. قال الغزالي في رد هذا الدليل : ويدفع هذا بما قررناه في ذهابهم إلى استحالة حدوث المادة والعناصر، واستحالة عدمها، في مسألتي أزلية العالم وأبديته. ومنشأ التلبيس وضعهم الإمكان وضعاً مستدعياً محلا يقوم به، وقد تكلمنا عليه بما فيه مقنع، فلا نعيده؛ فإن المسألة هی ولا فرق بين أن يكون المتكلم فيه، جوهر مادة، أو جوهر نفس.
The second proof: their statement: 'Every substance (jawhar) that is not in a locus (lā fī maḥall) is impossible for it to become nonexistent.' Al-Ghazālī said in refutation of this proof: This is repelled by what we established regarding their denial of the possibility of the origination of matter and the elements, and the impossibility of their non-existence, in the two issues of the world's pre-eternity and its perpetuity. The source of the confusion (talbīs) is their positing possibility (imkān) as a positing that requires a locus to subsist in, and we have spoken on this sufficiently, so we will not repeat it; for the issue is the same, and there is no difference between the substance spoken about being a material substance (jawhar mādda) or a soul substance (jawhar nafs).
Source pages p. 367

Commentary
This short passage presents the philosophers' second proof and Ghazali's refutation. The philosophers claim that any substance not in a subject (i.e., an immaterial substance like the soul) cannot cease to exist. Ghazali responds by referring back to his earlier arguments against the philosophers' denial of the possibility of matter's origination and annihilation in the discussions on the world's pre-eternity and perpetuity. He identifies the source of their confusion: they assume that possibility (imkān) requires a substrate (maḥall) to subsist in, which is a flawed conception. Since the issue is the same, he does not repeat the full argument, noting that it applies equally to material and soul substances.

Key Terms
جوهر (substance)
محل (locus/subject)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What do the philosophers claim about any substance not in a locus?
A) It can only exist accidentally.
B) It is impossible for it to become nonexistent.
C) It cannot act upon other things.
D) It must be eternal in the past.

Key Takeaway
The second proof hinges on the idea that only things in a subject can change and perish, so the soul, being free of location, must be imperishable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your soul is truly independent of your body and cannot be destroyed, what does that mean for how you prioritize your bodily desires versus your spiritual growth? Are you living as if your soul is eternal or as if your body is all that matters?
?
The philosophers argued that what has no location cannot be destroyed. Do you live with the certainty that your soul will outlast your body? How would your daily choices change if you truly believed in the immortality of your soul?
?
Consider that decay and dissolution require a physical substrate. Your soul is not subject to physical decay. Are you investing in your soul's nourishment as much as you invest in your body's health? What specific change can you make today to prioritize your soul?

Ghazali's Refutation of the Second Proof
Ghazali refutes the second proof by referencing his earlier arguments on matter's origination and annihilation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's refutation of the philosophers' second proof, which directs it back to his earlier critique of matter's possibility.

Core Idea
Ghazali's refutation: the same argument applies to matter, so the proof is not special.

Ghazali does not need to develop a new argument. He points out that the philosophers already faced a similar problem with matter and elements. Their confusion lies in their understanding of possibility. This lesson unpacks that refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read Ghazali's response
Read the Arabic and translation of Ghazali's refutation. Notice how he connects this proof to earlier discussions.
2
Connect to earlier discussion
Understand that Ghazali references his treatment of the world's pre-eternity and perpetuity. Why does he bring up matter?
He already showed that the philosophers' denial of matter's origination and annihilation is flawed.
3
Identify the source of confusion
According to Ghazali, the philosophers mistakenly treat possibility (imkān) as something that needs a physical substrate. Contrast this with the correct view that possibility is a rational judgment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الدليل الثاني : قولهم : كل جوهر ليس في محل فيستحيل عليه العدم. قال الغزالي في رد هذا الدليل : ويدفع هذا بما قررناه في ذهابهم إلى استحالة حدوث المادة والعناصر، واستحالة عدمها، في مسألتي أزلية العالم وأبديته. ومنشأ التلبيس وضعهم الإمكان وضعاً مستدعياً محلا يقوم به، وقد تكلمنا عليه بما فيه مقنع، فلا نعيده؛ فإن المسألة هی ولا فرق بين أن يكون المتكلم فيه، جوهر مادة، أو جوهر نفس.
The second proof: their statement: 'Every substance (jawhar) that is not in a locus (lā fī maḥall) is impossible for it to become nonexistent.' Al-Ghazālī said in refutation of this proof: This is repelled by what we established regarding their denial of the possibility of the origination of matter and the elements, and the impossibility of their non-existence, in the two issues of the world's pre-eternity and its perpetuity. The source of the confusion (talbīs) is their positing possibility (imkān) as a positing that requires a locus to subsist in, and we have spoken on this sufficiently, so we will not repeat it; for the issue is the same, and there is no difference between the substance spoken about being a material substance (jawhar mādda) or a soul substance (jawhar nafs).
Source pages p. 367

Commentary
This short passage presents the philosophers' second proof and Ghazali's refutation. The philosophers claim that any substance not in a subject (i.e., an immaterial substance like the soul) cannot cease to exist. Ghazali responds by referring back to his earlier arguments against the philosophers' denial of the possibility of matter's origination and annihilation in the discussions on the world's pre-eternity and perpetuity. He identifies the source of their confusion: they assume that possibility (imkān) requires a substrate (maḥall) to subsist in, which is a flawed conception. Since the issue is the same, he does not repeat the full argument, noting that it applies equally to material and soul substances.

Key Terms
إمكان (possibility)
مادة (matter)
عناصر (elements)
أزلية (pre-eternity)
أبدية (perpetuity)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Possibility (imkān) is an inherent quality that must reside in a physical thing.
Correction: Possibility is a rational judgment about what God can do, not a property that inheres in a subject.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what is the source of confusion in the philosophers' second proof?
A) They assume the soul is a divisible substance.
B) They think possibility requires a physical locus.
C) They deny the existence of God.
D) They ignore the concept of accident.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali refutes the second proof by showing it is the same erroneous reasoning the philosophers used for matter, already addressed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever treat your own spiritual capacities—like your ability to repent or grow in faith—as if they were fixed 'properties' of your soul, rather than possibilities that depend entirely on God's power and your will? What would change if you truly saw them as possibilities contingent on God's constant creation?
?
Ghazali exposes the hidden assumption that possibility must have a physical foundation. Have you ever believed that your good deeds or your salvation are 'impossible' because of your past or your circumstances? Are you implicitly denying that God's power can create possibility where none seems to exist?
